All the true friends of Great Britain, and of civil and religious liberty, arc exhorted to unite in support of the Tio-testant interest before it is too late; for unanimity and firmness in that glorious cause can alone prote<5t us from the dangerous confederacy of Popish powers, if we unite, like one man, for the honour of God, and the liberties of the N n people,

In pursuance of an advertisement, dated, May 5. a meeting was held, and its proceedings appeared in the papers, viz.

Protestant Association.

Monday evening [May 29.] the meeting of the Protestant Association was held at Coachmaker’s hall, pursuant to public advertisement. At half past Gx o’clock the hall was quite full, and exceedingly crouded. Lord George Gordon took the chair exactly at seven, and addressed the meeting for near half an hour. His Lordship observed, that the Popish bill was carried through both Houses of Parliament with such amazing rapidity, that the people had not time to form an opposition, or to make themselves acquainted* with the pernicious consequences that must attend its passing into a law; that the indulgence given to Popery by the repeal of the act of Wil-V ^lit. Was inconliftent with the princip.-i jf liie Resolution, had a tendency to endanger the fucccflion in the House of Hanover, and threatened deftrndtion to the civil and religious liberty of this country. His Lordship read an extract from a Popish catechism, just published by a Popish printer in Grosvenor square, and JifperfcJ among the ignorant and unthinking part of the community; Pke-wife an indulgence granted by the Pope, this present year, to his Holy Catholic subjects and laints in this heretic country: and from these publications his Lordship bid the people form an idea of the rapid and alarming progref9 that Popery was making in this kingdom; and the only way to flop it was, by going, in a firm, manly, and resolute man-

ner, to the House, and shewing their representatives, that they were determined to preserve their religious freedom with their lives: that, for his part, be would run all hazards with the people; and if the people were too lukewarm to run all hazards with him, when their conscience and their country call them forth, they might get another President; for he would tell them candidly, that he was not a lukewarm man himself, and that if they meant to spend their time in mock debate and idle opposition, they might get another leader. This speech was received with the loudest acclamations of applause 5 and his Lordship then moved the following resolution: “ That the whole body of the Protestant Aflocia-tion do attend in St George’s Fields, on Friday next, at ten o’clock in the morning, to accompany his Lordship to the House of Commons on the delivery of the Protestant petition which was carried unanimously, with repeated burftsof applause. His Lordship then informed them, that if less than 20,000 of his fellow-citizens attended him on that day, he would not present their petition: and for the better observance of order, he moved, that they should arrange themselves in four divisions; the Protestants of the city of London on the right, those of the city of Westminster on the left, the borough of Southwark third, and the people of Scotland resident in London and its environs to form the last division; and that they might know their friends from their enemies, he added, that every real Protestant, and friend of the petition, should come with blue cockades in their hats. This also passed unanimously; and the meeting adjourned to Monday the 12th day of June.”

H. the Duke of Gloucester, the Dukes of Devonshire, Richmond, and Roxburgh, the Earl of Shelburne, Lord Camden, the Bishop of Peterborough, and many other patriotic noblemen, bad their carriages conducted with great respect to the door of the House.—The mob filled the lobby of the House of Commons, and pressed so violently against the door, that the members might truly be said to be closely blocked up: and they followed the Lords to the door of the House; but were there rc-pulsed by the wife precautions of Sir Francis Molineux.

Intelligence having been brought into the House of Lords, that Lord Bolton was in the power of the mob, Lord Radnor proposed, that some Lords should go down, and extricate his Lordlliip; Lord Townlhcntl offered to be one of the number; the Duke of Richmond offered to go also, but said, if they went as a House, the macc ought to oe car* ried before the learned Lord on the wool-sack, who Oiould goat their head; Lord Mansfield expressed his readiness if their Lordiliips thought it proper: but at that moment Lord Bolton got into the house, his hair all dilhevclled, and his cloaths ail bedawbed. — The Earl of Shelburne blamed administration for not having taken proper measures 1o prevent a tumult, as the advertisements calling the people together bad appeared in the news papers some days before; and in-

sinuated that it was a government-mob.

Lord Rayensworth said, the advertise-roent for the people to assemble in large bodies, in which the name of Lord George Gordon bad appeared, was pu-Wilhed some days before; and ought to have been immediately proceeded on. Lord Bathurst observed, that the mob had ill-treated more Lords in administration than in opposition: was it likely they would have done so had they been the mob of administration ? Lord Hills-borough said, that orders bad been sent to the magistratesof Westminster, warning them of this mob, and directing them to be io the way to quell any tumults. But Mr Sampson Wright, and another justice, laid, they bad neither received nor beard of such an order.*-Lord Mansfield, by order of the House, directed the two justices, to godirectly, and try by every possible force they could collect, to difperie the mob, and to come back and report. The justices on their return reported, that they had gone to Guildhall, Westminster, and that the mob bad so!-. lowed Mr W. Ellis thither, and had turned out Mr Addington and the constables; adding, that the mob was too large for them and the constables to be. able todifperfe. Lord Denbigh said, if the civil power were unequal to the talk, the military ought to be called out* and put under the authority of the civil power. Before nine o'clock the Lords adjourned to the next day.

In the House of Commons Lord George Gordon came several times to the top of the gallery, and harangued the mob. He told them to be decent and orderly; at the same time to keep to their purpose; to insist on an answer to the petition that night; for that Monday would be the King's birth-day, and probably the parliament might be prorogued on Tuesday, the day to which it was intended to adjourn the consideration of the petition, and then there would be an end of the affair:—“ Gentlemen, (said his Lordlliip), the alarm has gone forth for many miles round the city; you have got a very good prince, who, as soon as he shall learti that the alarm has fuzed such a number of men, will, no doubt, fend down private orders to his ministers to enforce the prayer of your petition." Mean time the chaplain of the House told Lord George, that all the fatal consequences that might happen that night, would lie entirely at his Lord ship's

Lord ship’s door; Gen. Conway asked him, if he intended to bring those men into the house ? the members would defend the door with their fworda; and Col. - accosted him thus: My

Lord George,* do you intend to bring your rascally adherents into the House of Commons ? If you do,—the first man of them that enters, I will plunge mv sword, not into his, but into your body. This dismayed Lord George not a little. —About r.inc o’clock the horse and foot guards arrived: the former were placed in Palace-yard; the latter lined all the avenues to the House of Commons; and a captain’s guard was placed in the court of Requests. After this no mischief cn-sued. —- Almost the whole time the House (at whs taken up in debating about the mob. At last Lord George presented the petition; and moved, That it ihoold be then taken into consideration. But another motion was made, for postponing the consideration of it till the Tuesday following; and upon a division, the numbers were, For postponing 182, Against it 9. These nine were. Sir P.

Jen. Ckrke, Sir Mich. Le Fleming, Sir ames Lowther, Sir Joseph Mawbey, Ir Polhill, Mr Tollemache, Earl Vcr-ney. Lord George Gordon, and Aid. Bull. At eleven o’clock the House adjourned; and the mob being by this time gone off, the guards were ordered away. About ten o’clock the mob paraded in differentdivisionsfrom Palace-yard. Part weot to the Sardinian ambassador’6 cha-broke open the doors, pulled down the rails, feats, altar, See. carried them to the street, laid them against the doors, and set them on fire, by which the chapel was entirely consumed, and the house over the gateway much damaged. The mob would not suffer any body to extinguish the flames. About eleven the guards came, and seized several of the ringleaders. Another party broke into the Bavarian ambassador’s chapel, demolished it partly, and carried off the box which contained the poor’s money. Here they were interrupted by the military, and thirteen of the rioters seized. They broke also into the houses of the Bavarian and Sardinian ambassadors: three of the rioters were taken in the latter. Great depredations were likewise committed in and about Moorfields.

On Saturday, June 3. little or no dif-

turbance happened. — An official letter was Cent to the Lord Mayor, viz.

“ Mv Lord (St James's, J3. 1780.

My L.ORD, ,4 M

As information which I have received, gives me reason to apprehend, that tumults may arise within your Lordship’s jurisdiction, I think it my duty to convey to you immediately this information. I cannot too stronglyf recommend the matter to your Lordship’s attention; and am confident, from your known activity, that you will not omit any legal exertion of the civil power which may contribute upon thi9 occasion to preserve the public peace. 1 have the honour to be, my Lord, your Lordship’s most obedient humble servant,

Stormont.

The Rt Hon. the Lord Mayor.”

Thirteen persons who had been taken up the evening before, were this day examined, at Sir John Fielding’s, by Justices Wright, Addington, &c.; of whom eleven were committed to Clerkenwell prison, and two were admitted to bail.

In the House of Lords, this day, the Earl Bathurst, President of the Council, made a pathetic speech on the indignity offered to that House by an insolent mob the day preceding. Some Lords (he fakl) had been torn from their carriages, and absolutely robbed; a Noble Prelate had been obliged to take (belter in a gentleman’s house, to change his cloaths, and escape over the tops of the houses; for thirty of the rioters broke into the house, and searched every room in it for the Bishop, menacing him in the most horrid manner. Not content with this and other outrages, they had proceeded at night to the houses of some of the foreign ministers; had insulted them, and set fire to their chapels; by which they Had brought disgrace upon this country. His Lordship, therefore, thought it impossible for the House to proceed to any other business till they had taken proper measures to restore their dignity, to provide for their safety and tranquillity, and to maintain the honour of the nation in rel'ped to the security of the rights and privileges of foreign ministers. He trusted their Lordfhtps would not suffer an idea injurious to their justice, their respect for foreign states, and their regard for the laws of nations, to go abroad; and he had not a doubt, but every Noble Lord who heard him, felt his indignation rife as high as he did: he was therefore satisfied that they would ail readily concur

with

with him in a measure which he intended to submit to the House; a measure the more necessary, as the spirit of rioting was not extinguished. Their Lordships could not suppose, that outrages would be committed only on one fide; for there were whispers, and something stronger than whispers. that on that very night another description of mob fired by re-fentment, intended to rife, and retaliate. Government had already taken every precaution to prevent the perpetration of such outrages as had, on Friday night, dtfgraced the capital; but this was not sufficient: it was necessary to make some examples, that the multitude might learn, that however great their numbers, they must not expect that impunity should attend the violation of the peace and quiet of the community. For this purpose he Called upon their Lordships to concur with him in the following motion: * That an humble address be presented to his Majesty, that he will be gracious-Jy pleased to give immediate directions for prosecuting, in the most effectual manner, the authors, abettors, and instruments, of the outrages committed yesterday in Old Palace-yard, Guildhall, Westminster, and places adjacent, and upon the houses and chapels of several of the ministers from foreign states.”

The Duke of Richmond admitted the motion to be very proper; but could not help thinking that ministers had themselves only to blame for the outrages committed. They had had timely notice of the meeting; and by neglecting to provide for the maintenance of peace and good order, they were responsible for those consequences which it was in their power to have prevented. The mob it-lelf was a Scotch mob, raised by a Scotchman, and had paraded like a Scotch mob, with bagpipes playing before them. And he was not a little astonished that the other House of parliament, in the midst of the confusion created among them by the mob, could fit, tamely, and forgetful of its dignity, and fufter the first mover of the whole disorder to walk in and out of the House, for the sole purpose of communicating to the mob the proceedings of the Commons, and acquainting them with the sentiments that each member had delivered respecting the petition, and thereby exposing to the fury of the multitude' such of the Lower House as had exprefTedthemselves unfavourably of the petition.With

respect to the motion, be concurred in it most heartily: he was not an advocate for rioting; and bad too great a respedk for the laws of his country, and the laws of nations, not to wish that the violators of both should be brought to condign punishment.

Lord Shelburne reprehended ministers for not having taken effectual measures to prevent the riots of the preceding day, which they might thank themselves for, because most clearly the whole was but a continuation, and comparatively speaking a feeble continuation, of the riots in Scotland upon tb^fame subject. The mob here had copied the mob there, but they had. been less violent and less excessive. Here a member of parliament, a gentleman of a Noble and distinguished family, had summoned the people together by public advertisement, printed in the newspapers, some days previous to the day on which the mob met. Government ought immediately to have taken notice of that advertisement, and to have prevented the mischiefs that threatened, from taking place. In Scotland the advertise-, ments for the mob to aficmble, and resist tbc act for repealing the former acts against the Roman-Catholics, bad been more in number, and had appeared earlier, and with greater authority. What had been the consequence? Government, instead of puoishing the magistrates of Edinburgh, instead of bolding up to the milled populace an example which would teach them in future to avoid a matter of such great danger, (for a matter of great danger it ought always to be), as opposing the laws, or riling in opposition to any measure which the wisdom of the legislature chose to adopt, had not dared to hurt a hair of the ri6ters heads; they had not dared to institute an enquiry; but had fallen in with the prejudices of Scotland, and had accommodated matters: they had done more;they had promised all that the zeal and enthusiasm of an enraged mob had prompted them to demand[41.109.]. This was the so.urce of the present mischief i Here it was that the public were to look for all the riots that had happened, and all the riots that would happen, in opposition to Popery ! With regard to the late act repealing certain points of feveri-ty, which had been established by former acts, *in times when matters with regard to Popery stood widely different from their present situation, it was an innocent

cent ad, provided government had used that attention which it was at all times their doty to shew towards the conduit of the Roman -Catholics, but more cfpc-cuflf when, from having granted them fane indulgences, it waB natural to ex-pe& they would overleap the bounds which the repealing alt had left as limits to the exercise of their religion in this kingdom. When he was secretary of tare, intelligence was brought him, that a Noble Duke, one of the first nobility in the kingdom, bad not only built a new chapel for the exercise of that religion which he profefied, but had instituted a kind of academy or seminary for the e-docation of young persons: the moment be heard of it, he applied to the Duke; he told him, that if the seminary was not put an end to, he muff interpose his authority: the Noble Duke acted as became him; and the consequence was, the seminary was abolished inffantly, and no more heard of In a late instance, like-wife, he had been applied to by a Noble Lord of the Roman-Catholic religion, and told, that a meeting to difeufs religious matters had been instituted at Coachmaker’s hall, where such topics » were agitated as gave him reason to fee, the feeds of great popular tumult and disorder would be sown, if government did not take effectual measures to put a Stop to it. His answer to his informant had been, “ Go to government, apprise them as fully as you have apprised me of thecircumltaoces, don’t conceal a tittle of what you have said; and they can’t but fee their danger, and will, 1 make no doubt, take the proper measures for preventing it.”—Having acted in this manner, he thought hirofelf warranted to give government some advice jjow. In comparing the events of yesterday, } and the declaration of the justice of peace f at their Lordlliips bar, with what had fallen from the Noble Lord at the !* head of the council, it muff be evident [ to every one of their LordShips, that I the police of Westminster was an im-perfelt, inadequate, and wretched l'y-stem; the example of yefrerday pointed , it out as the first object of reformation, and

j IJicwed most forcibly that it ought to be entirely new-modelled, and that immediately. Let their Lordships recoiled what the police of France was; let them exa-! mineits good, and not be blind to its evil, t They would find itsconftrudion excellent, j its ufc ^nddireltion abominable. Let thorn

embrace the one and shun the other. The police of France was wife to the last degree in its institution, but being perverted in its use, its very merit became its mischief. — His LordShip then animadverted on the intentions of government with regard to prosecuting the rioters; they Should not confine themselves merely to wreaking vengeance on the poor deluded people who hadalfembled in the Streets. They were objects rather of pity than resentment; he had paSTed them with safety both in coming to and going from the House, and had listened to what they said. Their chief argument was, ‘4 No Popery ! we Shall be burnt; no more bonfires in Smithfield; let us have the Protestant religion, and let us have peace !” He added, that he looked in the faces of those who uttered, these sentiments, and he saw evident marks of Sincerity in their countenances; he saw that the people, from what motives he would not undertake to say, believed what they said, and ailed from that conviction. It behoved government therefore to conduct themselves selves with prudence; to distinguish between the efforts of religious opinion, however ill directed, however erroneous, and the violence of ill-intentioned men. Let them not madly hare recouife to the cutting knife: having the law in their power, and at their will, let them ufc it with caution, and at any rate accompany it with such other measures as would Shew a liberality of sentiment, and an equal readiness to enforce the claims of justice, and to do what was right in compliance with the wishes and petitfons of the people. Prosecution, however rigorous, however certain of effect, Singly, would do more harm than good; accompanied with other measures, it would prove equally wife and effectual. There were three subjects under discussion, each easy to be embraced, the punishment of the rioters, the repeal of the Quebec ad, and the regulation of the police of Westminster ! Take them up together, and there could be no doubt of producing all that sober considerate politicians could wifti for under the present circumstances

The Duke of Manchester wished for some explanation of the motion. The term outrages \va9 very extensive, he Should be glad therefore to know what fort of fads government designed to take notice of, and what those indictments

were which the Noble Lord had talked of: were the poor deluded people who hallowed out in the streets, “ No Popery !” to be profreuted on iodidtments for capital felony, or in what manner were they to be proceeded against

The Lord President said, that those persons who were apprehended in the Sardinian ambassador's chapel, in the ad of setting fire to the wainfeot, and such parts of it as they could not remove into the street, being apprehended in the fad of committing capital felony, would be prosecuted accordingly; and others as upon the examination of witnesses it should appear proper: but it was not intended to prosecute those who had only wore blue cockades, joined the mob, and called out, “ No Popery,” See.!

The Duke of Manchester only wiihed that government might not give occasion for any suspicion, that they had been under the influence of daftardnefs during the riot, and that, now the danger was over, they were actuated with revenge. His Grace said, he should certainly support the motion, because it appeared to him exceedingly proper, and indispensably necessary.

The Marquis of Rockingham supported the motion, but severely reprehended the inattention of ministers, who, having had the advertisement of Ld G. Gordon before their eyes for some days, did not take effectual means for preventing the assembling of such a mob. Every wife statesman preferred a preventive to a remedy, and the chief use of the authority office gave, was to intervene in great cases, and forerun mischief. When he was in office, it happened that while he was in the country, intelligence was brought him that a bellman of a neighbouring village had delivered hand-bills about for a considerable extent of country, summoning all persons residing in that district, to meet on an appointed day in the market-place at Pontefradt, in order to prevent by force the price of corn from being raised. This intelligence was no sooner given him, than he rode seventeen miles from Wentworth to a justice of peace who lived near the bellman, and directed him immediately to issue his warrant for apprehending the bellman, and if it should appear on his examination that he had delivered any such hand-bills, to commit him to York castle. The justice took his advice; and there was no mob: for the moment they

heard that the bellman who had fom-moned them together, was in York castle, they were afraid of being sent thither like wife, and therefore never went to Pontefradt. The fame fort of conduct ought to have obtained in this case. Lord George Gordon should have been proceeded sgainst, and his loud-tongued followers would have been appalled. He had heard the mob call out, “ No Popery ! ” and clapping his bands to his watch to secure it, he had felt several hands directed to it; it was pretty obvious therefore what some of the mob came for.”

Lord Mansfield having put the que-ftion, it carried turning disjenthnti.

Administration having been blamed for not preventing the mob, by giving early notice to the magistrates; and Mr Wnght, one of the justices of Westminster, who, the ministry said, had got stich notice, having, the day before, in the House of Lords, denied his having received such notice; the subject was resumed this day. On a motion by Lord Shelburne Mr Wiight was ordered to attend; and at Lord BathurfPs desire, Mr Chamberlayne, Solicitor of the Treasury, was likewise sent for. Mr Chamberlayne informed the House, that he went with a message from Lord North to Sir John Fielding, on Thursday, whom he found at Brompton; and Sir John told him, he would be in town early the next morning, and appoint proper persons to attend in St George’s fields, to give him intelligence if there was any appearance of disturbance, and he would take measures accordingly. Mr Wright gave a clear account of his conduct, and satis-fitd the House, that he had not heard of any orders from Lord North; but that be had come to Westminster on Friday from his own wishes to preserve the peace. But it was said, that there were two of the name of Wright in the commission.

As little or no disturbance happened this day, sober people were rejoicing, that the zealots for religion had exhausted their rage, and that peace and good order were again re It ore d: but those who first kindled the fire, seeing the feeble attempts that were made to oppose them, resolved to spread the flames far and wide, in order to make the conflagration subservient to their own purposes of plunder.

If any strong hope of success in the negotiation had remained, the evactia-I tion of Philadelphia, and the consequent retreat of the army to the northward. Just at the arrival of the Commissionerv, would have compleatly frustrated them. Commissioners accompanying a retreating army, which was in the act of abandoning the principal advantage of two ! years war, could not promise themselves a great superiority in any treaty; and the more advantageous the offers which they should make in such circumstances, the more their conceifions would be considered as proofs of weakness, not of '. good-will. This' measure was carried into execution on the 18th of June, and the whole British army passed the Delaware on the fame day, without interruption or danger, under the excellent dispofltions made by the Admiral, Lord Howe, for the purpose.

Wafhiirgton, having penetrated into the intention of abandoning Philadelphia! had already sent Gen. Maxwell with his brigade to reinforce the Jersey militia, in order to throw every possible obstruction in the way of the British army, so that, by impeding their progress, he might himself be enabled to bring up his force in such time as to profit of those opportunities, which, it was well to be supposed, so long a march through so dangerous a country would have afforded, of attacking them with great advan- tage. This detached corps and the mi-I litia, did not, however, efle# any thing j more of importance than the breaking ( down of the bridges; the great superio-; rity of the British force having obliged them to abandon the strong pass at Mount Holly, without venturing an of-position.

The British army, notwithstanding* encountered much toil, difficulty, and numberless impediments in their march* They were encumbered with an enormous baggage, including provisions; the number of loaded horses and wheel* carriages being so great, as to cover art extent of twelve miles, in the narrow line of march which the nature of the country and roads afforded. This in* combrance, so far at least as related to the provisions, proceeded, however, from the foresight and wisdom of the \ General, Sir Henry Clinton: who being ' Vot.XLlI.

well aware, that the hostility of the country would cut off every source of subsistence from the troops which was not within their own immediate comprehension; and being also uncertain as to the delays and obstructions which might occur his march, was too prudent to put the fate of a whole army in any degree of hazard, for the trouble or difficulty that Attended the conveyance of a certain and sufficient supply. The heat of the weather, which was then excessive, with the-dofenefs of the narrow roads through the wood9, and the constant labour of renewing or repairing bridges, in a country every-where interfered with creeks and marshy brooks, were, all together* severely felt by the army;

From all these causes, its progress was exceedingly flow; and nothing less than these could have accounted for itsfpending so many days in traversing so narrow a country. When the army had advanced to AllenVtown, it became a mattet4 of consideration with the General, whe* ther to keep the direct course towards Staten island, across the Rariton, or whether, by taking the road to his right, and drawing towards the fca coalt, he should push on to Sandy-hook. He knew that the Generals Wafhingtoit and Lee, with the whqle Continental force on that fide, had already passed the Delaware; and be had heard that Gen# Gates, with the northern army, was advancing to join them on the Rariton. The difficulty of passing the Rariton, and the circumstances with which it might have been attended, under his incumbrances* in the face of an enemy, with other concurring causes, determined him to thtf right-hand course, as much the more c-ligible.

Sir Henry Clinton, on the march to a place called Freehold, judging from the Biimber of the enemy’s light troops which hovered on his rear, that their main body was at no great difiance, judiciously determined to free that part of the army from the incumbrance and impediment of the baggage, which be accordingly placed under the conduit of Gen. Knyp-haufen, who led the first column of the army. The other, which covered the line of march, being now disengaged, and free for allion, formed a body of troops which could not easily be equalled, and was under the immediate command of the General. It was composed of the jd, 4th, and 5th brigades of British, two battalions of British, and the Hessian grenadiers, a battalion of light infantry, the guards, and the loth regiment of light dragoons.

On the morning after this arrangement [June 38.], Gen. The second division, under the commander in chief, continued for some hours on their ground in the neighbourhood of Freehold, both to cover the line of march, and to afford time for the chain of carriages to get olcJr on their way.

Having begun to march about eight o'clock, some parties of the enemy which appeared in the woods on their left flank, were engaged and dispersed by the light troops; but as the rear-guard defeended from the heights above Free* hold, ioto a valley about three miles in length, and one in breadth, several co* lumns of the enemy appeared, likewise defeending into the plain, who about ten o’clock began to cannonade the rear.

The General at the fame instant received intelligence, that the enemy were diico-vered marching in force on both his flanks. He was immediately ftfuck, that an attack on the baggage was their principal objeH; and as the carriages were then intangied in defiles which continued for some miles, it seemed a mat* ter of no small difficulty to obviate the danger.

In this critical situation, the General, with great quickneL, and presence of mind, judged, that a vigorous attack and severe prefTure upon that body of the enemy which harassed his rear, would recal the detachments on his flanks to its affifiance, and seemed to be the only probable means of saving the convoy.

For although be had good information, that Gen.Wafiiiogton was at hand with his whole army, which be heard was e* ftimated at 10,000 men 5 yet, as be knew that his main body was separated from that corps which attacked Lord Cornwallis in the rear, by two considerable defile6, he was not apprehensive that he could pass a greater body of troops through them, during the execution of the measure which he intended, than what the force along with him was well able to oppose \ whilst, on the other band, even with that division of the ar* ] my, Washington’s situation would not be a little critical, if he should chance to come upon him when he was firtig-gling in his passage through the defiles.

The British grenadiers, with their left to the village of Freehold, and the guards on their right, began the attack with sech spirit, that the enemy soon gave way. But their second line preserved a better countenance, and refilled a fierce and eager attack with great obstinacy. They were, however, at length, compleatly routed: but in this exigency, with a very unusual degree of recollection, as well as resolution, took a third position with so much judgement, that their front was covered by a marshy hollow, which fearcely admitted the practicability of an attack by that way.

Sir Henry Clinton brought up part of the second line, and made some other dispositions to attack the enemy in this pod, and the light infantry and rangers had already turned their left for that purpose; but the army in general was now so overpowered by heat and fatigue, that, - upon confederation, he thought it better not to press the aifair any farther. He was also by this time confident, that the purpose which had induced him to the attack was gained, in the preservation of the convoy. A bold attempt of the enemy to cut off the retreat of the light infantry, rendered some new movements, notwithstanding the excelfive toil of the day. Hill necef-Cary. The army at length returned to that position from whence they had first driven the enemy, after their quitting the plain.

The General's opinion with respect to the design on the baggage, was justified in the event; and the propriety of his fnbfcquent conduct in attacking the enemy on that principle confirmed. Sir Henry Clinton having now fully attained his object, (for the Generals Knyphaufen and Grant, with the first division and baggage, were arrived at Nut Swamp, near Middletown), could have no inducement for continuing m his present lit nation. The troops had already gained sufficient honour, in forcing successively, from two strong pofi-tions, a corps of the enemy, which, be was informed, amounted to near 11,000 men; and the merit of the service was much enhanced, by the unequalled circumstances of heat and fatigue under which it was performed. The enemy were much superior in force to the division immediately under his command; and if the equality had been even nearer, it would still seem imprudent to have hazarded an engagement at such a distance from the rest of his army, in a country not only entirely hostile, but which from its nature mull have been ruinous to firangers under any circumstance of defeat. And as the heat of the weather rendered marching by day intolerable, so the moon-light added much to the e-ligibility of the night for that purpose. Upon some or all of these accounts, the troops having reposed till ten o'clock, the army was again put in motion, and they marched forward to join their fellows.

Such was the detail of the action at Freehold, or Monmouth, as it is otherwise called, as given on our fide. The loss in (lain was not considerabte in point of number, but rendered grievous by that of the brave Col. Monckton. This day and action were also rendered remarkable by the singular circumstance, unparallelled in the biftory of the new world, of fifty-nine soldiers perishing. In the mean time, the British army, arrived at the high lands of Navefink, in the neighbourhood of Sandy* hook, on the last of June; at which latter place, the fleet from the Delaware, under Lord Howe, after being detained in that river by calms, had most fortunately arrived in the preceding day. It had hap* pened in the preceding winter, that the peninsula of Sandy-hook had been cut off from the continent, and converted to an absolute island, by a violent breach of the sea; a circumstance then of little moment, but which might now have been attended with the most fatal confei quences. By the happy arrival of the fleet at the instant when its assistance was so critically necessary, the ability of the Noble commander, and the extraordinary efforts of the seamen, this impediment was speedily removed; a bridge of boats being completed with such expedition that the whole army was passed over this new channel on the 5th of July; and were afterwards conveyed with ease to New York; neither army nor navy yet knowing the circumstances of danger and ruin in which they had been so nearly involved.

For an unexpected enemy had now arrived on the coast of North America, who was to give a new and a strange turn to the circumstances of the war. On the second day after the conveyance of the army from Sandy-hook, Lord Howe received intelligence by his cruisers, that D’Eliaing’s fleet had been feeo on the coast of Virginia, on the very day that the army had passed the bridge at Sandy-hook. If D’Eftaing had met the transports, either in the Delaware, or on the passage from thence, loaded and encumbered as they were, and convoyed only by two ships of the line, with a number of frigates, the consequence with respect to the fleet is obvious. But it may not so immediately appear, that the fate of the army was so intimately combined with that of the fleet, that the destruction of the one would have been the inevitably loft of the other: for as the army could not then, by any possible means, have profccqted it* iyay to New York, and

would

194 Mizelanchrha: An anecdote* America* VflI. 42.

Would have been inclosed on one fide by the American irmy, and on the other by the French fleet, cut off from all supply of proyifioh, and destitute of every resource, a repetition of the Saratoga ca-taftrophe must have been the certain con* sequence.

Although this fatal event was prevented by the bad weather, and unexpected impediments which D’Eftaing met with on his voyage; yet, if he had directed his course directly to New York, instead of the Chefapeak or Delaware, things could scarcely have been better; as he would then have come upon the fleet and army, when they were entangled, either with the laying or passing of the bridge at Sandy-hook. In either circumstance destruction would have been inevitable; and would have been of an amount and magnitude, with respect both to the ma* tine and land service, and the consequences hanging upon it, which, perhaps, has not been equalled of late ages. But D’Eftaing’s great object was the surprise of the fleet in the Delaware, and the consequent tnclosure of the army at Philadelphia: fortunately the winds and Weather frustrated his design. Upon the whole, it may not be easy to point out a more signal or providential deliverance.

Mizelandwitz: An Anecdote.

THE situation and obstinate virtue of the Baron de Mizelaodwitz, are remarkable. He was one of the Swedifti ftnate that was deprived of all power by the present Ring, upon the memorable resolution in the government, which changed it to an absolute monarchy; he was po flic fled of an estate equal to one of ten thousand pounds -year in England, and upon that event fled his country, saying, that he would fuflfer the most wretched exile abroad, rather than remain a Have where he had a right to freedom. He took up his refideoce at Hamburg, where be has lived ever fioce in great poverty, lodging in a very miserable apartment, and waiting entirely on himself. The lling has written twice to him in the moll flattering terms, inviting his return to his c-slate and honours;—but he never took any notice of the letters; and upon his Majesty lending him a remittance to enable him to live more comfortably, he refilled it, sternly saying. “ I will die rather than receive a dollar at the hands of one who his enslaved country I”

AMERICA, [*7.]

A LETTER, amdam ADDRESS, ctrtnd May zi. and the Addrefi ad by Lord George Germain next and most grecioujiy received.

The LETTER.

Gentlemen, NetoY

As President of the board of Loyal Refugees from the several revolted colonies of America, New York excepted. How within the British lines, I am directed to request the favour of you, in their behalf, to solicit of my Lord George Germain the honour of his Lordship*# patronage, and care of the inclosed Address to our Molt Gracious Sovereign, and that his Lordship will be so good ns to pass it properly to the throne.

Highly sensible of the firm and spirited support that his Lordship, as minister for the American department, has been pleased to give to every proposition for the relief and protection of his Majesty*# colonial suffering subjects, the Board beg Vou will express to his Lordship their warm acknowledgements for his atten* tion to their interests; and that his Lordship’s very generous and truly patriotic conduct will be ever remembered them with the most grateful sensibility. The Board also direct me to say, for his Lordship’8 information, that, not* withstanding the ignotant or wilful mif-representations to the contrary, front their own particular knowledge of the real state of the country, the spirit of loyalty and attachment to the British constitution (till remains firm and unabated among many thousands within the rebels power.

In duty to his Majesty,—from a regard to government, and that the national councils may not be longer milled, the Board direct me further to add, that could the natural force of all, or indeed any, of his Majesty's colonies, be properly collected to one point, in a wa most agreeable to the people, or of those among them having influence, and pof-feffing their good opinion ;—could they be fuftantially supported by his Majesty*# troops, while embodying under their own leaders; — were their confidenct to be raised by certain assurances of protection 5 — were a sacred regard paid to the persons and property of the loyal subject, (heretofore too little attended to, and in many instancet [41. 603.1 lhamcfully

I

June 1780. America: Letter and Address of die LoyalRefugees, t

fhamcfully violated),—and civil government established upon constitutional principles, the moment any favourable Opportunity opened for that purpose; they are bold to affirm, that, in the colonies without exception, there might be found a number of loyal active subjects, who, by disarming the disaffected, intimidating the wavering, and driving out the violent, would be more than Sufficient to suppress the wanton and languishing rebellion; and in a little time restore the whole Continent of America to the former mild and just authority of his Maje* fty and the parent-state.

The Board are happy also to express as their sincere opinion, that from the prdent internal calamities existing among the rebels, as well as those impending and inevitable from the utter ruin of their currency,—the alarming apprehensions of Scarcity,— and many other very important diStrefles incident to their present Situation, there is the happiest prospect, if wifely improved, of suddenljr diflbl-ving the rebellious uoion. — They therefore trust, and hope, that neither the exertions of government, nor those of its faitbfnl fervapts, will be leflfened, or wanting, to complete so desirable an e-vent; but that, on the contrary, every fair opportunity may be afforded to his Majesty’s faithful and loyal subjects, as well those now fullering within the British lines, as those without, of testifying their undiminished respect and zeal for the person and service of their beloved Sovereign, — their wishes for a re-union with the parent-state, —and the sacred regard and veneration they entertain for theconftitution and authority of Great Britain, which it is their utmost ambition to express by their actions.

These, Gentlemen, are Sentiments, that, as founded in truth, the Board wiih to have conveyed to my Lord George Gerqnain, if in your opinion the freedom with which they are spoken may not be deemed difrefpedfiil. Should this be apprehended, they then wish them not to be intruded. The Board, therefore, with the highest regard for your characters and judgement, submits the whole to your discretion: relying however upon your politeneSs, and obliging attention to execute the substantial part of their wishes, in banding forward the address to the King, asdireded.

1 have the honour to be. Gentlemen, with the greatest respect, your most

obedient, and most humble servant, Dan. Coxa, President.

To Sir William PepperrtU, Bt, and Joftph Galloway, Esq;

TksADDRESS.

To the King’s Most Excellent Majesty,

The humble Address of the Loyal Refugees from your Majesty’s revolted Colonies of New Hampshire, MaSTa-chuSTet’s-bay, Rhode island, Connecticut, New*Jersey, Pennsylvania, the Three Lower Counties on Delaware, Maryland, Virginia, North Carolina, and South Carolina, now within the British lines at New York.

Most Gracious Sovereign,

You a faithful and dutiful American subjects, who, to avoid the resentment and persecution of the rebels, have been compelled to take refuge under the pro-tedion of your Majesty's troops, humbly beg leave to approach your Majesty, with the Strongest assurances of their inviolate attachment {to your most Sacred person and government.

The bappinefs we have ever enjoyed under the mild and parental authority of your Majesty, and the prosperity derived from our constitutional dependence upon the government of Great Britain, have fixed io our minds, in the most indelible characters, the warmest principles of gratitude and affection, and excited in us a just resentment at those men, who, under false but specious pretences, have bad the art to delude io many of their feUow-subjects, and who, to efted their own wicked and ambitious purposes, have not scrupled to overwhelm this once happy and flourishing country with misery and ruin.

Convinced that our interest, as well as . our duty, requires our utmost exertions to procure a restoration of your Majesty’s government throughout America, we Shall press to give every affiStance in our power to accomplish that most desirable event. And although the very great efforts of your Majesty and the Parliament, which with due gratitude we acknowledge, have not hitherto had the fucccfs that in human probability was to have been expected; yet we truit that the period is fast approaching, when the infatuation will ceaSe, and the deluded people of this Continent will, with joy, recognifc your Majesty’s just authority, add sue for reconcilement with the parent-ftatc.

6 ’ sAmcrica: Proscriptton*;

It is with the utmost surprise, and the deepest concern, that we find some per* fons have so far mistaken the characters of your American subjects, as to conceive, that the people in general are dif-affected to your Majesty’s government; and, therefore, think ourfclvesindifpen-Tably called upon to allure yeur Majesty, that very many of your faithful fub-jects in America, rather than violate their duty, have abandoned their estates, and chearfully submitted to all the mile-Ties of penury and exile; that others, for being actively instrumental in supporting your Majesty’s government, after long confinement in dungeons and in chains, have fuflered death by the hands 'of the executioner; and although the dread of the sanguinary laws which have been enacted, and are daily multiplied, by the tyrannical American rulers, procures, in many instatices, a reluctant acquiescence under their usurped authori ty, yet we can With truth assure your Majesty, that whatever proper measures can be adopted, to call forth theftrength of your loyal American subjects, they will contribute an aid, which will give so detifive a superiority to the British cause, as will speedily restore your Majesty’s government, with peace and liberty to this distraCted country.

We most fervently pray that your Majesty, enjoying every possible felicity in this life, may long reign in the hearts and iffe&ions of all your subjects, and that every part of your extended empire may never cease to be governed by your po-fterity.

Signed by order and appointment of the Board chosen to represent the American Refugees,

Das. Coxe, President.

New York, Dec. 23. 1779.

“ New York, April 28. In March last the Congress passed a number of resolves, in which they announced the depreciation of their currency to be so great, that it was no longer sufficient to carry on business; —a circumstance well known and felt before. They declared themselves insolvent, and that forty of theii* dollars should be deemed equal to ond Span if!) dollar, which was coufequently paying only sixpence in the pound; they ordered the monthly alTeflment of fifteed millions of dollars from the Thirteen Colonies to be continued to April 1781, in-ftcad of October 1780, as they at first ordered; they repealed the laws by which Congress money was made a legal ten- der, and permitted gold and silver to become current, which before was prohibited; and they ordered that each State should, on its own credit, emit sums of money agreeably to the proportion formerly settled, four tenths of which sums should be at thedifposal of Congress, the other fix tenths appropriated to the use of the several states respectively; and these newly-emitted dollars were to bear interest, redeemable with hard money, dollar for dollar, at the end of six years, or by bills on their bankers in Europe, at the option of the possessors. These resolves had the effect, with respect to the Congress money, which any man of common fenle might forefec f they have wholly ruined its creditit is no longer current in most places; and the great-eft confusion and dissatisfaction prevail every where among the rebels. Whrft through the distresses of the people, and ' our naval fuccels,’ the rebellion trembles to its foundaf/on/’

Gentlemen,

I forgot to mention, in the letters I had the honour of writing to you, acir-camftance which may possibly be of some consequence to the officers of the 9th, aoth, sift, 24th, 47th, and 6»d regiments; which I request you will communicate to the colonels of those regiments, and such particular friends of the officers who may be within your knowledge. It is, that whatsoever promotions, by raising of new corps, or otherwise, in England, may take place in favour of the officers of the troops of convention, upon notice being sent of it, they can be immediately exchanged.— It becomes, therefore, a matter well worthy theconsideration of their friends; and as such 1 recommend it.—I am, &c.

Miff* Cox and Mair* W. Philips.

The difficulties under which the revolted colonies labour, may be gathered from an address to the citizens of Virginia after the arrival of the kihg’s army in the southern provinces, signed, By -vict if the Council, Thomas Jeffcrfon; —

from which we extra# a few passages, viz.

The depreciation of our paper-medium, if tried by the rules of ftrid rea* son, was real and great; but the artifices of your internal enemies were such, as to render it, in fad, many times greater than it should have been in reason. Sen-fible of this, your representatives in Con-grefs determined to issue no more; and your General Assembly have followed

I their example. As the original introduction of them was wife and salutary,

' so was the discontinuance of farthet emissions under our then and present circumstances. It became necessary, therefore, by other means, to raise money for public exigencies. The amount of the supplies necessary was considered by your assembly at their last fession, and they adopted means which bid fair for procuring the m. As far as they relied on you, upon application by way Of iax% heavy as is that application, we fee a readiness to comply with it, which so far leaves no fear of di(appointment. The rcfidueqC their dependence was a reasonable and just scheme of borrowing, addressed tq the interests of the monied men. This I has failed totally. Your treasury is at this moment destitute of money, and re-Vol.XLII.

celves It supplies, from the tardy band of the collector, only in such final! portions as his convenience dictates. A heavy invasion impends over our lifter*state of South Carolina. The south^ru states arc likely to be the seene of the enfping campaign, and ill-timed tenacloufnefs of private interest may advance it even to your own doors.—If the treasury do not re* ceive instant supplies for the use of the commifTariate, an Invasion, should it happen, will find yon without provisions to feed those of your brave countrymen who should enter the field for the defence of your altars and household: nay, farther, submiffinn heing of all misfortunes in this world the greatest, and of-position necessary, whatever violences it may call for, may we not fear, that our houses, the lacred palladiums of dome ftic happiness, may be thrown open to military scrutiny, and the pittance refer-ved for the consumption of our families, laid under contribution for the sustenance of our army! During the course of the present month, and the earlier part of the next, a moiety of the forces of this state, on Continental establishment, will leave the service, having continued the term of their original enlistments: we have reason to believe they might be re-enlisted to a man, could money be raised to pay the legal bounty; they are difei-plined, brave, and veteran, and habit has made them proof against the diseases of a camp. Their dispersion will be loss never to be retrieved. The involuntary fnbstitutes who must be dragged into their posts, to replace our number, will be a miserable exchange indeed.,r

300 America: A Popish funeral

The Bier covered with black cloth, Mons. Lucerne, the French Resident, The Congress,

The Citizens.

\Vheo the procession arrived at the Roman-Catholic chapel, the Prielt presented the holy water to Mons. Lucerne; who, after sprinkling himself, pre Tented it to Mr Huntington, Pre(id?nt of the Congress. The Calvinist paused a considerable time, near a minute; but at length his afft&ion for the great and good ally conquered all scruples of con-cience, and he too besprinkled and fane-ified himself with all the adroitness of a Tcteran Catholic; which his brethren of the Congress perceiving, they all without hesitation followed the righteous example of their proselyted President. Before the company, which were extremely numerous, left the chapel, curiosity induced some persons to uncover the bier; when they were highly enraged at finding the whole a (ham, there being no corpse under the cloth, the body of the Spanifii gentleman having been several day8 before interred at Morris-town. The bier was surrounded with wax candles, and every member of this egregious Congress, now reconciled to the Popish communion, carried a taper in his band.” “ York, May 6. Mr Joseph Reed, the present titular prelident of the council of Pennsylvania, has within fourteen days set his name to a proclamation, offering a reward of 1000 dollars for the head or scalp of every Indian or Tory taken on the frontiers, and 500 more for a prisoner.”

“ Williamjburgh, Vi, April 8. On

the 5th instant was hanged at Charles-town, Colonel Hamilton Baltndine, for drawing draughts of that city and its fortifications. He was taken by a piquet-guard, which Gen. Lincoln had sent out that night to Stono, as he was making his way to the enemy. When he was hailed by the guard, his answer was his own name; the guard told him that would not do, and carried him to the commander of the piquet; upon which he pulled out of his pocket the draughts. The officer tcld him he \va9 miltaken, and had him carried before Gen. Lincoln; who ordered him to execution.”

A citizen of Chavleflown writes thus. May 14. Gen. Lincoln would have surrendered before, but was prevented by the lieutenant-governor, the council, and

; Siege of Charlestown. Vol. 4*.

a few others, who (report lays) secreted the offers, and imposed on the citizens, by making them believe that the terma were quite different. However, th6 truth at length came out; when the inhabitants, to the amount of about 700, signed and sent in a petition to Gen. Lincoln to accept of the terms. Farfon Smith endeavoured to prevent the measure, although the town was invested on every fide, the fleet spread in our harbour, the British troops in possession of Cooper and Wando rivers, and of every station in Christ-church parish, Fort Moultry taken, James island guarded c-very way, Fenwick’s place, at Wappoo, equally guarded and strong, and the Town neck possessed by the army, fa that we could not stir either by land or water; our provision exhausted, except rice and coflee; not an ounce of meat bad been delivered to the military for some days before the surrender; the small pox in town; and a pestilential fever raging; seven doctors nek, and some of them dangerously ill.—I have not had an ounce of fresh provision this month past in my house. I had some bacon, which 1 was under the nectffily of hiding, for my house has been searched three times by the American militia and Tailors, who have plundered several hou-fes in open day, and God knows what situation we should have been in had the siege been raised. The English officers have been diligent in preventing abuses and plunder as far as possible. A small matter of rudeness will be sufficient fora northward gazette. — Our Governor, John Rutledge, Charles Pinckney, with l'cvcral others, have gone northwardly. Mr Bee was Lieutenant-Governor; but being ordered to Congress, Mr Christopher Gadidcn was appointed in his room; who, together with young Mr Laurens, Dr Ramsey, John Edwards, Tom Fcr-gufon, Charles Cotesworth Pinckney, Parson Smith, and a few others, who had their estates in the treasury, manifested a disposition to sacrifice both town and inhabitants. All the fuburb8 were destroyed last May as far as Strickland's by our own people; this expedition has carried the devastation further; RufTel’s distillery is erased, and all the woods beyond Burrows’s and Williams’s, ruined in a manner that would surprise you. Oa Thursday last the btfiegers, by carcasses, set fire to Lieut. Gadfdenrs house, which was cun fumed, together with all the row

upwards

Juno 178a upwards to the comer where Col. Isaac Motte lived, and both of Daniel Legare’s houses. Thirty-two and twenty-four pounders were sent into town frequently, from James island, Wappoo, and beyond the gates. Carcasses were thrown at far as the Exchange, where fifty tons of gunpowder were lodged. Several thirteen-inch (bells were also thrown in; one reached as far as the new church, which made a monstrous hole in the street; forae fell upon houses and did great execution; the cannon*balls also made a terrible noise, and from the very great nmber, it is wonderful that so few lives have been loft.— One half of the inhabitants are already reformed.'*

Whitehall, July 5. The following letters from Sir Henry Clinton, K. B. to Lord George Germain, were this day received, by Lt-Col. Bruce, one of Sir Henry's aids-de-camp, who arrived in the South-Carolina Packet.

v. 1 f" Head quarters* ,

My Lord,| SomhCaroU%mm4.1780.

I had the honour, in my difoatch, N° 88. by the Earl of Lincoln J_»6x-3* to communicate to your Lord ship the surrender of Charlestown. I am now able to give your Lordship a return of the prisoners taken, amounting, as you will observe, exclusive of near a thousand fail ora in arms, to 5618 men *.

1 informed your Lordship, that Lt-Gen. Earl Cornwallis was to march up the north fide of Santee, whilst another corps moved up the hither shore of that river towards the district of Ninety-six. These corps are in motion, as well as one up the Savannah river in Georgia.

The troops immediately under his Lordship’s command have pressed so effectually upon a body of the rebels which remained in the province, that the Earl, by detaching his corps of cavalry, and with them the legion infantry (mounted) has completed the deftrnCtion of every thing in arms against us in this province.

viz. % major-generals j brigadier-generals, 3 ms jots of brigade, id colonels, lieutenant-colonels, ai majors, 145 captains, 16% lieutenants, 41 cornets or ensigns,

a paymaster, 7 adjutants, 6 quaiteimatters, 28 surgeons, 6 mates, 3x9 ferjeanu, 137 dtummers, 4710 rank and file.

America: Letters from Gen. Clinton. 301

Lt-Col. Tarleton beaded this detach- ment, whose celerity in performing a march of near an hundred miles in two days, was equal to the ardor with which they attacked the enemy. These refuting the terms which were offered them, were charged, and defeated, with the loss of one hundred and seventy-two* killed, and some taken, together with the remaining field-artillery of the foutheru army, their colours and baggage.

With the greatest pleasure 1 further report to your Lordship, that the inhabitants from every quarter repair to the detachments of the army, and to this garrison, to declare their allegiance to the King, and to offer their services, in arms, in support of his government. In- many instances they have brought prisoners their former oppressors or leaders; and I may venture to assert, that there are few men in South Carolina, who arc not either our prisoners, or in arms with us«

1 have also the satisfaction to receive corresponding accounts, that the Ioya-lifts io the back parts of North Carolina are arming. 1 dare entertain hopes* that Lord Cornwallis's presence on that frontier, and perhaps within the province, will call back its inhabitants from their (late of error and disobedience.' If a proper naval force can be collected, I purpose fending a small expedition into Cape-Fear river, to favour the resolution I look for higher in the country.

I am, with the troops I could take, quitting the harbour of Charlestown, on my way to New York, hoping no foreign armament can yet have reached the coast, or have been able to attempt any thing in our absence against that place.

Lt-Col. Bruce, my aid-de-camp, will have the honour of presenting these dif-patches to your Lordship. He has fer-ved with distinction during this whole war, and is well able to satisfy your Lordship in any inquiries you may be pleased to make concerning the late operations in Carolina.

Your Lordship will receive by Mqj.-Gen. Prevoft, who fails in a few days, the account from Earl Cornwallis of what shall have occurred to that time.

I have the honour to be,

H. Clinton.*

SIR, Campditt, June %. 1780.

In my letter of the 30th of tass month, 1 inclosed a note from Lt-Col. Tarleton* wrote in great haste from the field of action, and I explained my reasons for fending the detachment under his command in pursuit of the enemy.

I have now the honour of transmitting to you his account of the march and engagement, with thelofson both fides.

I can only add the highest encomiums on the conduit of Lt-Col. Tarleton. It will give me the most sensible satisfaction to hear that your Excellency has been able to obtain for him some dtftinguished mark of his Majesty’s favour.

‘ My Lord, Wacfaw, March 30.1780.

I have the honour to inform you, that yesterday at three o'clock p. m. after a march of 105 miles in fifty-four hours, with the corps of cavalry, the infantry of the legion mounted on horses, and a three-pounder, at Wacfaw, near the line which divides North from South Carolina, the rebel force, commanded by Colonel Buford, confiding of the nth Viiginia, and detachments of other regiments from the same province, with artillery and some cavalry, were brought to attion.

After the summons, in which terms fi-milar to those accepted by Charleftowo were offered, and positively rejetted, the attion commenced in a wood. The attacks were pointed at both flanks, the front, and reserve, by 270 cavalry and infantry blended; and, at the fame instant, all were equally vittorious, few of the enemy escaping, except the commanding officer by a precipitate flight on borfeback.

It is above my ability to say any thing in commendation of the bravery and exertion of officers and men: 1 leave their

merit to your Lordship's confederation, I have the honour, &c. &c.

Ban. Tarleton, Lt-Col. Comm. Br. Legion. Lt-Cen. Earl Cortrwallit.”

THE late Duke of Montagu one evening, accompanied by two or three facetious friends, took a hackney-coach, and ordered the man to drive to the back of St Clement's. When they were arrived there, the Duke got out, and walked round the coach to the other door; and was, in lonfequence of a concerted plan, followed by his friends:

be made a party in annihilating the constitution, and dismembering the empire* the dernier resort was to be tried; the sovereign himself was to be attacked. That fine veil which this constitution had so wifely thrown round the person of the king was to be torn away, and every insult offered which could annihi* late the respect for an office so eflentiai to the constitution, in the mifreprefenT tations of the man who held it; and the sense of treason being in a manner ex-

ihey entered the coach on the other fide tinguiftied by gradual and daily usage, to where the man flood, and passed the grossest indecencies were made ufc through the coach one after another 0£ with that security and impunity eighteen times, to the aftonilhment of which cowardice required, and which it the coachman 5 who ran into the first found in attacking the only being ip.

public house be few, and in the utmost fright declared he bad been carrying a legion of devils, for he had counted eighteen of them, and they were coming o»t ftilL Vol.XUU

Britain who could not personally resent, and in whose favour the laws, by those perverted sentiments of the pmple, had lost their force. But the time will come Q^q wbc(\

306 Condufion of Mr Smelt’s speech* Vol^i.

when those infatuated men will fee the injustice as well as the absurdity of such a conduit, and when that sovereign, who neither could be brought to consent to the diminution of that empire which he was called to preserve entire, nor to despair of his means of defence, although the whole house of Bourbon thundered upon hiscoaft, will have ample justice done him in the hearts of every real friend of liberty, and of the glorious constitution of this country; and when, both from the example of his private virtues and his public firmntTs, real pa-triotism will receive its best encouragement.

Happy as we may be in such an example in so arduous a moment, this constitution would ill deserve the praises bestowed upon it by the wisest men of this and of every other country in Europe, if it depended rather upon the uncommon virtues of the prince, which an hereditary monarchy must render precarious, than upon the attributes of the sovereign. It is in that political perfection which this constitution has attributed to the sacred office of a British sovereign, that consists the shining part of the most admirable form of government that was ever conceived by the mind of any legislator. It is a false opinion, that the king is the servant of the public. He is the foul of the constitution, that which frees it from the tyranny of an aristocracy, and the anarchy of a democracy. The servants of the public are those ministers who surround him, through whom every alt that is done must pass, who arc alone respon-fible, amenable, and punishable. Nothing, therefore, can pass from the fove-Teign to the subject, but through the a-gencyof accountable servants; and thus the efTence of the constitution is never in danger, unless the madness or the wick-ednefs of the people render it so, as the sacrifice of the minister does not affelt it. Contempt, oravcrfion, may be nationally felt towards one minister, and his successor may fully restore the dignity of the office, because it is dependent upon the individual who fills it: but if the crown becomes contemptible in the eyes of the people, all its attributes fink for ever. That the king can do no wrong. That the king never dies, That the throne is never vacant, are ideas of the highest import ro the preservation of this constitution, and that liberty it was won-

I

derfully framed to preserve; and the difficulty felt at the Resolution, by the greatest men of that age, in making the word abdication con lift ent with the sense of the constitution, plainly shewed their knowledge of the importance of the royal office, for the preservation of that liberty they were then met to establish.

How different are the ideas which prevail in the present age ! Every man is now a legislator, and so far from being satisfied with that time-tried form of government, under which he finds himself, by which he had been protelted from infancy to manhood, and by which his property has been secured to him, he disdains its orders, arraigns its competition, and would 'totally change its form, as not coinciding with his own crude ideas of the state he would chuse to live under; and to effelt his purposes, avails himself of the moment in which the empire is struggling against the most powerful and most despotic of foreign enemies* The strange situation to which this poor country is now reduced, confirms too justly that which has been already obfer-ved, that Britannia, which formerly moved in a line so dignified as to draw the attention and respelt of all Europe, now finks down in a state of corruption, (and gives birth to millions of reptiles, each creeping their own separate way.

When those who possess, from an hereditary claim only, all the distinctions of society, and such an excessive disproportion of its benefits, as to have a thousand of their fellow-creatures employed in the hardest work, and with the poorest subsistence, to contribute to their case and luxury; — when such talk of the natural equality of men, and their right to change the government they live unr der, who can with-hold their aftonish-ment ? and yet this,ha9 been so long the fashionable language of this country, that all subordination and order, all decency is at an end; and has carried its effelts up to the crown; which, so far from having too great an influence, has been deprived of that most essential part of it which a proper education and early habits formerly implanted in the hearts of men; a general disposition to respelt it even in the warmest political contests.

Is the zeal of the present majority in parliament for carrying oo the war with vigour, against the most perfidious of enemies, meant to be produced as the proof of the undue influence of the

Grown ? n that majority who would not yield, to the independency of America; to the ruinous and di(graceful terms prescribed by the most ancient and most inveterate enemy of this country, France; to that uti pofftdetitof Spain, which was

to annihilate this country, by checking it in the only moment of effectual rcfift-ance, and by railing up a superiority of power against it during the cessation of arms; is a majority, 1 say, against such degrading proportions, the proof of the undue influence complained of! Iffo, Heaven grant that we. may never want such influence; and depend upon it, that this will be the universal prayer ot Britain. The tide of popularity will flow towards those men who have supported the empire under the greatest difficulties; and those who have preached up doCtrines which must tend todifgrace us as a nation, will flnk to their proper level.

Recover yourselves, citizens of Britain, if possible, from these delusions. Examine well those who are loudest a-tnong you; and fee whether there arc no profeffed republicans, no supporters of aristocracy amongst them. If they > have a Tight to change the constitution, what becomes of your property, what of your tenures? The leafcholder, the copyholder, nay the no-holder of any thing, may claim your freeholds: all right is annihilated, or endangered, when the government from which it is derived is annihilated, or cffentially changed. Unequal property can never be derived from the boasted natural rights or cqua-» lity of men. Anarchy, and its certain

i follower, defpotism, can only arise out

| of such doCtrines. Be affiured that in this constitution, as it now Hands, true j liberty, protection, and security, to per-suns and property, find their surest basis.: That fraall proportion of virtue which is

pecefTary for an elector to do justice to himself in his choice of a difcretc, fenfi*

1 blc, dispassionate man, for his reprefen-tative, and one whose fortune is sufficient to set him above the necessity of corruption, will ever preserve this consti-tntion. No changes in modes of election, no further exclulions from offices, are necessary j as such exclulions could only operate in preventing the men who are the ablest, and the fittest both for the senate and the ftatc,to become more useful to their country, by their double capacity \which can never be impoted up-

on the electors, as a re-elcCtton mnft follow the appointment.

The last object of this petition is, “ That no new taxes may be granted till effectual measures are taken for this general reform, the produce of which is to be applied to the necessitics of the state.” And as the two subsequent resolutions of the meeting, in favour of the petition, relate expressly to this part of it, viz. “ That a committee be ap-’ pointed to carry on the necessary correspondence for effectually promoting the object of the petition; and to prepare a plan of an association, on legal and constitutional grounds, to support that laudable reform, and such other measures, as may conduce to restore the freedom of parliament,*’ it may be proper to consider the whole under one view.

The money which is to be raised for the increasing efforts of the war, and for the immediate subsistence of the present means of defence, constitutes that loan, for the payment of the interest of which, new taxes must be granted: the loan, therefore, although instantly wanted for defensive as well as offensive war, must be delayed, till the object of the petition is effectually carried into execution; and as the measure petitioned for is a measure of latitude, the epithet effectual must likewise be deemed so. Who then can be the judges of its latitude, but those who first made use of it ? and that they indeed constitute themselves so, is evident from the subsequent refolatlons. The House of Commons must therefore correspond with the committees of reform; the committees of reform with each other, as well as with their full' meetings; and of course draw on a long difeuffion, before they can deem the term tffedual complied with, so as to proceed to the taxes, and of course to the existence of the loan. And should' not the House of Commons think themselves obliged to pay this attention to this awful committee, but proceed as their conscience and wisdom may direct, the association come9 in aid of this committee. Is this aid to be understood, a9 was dropped at the meeting, uncenfu-red ? are they to be told, that Ireland gained its object by 60,000 men in arms ? is this the mode the association is to adopt, for effectually carrying on this laudable reform ? Ireland in her most biting necessities granted the taxes for fix Qjl % months.

months, a tune sufficient for the Britishi parliament to judge of and resolve upon her petition; tad Ihe has generosity to be convinced that (he had been hasty in the hoftite appearance (he nude, since justsce alone would have effected her purposes. But British petitioners do not only threaten, but they with-hold the iastant means of defence against a most powerful and most insidious eoemy at our door. Surely those respectable names which accompanied the first reqtieft for this meeting, could not have intended measures which carry such extraordinary appearances; appearances which mult have greatly alarmed, had there been the lead time given to conli-der them. But it is well known, that these subsequent resolutions were read in great haste at the conclusion of the meeting, after the passing of the petition, and in a manner that did not seem as if they were meant to be deliberately considered. But it is unnecessary to say more, to call the attention of the public to these subsequent resolutions, since they speak, as they were meant to do, in thunder.

When the state of this empire is considered, in the moment in which this petition is brought forth,—a moment in which the astonishing efforts of this country were railing it to a superiority over the forces of the whole House of Bourbon, and its own revolted colonies, — a moment in which the common danger was awakening again the and an-

nihilating that narrow felfilhnefs which counteracted the consolidation of the whole empire, — when the true principles of trade were beginning to be understood; which prove, that it should take its feat with equal freedom in every part of the empire, availing itself of every local advantage and produce, «— when the navigation*act, in America, and the restraints In Ireland, would be Judged as prejudicial to the whole empire, as if they existed in London,— and when, out of our evils, had arisen that liberality of mutual advantage, which most consolidate the empire, more than it could have been under that selfish character which pervaded every part before theconteft,—when there wanted nothing but temper and unanimity in the mother-country, to open the eyes of America to her true interest, and to effect a complete union of the whole empire under common advantage, common liber-

ty, and common support, the means for which might be settled without admit* ting the least possible injustice to the parts; — at such a moment to give (auction to dtvifioo, and to tell all our enemies that they might expect, from our internal coovulhons, what their united arms could not effect, is indeed a melancholy, and mottuoexpc&ed event.

A new method of Ro*t-Grefti*gf~from a tift9 intuitd , A new Grafting and Inoculation,

fT,0 perform it, take a graft or sprig of a A young tree, which you ioteod to pcopa-

5ate, and a small piece of the root of ano-ler tree of the fame kind, or of a like genus, and whip-graft them together; oblcrving that the rind of the root join closely to that Of the graft. The piece of root will feed the graft, as the (lock does the other way.

This method of propagating fruit-trees »♦ both easy and expeditious; roots being more plentiful than (locks. By this method the pieces, or roots, of one erab-stock, for example, or of one apple-dock, will ferye for twenty or thirty apple-grafts; and the like of other trees, it is also an excellent method for railing such trees as will hardly bear grafting in the dock. Add, that trees thus grafted, bear (ooooer, and are more easily dwarfed, than any other way.

In this treatiie, reiterated grafting, or grafting by a double and treble incifioo, is recommended.
