He knew it might be objected, that summer was an improper time for parliament to fit; and that if it should be required that the parliament should fit beyond its usual time, the business of the nation would not be a jot the better attended to, as both business and pleasure would call away the greatest part of the members, and leave the benches empty. For his part, be did not care how empty the benches might be; he did not care how the House was filled, provided the objects he had in view were fulfilled. He had engagements both of a profitable and pleasurable nature; and he was ready to sacrifice both for the public good. — The objcCt of the county-petitions was twofold: to promote (economy in the expenditure of public money, and to leflfcn the influence of the crown. The former might perhaps be answered by the hill brought in by the Noble Lord in the blue ribbon, for appointing a commission of accounts: the latter must be answered by various expedients. A large foundation had been laid by the resolution of the 6th of April: it could not be.supposed that the resolutions he had hitherto proposed could raise the fu-perstructure to any height proportionable to the foundation; it must be the business of time to find out materials for that purpose, and it was to gain that time that he had resolved to draw up the following motion, which he then made, “ That an humble address be presented to his Majesty, reqtiefting that he would not dissolve the parliament, nor prorogue the present fession, until proper measures shall have been taken by this House to diminifit the influence of the crown, and to correct the other evils complained o. in the petitions of the people.”

Mr T, Pitt seconded the motion.* The aueftion before the House was big with the fate of the nation: if the waters of discontent should be collected by the rejection of it, and he made no doubt but they would, the torrent would mock all bounds; it would sweep all before it;

for no one could say to it, So far thou shalt go, and no farther.” He therefore conjured the House to adopt the motion, and thereby prevent those dif-afters which he feared would fall upon the nation if it should be rejected.

Lord Nugent opposed the motion, for two reasons: 1. Because it was impoffi-ble to judge how long gentlemen might make the parliament fit if they should carry their motion; and, 2. Because the measures that might be proposed, and for the passing of which the parliament was to be kept fitting, did not depend solely upon the House of Commons, as another branch of the legislature must debate every matter before it could pass into a law. Upon the whole, the measure smelt strongly of 1641* when the parliament was made perpetual. The distradtions among the people were certainly alarming; by Handing on American grounds the present opposition had certainly, till very lately, been very unpopular; he recommended, therefore, moderation to them in their prosperity; for if the present war, and rage for reformation, should continue much longer, we should haveabankrupttreafury, a difeon-tented people, and an impaired constitution.

MrTownfhend said, he had not flood tipon American, but on English ground, and there he had opposed the measures ofadminiflration.

Mr Adam expressed the greatest respect for many of the names subscribed to the petitions, and for many of the persons who were of the different committees. They were men of the greatest rank, the greatest property, and the greatest abilities. No intentional danger to the constitution could be attributed to such men. But he could not carry his deference to them to such excess, as to bind up his own understanding, and fetter his judgement. It became requisite, therefore, before he proceeded to the question, to consider how far the petitions were binding upon parliament. Many people say, that the petitions must be binding: but ff you examine upon what ground any person, the greatest favourer of the people, makes that affection, it is because he agrees in opinion with the prayer of those petitions. But let us suppoie, that those petitions went farther than any person in this house thinks they ought to go, to alterations in the ^onflitution that we are all agreed in

thinking detrimental: should we not all agree, that such a petition would not, ought not to bind the House ? If that is the case, we ought to hear no more of that argument so derogatory to the independence of this House, that this, that, and t'other thing, must be done, because it is required by the people in their petitions, but ought to difeufseach question upon its special merits.

He then came to the motion, and said, it naturally divided itself into two heads; to add refs the crown not to prorogue the present session, or to dissolve the present parliament. With respcdfc to prorogation, he was well aware there were many instances of such motions: but with respect to dissolution, not knowing what the motion was to be, he bad had not an opportunity of examining the journals as to the precedents; but be believed it was the first instance of such a motion; and he was confirmed in that opinion, by the Hon. Gentleman who made the motion not taking notice of any precedent. It was hardly to be supposed, that a person so learned in the parliamentary, as well as the municipal law of the kingdom, would have passed it over, if there had been any such. As to the case of 1641, which made the long parliament, he did not conceive it to be at all applicable to the present purpose. The circumstances of passing that act were so remarkable, that it was impossible to forget it. It was carried to the King for the Royal assent along with the bill of attainder of Lord Strafford; and Charles is supposed to have been so much occupied with the cruel necessity of giving his assent to that bill, that the other passed unnoticed. But they differ in this respect: In 1641, it was a bill, this is an address. And they further differ, that the septennial act extinguishes this parliament at the end of seven years; at that time no fixed duration being appointed for parliament, that act rendered it perpetual, that is, gave it existence till the grievances of the people were redressed, while these grievances were In the creation of those who promoted the bill. But he did not mean to argue, that because there was no precedent for addressing the crown not to dif-folvc the parliament, that therefore we have not a right so to address the crown. That right is one of those that he ever would maintain in this House. But the motion was to be adopted with greater

^aution^

.caution, on account of its being unprecedented.

But it it necessary to consider what the consequence of this motion is to be. If you arc to redress the abuses complained of, it must be done by bill; if that bill is rejected either in this House or the other House, it is impossible, according to the law of parliament, to bring that bill back again during the fame fclfion; and therefore no good can arise from it. And it is probable that the lame parliament that rejects the bill this fession, will rejetft the fame bill next felCon.

In aquestion that relates to parliament fitting to redress grievances, we should know the extent of them. The petitions were Ample and intelligible, but the explanation given of them amounted to a total change in the constitution. — The petitions prayed for three things; an abolition of unmerited pensions, of useless finecurcs, and of the exorbitant emoluments of necessary offices. And this they pray for in order to attain the reduction of influence, and the promoting oeconomy. But as these petitions are explained by saying, they pray for a free parliament, every species of alteration of the constitution, dangerous, and perhaps destructive to the happiness of the people, is brought under this head. Annual parliaments, and an increased representation, were already proposed; and if we were to form a calculation of the firides that reformation had taken from the first York meeting [50.] to the last meeting in Westminster-hall [171.], it would be difficult to say, to what lengths we might go in innovating upon a constitution, in which civil and political liberty was more extended, was more complete, than it ever had been in any age or any country. That the liberty of antiquity was narrow and confined when compared with that of this country. In the republics of ancient times, a few individuals enjoyed perfect freedom, while the great bulk of mankind were the absolute slaves and property of those few. Here slavery was unknown, and a complete enjoyment of every valuable right, of every blessing society can bestow, was extended to eight millions of people, unimpaired and uncncroached upon. Was it a matter of small importance to agree to a motion that might impair this glorious system ? Had any infringement been made upon the great bulwarks of our liberty, which demanded such an alteration ? External grievances h.ul ta-

ken place; and the Noble Lord below him [Nugent] had given an epithet.to the war, which sufficiently described the extent of those calamities. But was it wife, was it just, was it reasonable, to new-model the constitution, and run a risk of destroying what our ancestors had handed down to us as the first of blef-sings ? to make internal changes, bc-cause we had external grievances to complain of? Was it to be supposed, that an increased representation, or annual parliaments, would ease us of the necessary taxes, diminish the burthen of the national debt, or bring about that safe and honourable peace, which wc must all agree in wiihing to fee accomplished

He then went into the subject of tells, as they had been spoken of, and proposed, at some of the public meetings; fta-ted their unconditional tendency; laid* that he had always entertained the most perfect con vision, that an obligation to obey the instructions of constituents, was unconstitutional *. This being the Cafe, he reprobated the idea of tells, as being Hill more destrultive of the freedom of parliament. The very essence of parliament was the power of deliberation; and the House should be equally1 jealous, and equally averse to every thing that destroyed that privilege. Its consequences were equally detrimental* whether it flowed from the undue influence of the crown, or the undue influence of the democracy. It was the business of every man who loved the constitution, to reprobate every test that fettered parliamentary freedom. [Here Mr Townihend said across the House, * there are no such tells.”] Mr Adam took up that assertion with great animation. Does the Hon. Gentleman mean to assert, that no such thing was talked ever of ? does he mean to say, that no meeting has ever difeufled the idea, and approved of it ? And if we are to judge of the spirit of reformation, of the rapid progress it is making, in’ time to come, by what has already passed, will it be at all furpriQng to find the spirit of this constitution ddlroyed, the liberty of parliament annihilated, and a wild democracy controuling in every inltance the wisdom of the legislature? gq. The history or all ages evinces, that reformers have always exceeded their original designs. It is not that I impute an intention to produce those evils to any one. He agreed with the Hon. Gentleman who seconded the motion, [Mr Pitt], that you could not say to the people, “ Thus far (halt thou go, and no farther.1' it was upon that principle he wished to avert an appeal to that quarter. The madness of popular reformation had never failed to deprive those of power who wish to check it, and to place it in the hands of those whose desperate situation makes them rejoice in changes. Those who wish to carry innovation to a certain length, will find, that their withdrawing themselves when it exceeds that limit, will be of no avail. — He agreed perfectly with an Hon. Gentleman, who spoke of the virtues of the last century, in the reign of Charles I. in all the language of pane* gyric. If ever there was a time in which human nature displayed a pre-eminence of virtue and capacity, it was upon the breaking out of the troubles of that period. But the hiltory of that period proves the dangerof innovation, when begun, even from the most powerful reasons, and the most upright motives. In the beginning of that unfortunate prince’s reign, every dangerous encroachment had been made in the political and civil liberty of the country. The property, even the life, of the individual, was hardly safe. It became the great, the virtuous characters of those days, to maintain their freedom, and to check the encroachments of a mi (taken monarch. Yet the virtues, the abilities, of those times, were not able to stop the wild spirit of reformation when it bad once got loose. The eminent characters that

withdrew themselves from the popalat cause when it had gone too far, in vaift attempted to re-establish the constitu* tion. They had allowed the defperatl, the needy, and the unprincipled, to fix themselves in power, beyond the poffi-bility of controul. Then it was, that the amiable, the virtuous, the enlightened motives of Lord Falkland were of no avail; then it was that Lord Northumberland, Lord Manchester, and the Earl of Essex bimfeif, in vain relinquished the party whose power they had too fatally established. And what was the consequence of that fatal spirit? The person who got the power, firitt voted one part of the other House useless, then voted the whole of it unuecefiary. Armed men were brought into the lobby, to detain those members in custody who were likely to have voted against those violent resolutions. At last, the only remaining part of the constitution, which indeed was so garbled, that it was hardly valuable, was dilTolved. And how was this cffc&ed ? Three file of mufqucteers were brought within that House. The mace was taken from the table. And when this violent step was opposed by a young man of considerable falhion, of great abilities, of remarkable eloquence, whose quickness of conception enabled him to seize every advantage for his party, whose pre-eminence was great when the Protestor's political existence had not begun, upon whose (boulders Cromwell rose to power; when this person opposed the dissolution of the House, Cromwell seized him by the cloak, and in the ludicrous language of those times said, “ Oh ! Sir Harry Vane 1 Sir Harry Vane! the Lord deliver me from Sir Harry Vane.’* Thus did he take the mace from that table, and establish the most oppressive and arbitrary defpotism that ever cursed a country. Mr Adam said, he thanked God there was a great difference between those times and these; that then encroachments on the liberties of mankind induced the greatclt and most virtuous to seek redress, while at present the liberties of the people remained unimpaired. But, he said, all attempts to appeal to the people were dangerous. Id describing youth, he fays, * Youth grasps more than it can hold, stirs mure than it can quiet, and flies to the cod without considering the means.” He wished those who appealed to the people, and made them the instrument of reformation and change, might not grasp more than they can hold, stir more than they can quiet, and fly to the end without confidenng the means.

Mr Adam then begged leave to recal the house to the question. The present motion, he said, if carried, implied, that parliament must agree to whatever should be set forth in petitions as necessary to be done: without supposing parliament was to do so, the motion was unnecef-lary and nugatory; therefore parliament must be supposed by implication to be obliged to redress what are, or what are called abuses: A doctrine so ruinous, so onconftitutiona), that he never could subscribe to it; and trusted that the House would not give its assent to such a principle, by adopting the present motion.

Mr Fox spoke next. He said, the ' House had solemnly pledged themselves to destroy that baneful influence which undermines the liberty of the country: unless they agreed to the motion, they betrayed the people, by leaving its in the power of the minister to prorogue or difiolve the parliament before the means of redress are applied. If the motion ibould pass, though it did not bind the crown, there still remained in the executive power the ability to difiolve the parliament, or prorogue the present session: yet, he believed no minister would be so rash, or so wicked, as to advise his Majesty to difiolve the parliament, when that House had addressed him not to do it. He trusted there was yet enough of weight and of power in that House, to make good its own resolutions, and to carry them into effect; that the respect ' In which the executive power held its advice, was too great to admit of a con-dud contrary to its wifties, properly and humbly expressed. He then alluded in the most glowing expressions of rhetoric to the Etuation of the country, and said, that upon the fate of the present question depended, whether this constitution, which had been the glory and pride of the world, was to remain the boast of mankind; or whether Englishmen were again to fight for their liberties, were again to take the field in opposition to ar-

bitrary power 5 and whether the days of anarchy and defpotism, which the Hoffi Gentleman had gone over, were to recur, and after a seene as mortifying as that struggle bad taken place; a rest oration was to follow upon those abject terms of fcrvility and meanness, which was perhaps more disgraceful to this country than the seene that preceded it on bringing back Charles II. without terms or Amputation.

The Hon. Gentleman, he observed, had said, that the motion supposed a ne-ceflity on parliament, implicitly to comply with what the petitions required; and that either House of Parliament, by refuting bills offered for the redress of abuses, might prevent any advantage from the motion being carried. The motion, he laid, was, to address the King, not to prorogue the session, or difiolve the parliament, till measures were taken to redress the abuses complained of: did the taking of some measures oblige parliament to do any thing that was improper? Must not some measure be taken to comply with the petitions ? and was it not necessary, if they meant to grant the people their just requests, to keep parliament fitting till that was done ? And though bills might be refused, in either House, was it not possible to redress abuses by other means than by a bill? Might not that house, for instance, address his Majesty, to instruft his miuifters, not to give a contract to a member of parliament [188.] i and would it not be a matter that the executive power would hardly chuse to deny upon an address of that House? The grievances of the people would be heard, ought to be heard, nay must be heard.

The Hon. Gentleman had talked of tests to members of parliament being uncon ftitutional. Though they had been talked of, Mr Fox laid, they had not been adopted but in three places, Yorkshire [218.], Middlesex [2x8.], and the city of Westminster [174.], Ls*r Joseph Mawbey taid, “ and Surry.’j. — Well, then, four places have agreed to tests. But, continued he, suppose them general, is it remarkable, that those who are going to vote for members of parliament should be desirous of knowing the sentiments of the person they are toele&, upon any particular and important subject that is to come under parliamentary dil-cufiion ? A test is only a means to come at the opinions of those we are to chuse.

3t$ Parliament s On prorogation or dissolution. Vol. 42;

He took notice of the rejection of the contract-bill, by the Lords fiM.]: did not every part of that business shew, laid be, that mioifterial influence had interfered? that the rejection was carried, contrary to the sense of the majority of hereditary and independent Peers, by the Scotch Lords and the bench of Bl-ffiops? Gentlemen argued about the right of the House of Lords. They had a right: but would they dare to counteract the wishes of the people in matters in which they themselves had no concern? — He concluded, that the motion was so material for what the House had pledged itself to, that he could not believe it would be rejected.

The Lord Advocate opposed the motion. The contract-bill, he said, was a ridiculous bill, brought in merely to court popularity, and with a view to 4am favour with the mob. [Here he was called to order by Mr Townshend]. He rofc again, and said, he could oot but think it a little unfair, to be debarred from speaking of a mere abortion with some little freedom, when he, from day to day, heard acts of the legislature, acts now in full force, reprobated without mercy. He saw no reason why the strangled child should be more gently handled, than the maturer offspring of the legislature. He paid, the highest 'Compliments to the Lord Chancellor, for his conduct respcCting the bill; declaring, that it well became a man of his manly mind, of his majcftic foul, of his solid judgement and excellent understanding, to act exactly as he had done respcCting it. That Noble and Learned Lord, he was sure, would always treat nonsense so whenever it came into his hands. He would neither court one fide nor the other; neither dread popular resentment, nor be influenced by ministerial favour; but would walk (trait forward, and do his duty as became a British Peer in parliament. . He reprobated the motion, which he ludicrously termed a recruiting officer, sent out by.oppolition to beat up . for grievances and enlist motions!

Gen. Conway supported the motion warmly.

Ld George Germain spoke against the question, as a mode of interfering with the prerogative of the crown, neither warranted by proof of its being necessary, nor supported on any grounds of good argument. His Lordship defended the conduct of the other House, and quoted,

as a case in point to their conduct respcA-ing the contract-bill, a Peerage-bill ient down by them to the Commons, which the Commons rejected as an attempt to alter the conffitution, which they at the time declared the Lords had no right to meddle with. His Lordship argued upon the rights of the House of Commons, describing its power over the purse of the people as sufficient to answer every end the Commons were cooftitutionally meant to have at their will and pleafnrc.

Col. BarrC made an animated reply, stating two extrads, the one from the parliament-roll of Edward 111. in which that prince called a parliament expressly to hear and redress the grievances of the people, and for no other purpose. The Colonel said, he took his extract from Lord Somers's remark on the passage; which remark he read to the House: the amount of it was, the learned Lord’s declaration, that the passage (truck him u> forcibly, that be wiffied it possible to have had it transcribed with a run-beam! The other extract the Colonel read, was from an old Latin book, intitled. Da /#-ntndo parliamtntot; and dated, that if a King dissolved or prorogued his parliament while one petition of the people, complaining of grievances, remained uaredrefled, that King was perjured.

Upon a division the motion was rejected; Ayes 203, Noes 254.

Mr Dunning moved, that his committee should be adjourned to Monday next.

Mr Fox said, he wiffied he had adjourned it for ever; for the House had that day completely rejected the petitions of the people, and bad falsified and broken their words with them: that they had violated their faith, and refeinded the resolution of the 6th of April.

Ld North laid, there was yet time for deliberation, and for business; and though a majority bad rejected the motion of to day, the House might be very seriously inclined to attend to the prayers of the petitions.

Mr Fox said, they muff leave the House, if those members, who, after having declared that the influence of the crown ought to be diminished, had voted against every meafute that tended to that effcCt, did not themselves propose measures; /which he thought they were pledged to do, and which he trusted they would.

Capt. Gowland arrived in London, April 29. from Calcutta, with di (patches for government, which he brought by ' sea to Baflbra, and from thence by land through Aleppo, Asia the Left, Con-ftantinople, and Vienna. In his way from Baflbra to Aleppo, being acccom-. panied only by fix guards, a servant, and an interpreter, he was attacked three times by the wild Arabs; against whom he defended himself: but within a day’s journey of Aleppo hewasfnrrounded by a hundred barbarous people called «/, by whom he was plundered of his money, deaths, and other etfe&s. Capt/ Gowland saved, however, bisdifpatches, by fitting upon them, and swearing that be would only surrender them with his life. The object of the dispatches is to bring the complaints both of the British and native inhabitants of Bengal against the supreme court of judicature* The English law, in particular, of arrests and bail, is To totally contrary to the ideas of the people of the East, that it is regarded by them as in the bigheft degree odious and oppressive. He brought an account of the lofsof the Stafford, a com-pany'sship, in Bengal river, with 80,0001. jo fpccic aboard.

A petition to the King, or the legislature, is received, from the British inhabitants of Bengal, Bahar, and Orissa, signed by above 600 Whites, and a great number of Gentoos; stating the various hardships the inhabitants have differed since the introduction of the English laws amongst them[35. 3*8.;—36.167- ]; that not only is their property under former acquirements unsafe in their hands, but that a number of acts now made crimes, leave them in that (late of ignorance and suspense that they know not what to do; — and therefore praying, to obtain a trial by jury in all cases where it is by law established in England; to limit the rctrospective powers of the supreme court of judicature to the time of its establishment in Bengal; to define, beyond the power of discretional distinction, the persons who are and who are not amenable to the jurisdiction of the court; to declare what statutes shall, and what ftatntcs shall not, be in force in Bengal; to appoint diltinlt and separate judges for the law and equity lides of the court; and to lodge a power of . Vol. XLlIf

(saying executions in criminal cases, till his Majesty’s pleasure be known, in the Governor-General and Council.

Advices from India mention a fire happening in the company’s warehouse in Bengal, by which thirteen lacks of raw lilk were consumed. A fouadron, under the command of Com. Walsingham, with the West India outward *bound fleet under convoy, (ailed from St Helen* on the 8th of April; as did, about the same time, A dm, Greaves with another squadron.

The Invincible was to nave failed with one of those squadrons; but the men refused to go until they were paid their wages. Adm. Sir Thomas Pye sent Capts Balfour, Duncan, Nott, and Buck-’ ner, to persuade them to do their duty.

But the men refused to go to sea till they were paid their wages, though they were not intitled, by the act of parliament for regulating the payment of wages in men of war, to demand payment till a full month after: they founded their claim on the Thunderer’s ship’s company having been paid a few days before, when exactly under the famecir-cnmstances. Upon this the four captains seized four of the mutineers, who seemed to be ringleaders, and car- > ried them to the Admiral; by whom they were ordered into dose confinement. Next morning, the Admiral directed Capt. Falconer, of the Invincible, to tell his ship’s company, that if they would go to sea, and promise to be- 1 have well, he would release the four 1 men. But they would not agree.

They behaved peaceably, obeying' all i the orders of their officers very readily, except that of putting to sea, which they absolutely refused, till the boats of the whole fleet were armed, and furr ounded I the Invincible, and till Lord Longford’s ship the Alexander was srarped alongside of her, and pointed her guns to fire s broad fide among them. Then their ob-ftinacy gave way, and tftey submitted to their officers, who were on board the whole time. A court-martial was held on the mutineers, April 18. two of them were ftntenced to receive 500 ladies each; the rest were acquitted.

It is observed by a writer in the London papers, that the (laughter on board Blips in action is principally caused by the fptinters which fly to a great extent on every fide when a ball enters through a ship's fides. /To prevent such daughter, every ship in the French navy is lined within-fide, as far as the lower deck, with a strong dote netting, which catches or hangs the splinters; and it has been Ibuod, that in every action, many fewer are killed since, than before this netting was used. Some Britishi privateers have already adopted this plan, and the writer hopes it will be adopted by the British navy.

M London, June%i, Certain advice is received from Macao, a Portuguese settlement in the river Canton, of the arrival of the Resolution and Discovery in great distress, and in want of provisions. Upon the death of Capt. Cook, Capt. Clerke succeeded to the command of the two ships, and Lient. Oore to be captain of the Discovery; but on the death of Capt. Clerke, a fatal misfortune to the world in general, and his friends in particular, Lieut. King succeeded to his place.” [46.]

Notice was given from the general post-office, June 19. That “ Charlestown being restored to the King’s possession, the direct intercourse between G. Britain and the Southern provinces of North America through that town will be renewed, and a packet-boat will (ail every month from Falmouth as formerly; the first mail to be dispatched from that office on the 5th of July.

On Sunday, July 4. the Earl /of Surrey (the Duke of Norfolk’s son) and Sir Thomas Gascoygne read their recantation from the errors of the church of Rome before the Archbishop of Canterbury, and received the sacrament. They have since taken the oaths before Mr Baron Hot ham. His Lord A) ip is candidate , for Carlisle, and Sir Thomas for Bever-Jey.

On the 10th of April, Reed a plaister-er, and Smith a coachman, sentenced for fodomitical practices, were carried from the New Goal privately, in a hackney-coach (to save them from the mob), and set in the pillory at St Margaret’s-hill; The under-ftieriffs, with their officers, and a great number of constabless, attended. The unhappy wretches were* nevertheless, so severely pelted by the populace, that in half an hour Smith funk down; in which position he remained, till he appeared black in the face the blood goshing from his ears; when he was taken out, and laid on the pillory. When carried back to the gaol, a surgeon was sent for; but Smith was found to be dead; and Reed’s recovery Was doubtful. On an inquisition taken, April 12. it appeared to the coroner and his jury, that Reed turning round fader than usual, and Smith being just then seized with a giddiness and fainting from the extreme severity of the populace, loft the strength of his legs, and hung by his head. The jury’s verdict was, “ Strangled in the pillory.”—The Solicitor-General moved the court of king’s-bench, April 20. for an attachment against the under-(heriff, for his not preventing this mischief. But Lord Mansfield, upon hearing the affidavits, said, they did not appear to prove any inattention in the under-sheriff: so the affair was deferred till further inquiry should be made.— Can a country be laid to be civilized where such barbarity is committed at mid day in the capital!

On the 19th of April MrGrattanmade a motion in the House of Commons for a declaration of right. He said. It becomes the Commons of Ireland this day to lift up their hands against the oppression of Ergliffi l.uvs, by a declaration of right. The people call upon the Commons, as the guardians of public liberty, and conservators of their rights. Commerce, indeed, we have got from England, but ret constitutiun ! The very manner in which they have given that commerce, by afcribir.g it to expediency to give us trade, shew* the absolute necessity of a declaration cn our part. A trade so circumstanced, is a trade but rot dc iun.

It may be alledged, that the time is

inopportune, whileG.‘ Britain is erobarrafc fed. I insist, for that very reason, the time is now the only one to speak to her, while file smarts under the effects of the American war, which her wild fehemes of parliamentary supremacy has brought, on, and which ideal supremacy (he has given up to America[i99.]; whileherenemies are unnumbered, her councils dif-tra&ed, without a minister, without an admiral of consequence, or a general that (he has not disgraced. Why, it may be aiked, did not the Commons of Ireland make their claim before ? Because Ireland had no Commons before in whom the nation confided* A spirit is raised; the nation is in arms, inspired with a martial and a constitutional spirit. You cannot, you dare not, deny the call of your constituents; it is not the command of a Buckingham, a North, or an HilKborough, but of — God

You afited a free trade, and received it with joy: it was the rapture of a beggar receiving more alms than he expected, not the joy of a man receiving his just right, you may export bales of drapery, yards of frize, and ells of bays; but freedom is denied to you. The foundation of the temple of Liberty cannot be laid, and you are but a provincial assembly, without the privileges of a charter. But let us hear what Great Britain fays on this great subject. [Here he read the declaratory act [36.45$.] 6° Geo. I.]. I now appeal, continued he, to the gentlemen of the long robe, to the judges, and to the justices of the peace, whether they do not execute English laws, and trample on the charter of our land? Are you, the Commons of Ireland, then afraid, with three millions of people at your back, to tell the parliament of England,~ you are a parliament ? Is the government of this kingdom to continue only with the general opinion; and will the volunteer army of Ireland be satisfied by a government of connivance?

Eighteen counties alk a modification of the law of Poyning, and a declaration of right, from the mifrepresentations of governors that loft America. Our governors opposed our demanding a free trade, and now are throwing themselves on some of the brft men in this country* to supprrfs our further claims. I conjure those men, I conjure the country-gentlemen, not to give the final a flint to the money-bills, until our constitution is secured.

He then moved to resolve, That the King’s Most Excellent Majesty, and the Lords and Commons of Ireland, are the only power competent to bind, or enalt laws in this kingdom.”

The Attorney General proposed to adjourn the question until the first day of September next; and assigned for reaIons, if the resolution proposed should take place, it would militate against many Knglifl) acts of parliament, which heretofore disposed of millions of acres in this kingdom; that almost every family in this country would be extreme sufferers, by haring their titles invalidated to what they had long enjoyed as an hereditary right, and which would be liable to impeachment and forfeiture. At the same time he reprobated all English acts of parliament which should be attempted to be enforced in Ireland, without being vecognifed by the frish legislature.

, MetT. Forfter, Fitzgibbon, Flood, and the Provost, spoke against the motion; and Mefl*. Burgh, Yelverton, &c. for it.

The Provost pointed out various laws made since the reign of Henry II. tending to ihew, that Ireland was a separate and distinct kingdom, not bounden by any legislature but her own; and deci-fionsby the judges of England, importing, that Ireland, having a distinct parliament, could not be taxed by the parliament of England, nor could any law of restraint be binding without the recognition of the Irish parliament; and that the rights

Ireland were so well founded, and known, that the declaratory resolution was needless. He added, that the refutation, if carried, would involve this country in new difficulties, and be an ungenerous return to G. Britain for her late commercial concessions to this kingdom, when fhewasftruggling against the united powers of Bouibon and America, and seemed inclined to grant us every thing we could ask.

Mr Fitzgibbon having observed, that a similar resolution was entered into by -the Commons of Ireland iu 1641, Mr Burgh moved an amendment, viz. “ That there being on the books a resolution G-miUr to the one now moved, the const-

deration of the fame be, for that reason, adjourned to the first day of September next.”—The amendment was supported by the Provost, MelT. Flood, Fitzgibbon,' Metge, Brownlow, Osborne, flee.; and opposed by Sir Richard Heron, the Attorney-General, Solicitor-General Carle-ton, Counsellor Browne, Mefl*. Forfter, Dennis Daly, flee.

At twenty minutes past fix on Thursday morning the question was put on the original motion; and the numbers were. Ayes 97, Noes 136.

On the a7th of June a cause upon the game-laws was determined by the high court of justiciary.—John Kelly, game-keeper at Blair, in the county of Air, brought a complaint, in August 1779» before the justices of peace of that county, against John Smith of Swineridgemuir, libelling upon the Statute 13* Geo. III. which enads, “ That every person whatsoever not qualified to kill game in Scotland, who Shall have in his or their custody, or canry, at any time in the year, upon any pretence whatever, any hares,* partridges, pheasants, moor-fowl, tarma-gan, heath-fowl, snipe, or quail, with-, out the leave or order of a person qualified to kill game in Scotland, for carrying such hares or other game, and for having the Same in his or their custody, he Shall, for the first offence, forfeit and pay the sum of twenty Shillings, and for every other subsequent offence the sum of thirty Shillings Sterling [35.449-]?*' and fubfuming, That the Said John Smith bad transgreSTed the said Statute, and was therefore liable in the penalties and forfeitures thereof. Mr Smith appeared before the justices, and contended, That the ad I3# Geo. III. libelled on, bears reference to foroe prior act of parliament fixing the qualification: That the only two acts respecting qualifications were the ad 1611 and the ad z685: That the )a(t of these acts, which establishes toco 1. Scots valued rent as the qualification, cannot now be in force, as there is another requisite in that act to intitle a man to this privilege, viz. a licence from the Matters of the Game, which cannot now be obtained, as that office no longer exists: That the ad 1621, which establishes a ploughgate of land as the Qualification, was therefore the only rule which

could now be observied; and be beiu£ posseflTed of three ploughgates of land,' valued at 1301. Scots, was fully qualified in terms of that ad. Learned informations respecting the game-laws of this country were accordingly laid before the court; upon advising whereof, their Lordfiiips unanimously “ found, That, by the common law of Scotland, all men had right and privilege of the game on their own estates or property: That, by the ad 1621, this right and privilege, or qualification, was confined to persons who had a ploughgate of land, or more, in property: That the ad 1685 ratified and confirmed the general rule laid down in the said act i6ai, but introduced a new regulation respecting the particular mode of hunting with fowling*pieces and set-t i ng dogs ,u nde r an except i on to t hofc pof-feSTed of 10001. Two overture* were taken under consideration May 31: — one from the fy-nod of Fife, via, Wbcrea* it i* expressly enjoined in scripture, that ministers mould give thomf wholly to the bufiocfe of their sacred function, and bo infant in feafote and out of feajon in the discharge of it; as it i* likewise required, by diver* (landing laws of thi* church, that they should avoid engaging in employment* that might distraft them from the duties of their charge; notwithstanding which there are already instances, and, if a timely check be not given to the practice, there may soon be more, of persons holding at the same time the office of professor in an university with that of minister in a parish; by which means they mud not only lose much of that time which ought to be employed in preparing for the pulpit, but may often be so taken up in attending upon their claflc* in the college, a* to prevent them from visiting the sick, and performing the other very necessary, though more private part* of the mini-Aerial work: — Whereas also it hath been always understood to be a rule of this church, that it ought not to be pmnit-ted to clergymen to hold a plurality of benefice*, that is to say, that no minister should be possessed of an office that gives him the care,of fouls excepting in the particular congregation of which he is appointed ovorfetr; yet of late it has become a frequent practice, for ministers to accept pt chaplainries of regiments, and of the garrisons in cables, not litu-ated within the bounds of their own parishes, the duty of which they cannot personally perform without absenting themselves from the flocks of which they have formerly and in the most solemn manner undertaken the charge [26.289.; —38. 34*.40. 39*— 41. *95. 34*j 5 so that they roust be under the necessity of neglecting the * important functions belonging to one or other of their office* of parochial minister* or of chaplains: — it is overtured, That the general assembly should take such method* a* to their wi(dom shall seem meet for properly restraining the former, and for absolutely prohibiting the latter of the above-mentioned practices, and also in general for preventing minister* from engaging in any employments that mav divert them from }bc proper dqtics of their faeftd

function; which as they cannot welt be reconciled to the engagements that minister* have come under at their ordination, may proveof most dangerous consequence to the interest of religion, and to the fouls of men committed to their trust.” Thi* overture the synod unant-moufly agreed to transmit to the assembly.

The other overtore was from the synod of Perth and Stirling, viz. “ Whereas plurality of benefices with cure is contrary to the constitution, a* well as to the (landing laws, of this church, and yet of late many minister* have accepted and hold office* inconh(tent with that attention which at their ordination they become bound to pay to the edification of the people under their charge; and such a* have been nominated chaplain* to regiments, without doing any duty at all, receive that allowance and pay which is designed a* the encouragement of those who, by attending their respective regiments, promote the knowledge of the principles of religion among soldiers, who have scarce any other mean* of instruction in their power 5 therefore the synod humbly overture to the ensuing general assembly, that they would take some effectual measure* for remedying an evil so justly complained of, and preventing the like practices in time coming.”

On report of a committee to whom these overtures were referred, the af* fembly agreed to dismiss them.

The presbytery of Lochmaben'found, that it wa* not competent for Dr John Walker, minister at Moffat, to hold at the same time the kirk of Moffat and the professorship of Natural History in the' university of Edinburgh; but the synod of Dumfries reversed that sentence.

May 30. and the consent of the preffiytery, the assembly remitted to a committee to meet with the parties, with a view to an amicable compromise. — On report of whom, June 2. the assembly remitted to the preffiytery to proceed to the remainder of Mr Lawson’s trials, to finiffi the fame, and pronounce their final judgement thereon between and the first Wednesday of May next; and enjoined the pres-bytery, in case they should fee cause to reject all or any part of the trials, to fpreify the particular discourse or discourses to which they objected, or the defects on which they founded such fen-fence of rejection; and in case they should found the rejection on any part of the queltionary trials, to take down, or cause to be taken down, in their minutes, the questions put to Mr Lawson, and to allow him to put down in writing his answers thereto.

That a new election should take place on the second Tuesday after any vacancy should happen. The design of which was, that time might not be given for fomenting difeord. On an application by the subscribers, the presbytery of Paisley, Aug. 3. 1774, unani-moufly approved of the institution; directing, that the election, when made, should be signified to Mr Turner, (minister of the parish), or his succeslora in office; and that objections, if any were to be made, should be offered without delay to their ecclesiastical superiors, to be by them cognofced, and finally determined. But the prefby tery made alterations afterwards: They resolved, Od. 8. 1777, That proprietors of cha-pets of ease should give information to the minister of the parish, respecting any future vacancy, or intended election, of %a preacher, and invite him, at least ten free days previous to the election, either to preside himself at the election, or fend another minister of the presbytery in his place, in order that he might report the election to the first presbytery, if necessary; —and they resolved, Nov. 24. *779. That before the electors of a preacher in a chapel of ease should proceed to any future election, application should be first made by them to the presbytery, signifying their intention, and requeuing the presbytery to appoint the minister of the parish, or some other minister of the presbytery, to preside and moderate in the election; and that a copy of the whole minutes of procedure in the election, attested by the minister presiding, should be produced to the presbytery at their next meeting, that the presbytery might judge of it as they should fee cause. The proprietors of the chapel applied to the presbytery for red refs; but their petition was refused, March 22.

1780. ed in a judicial and sentences passed in a legislative capacity; that the former, it was allowed, were not to be altered, but to apply this doCtrine to resolutions and sentences passed in a legislative capacity, would be equally uuiuft and absurd; that the presbytery’s approving of an ereClion of a chapel in 1774, under certain conditions, could not divest theni of a power, according to future circumstances, to alter or correct, in 1780, what was done in 1774; and that the presbytery apprehended they were walking upon sure ground when they were only adopting a resolution of the very last assembly [41. 283.]. —The proprietors of the chapel contended, and the synod were of opinion, that the enactments of the presbytery in 1774, con ft i-tuted a solemn contract, which neither party could infringe without the consent

of the other.----- The assembly affirmed

general affcmWj. Vol, 4?;

And whereas you the Barons of our Eschew quer, to whom the Aid memorials were referred, to coufider, what might be reaion-able to be allowed for the time to come, and ia what manner, and how the lame might he paid with most eaic to the said Assembly, are of opinion, (as appears by your report which has also been laid before us). That the sum of five hundred pounds Stetling may he a proper and fuitabie allowance for defraying the aforesaid charges and salaries;, and that, if we (houtd be pleated to add the said sum for that use to the edablUhment of civil affairs in Scotland, to bepaidto any person having power from the Church to receive the same, and in the Hke manner with others oo that eftabHshment, it will be an easy and proper method for paying the said allowance: We, taking the premiffet into our Royal consideration, have thought fit to agree to what is io propoted; and, accordingly, our will and pleasure is, and we do hereby direct, authorise, and command. That you cause the said yearly sum of five hundred pounds Sterling to be paid to such pei Ton or persons, from time to time, as Hull be appointed by the General Assembly to receive the same, for the purposes a fore-fa id, out of any money that is, or shall from time to time be in the hands of the receiver-general of our land-rents and casualties in Scotland, applicable to the uses of our civil government there, during our pleasure, in such manner as other annual sums contained in our edablifliment of civil affairs are and shall be paid and payable. And, of our further grace and favour, we do hereby direct, that the said yearly Aim shall be reckoned to commence from the 24th day of June 1709; and that you the Barons of out Exchequer do insert what shall be due thereupon from that time in the next lid which you shall cause to be made up of what is due to our cfUblishment of Alaries and allowances in Scotland, pursuant to the directioos contained in our letters of privy fell in that behalf; and that the said allowance be for the future edeemed as paie of our Aid eda-blishment. And for so doing this shall be your warrant. Given at our court at St James's, the 19th of May 27 to, in the ninth year of our reign.

The speech of Lord Chief Lough-BOROUGH, to the Grand , convened in tht great hall in St Margaret*t Hill, on Monday, 780.

Taken in Short

Gentlemen of the Grand Jury,

"V F there are any <?f you. Gentlemen, I who came here totally ignorant of 1 the transn&iorts which have lately JL parted in this neighbourhood; if it were possible that you should neither have seen, nor heard of the devaluations that have been committed not long ago, the remnants of the flames that mull have (truck your view as you came to this place, will sufficiently declare to you the reason of your being called together. His Majesty’s paternal care for Vol. XLII.

the welfare of all his subjetfs, wi permit him to suffer offences of such daring enormity to remain without a trial, a longer time than the law requires for. assembling a jury of the county to inquire into them. The commission, Gentlemen, by which you are called, extends only to the crimes of high treason or of felony, committed by those who are, or shall be, within the time preferi-bed by the commission, within the common gaol of this county, charged with such offences.

It was not thought proper to blend the common business of an assize, where an inquiry is instituted into those crimes that the frailty of human nature is but too liable to commit, with crimes of so deep a guilt, and so much above the or-U u dinary

338 Lord Loughborough’s charge to the Grand Jury. Voi. 4a.

dinary pitch of human wickedne&, as those offences which will fall under your confederation. The general circumstances under which these crimes were committed, are of too great and too shameful notoriety; but it will be necessary for you, who are to take into confederation the nature and quality of the alts imputed to the several persons who may be brought before you as offenders, to mark the several parts, in order to observe the connection of these parts with the whole, and to determine upon the nature and quality of the acts imputed, upon a general observation of the whole circumstances of the case, applying the circumstances always to the different parts, that the particular different informations laid before you will assign to the persons accused. I shall think it a part of my duty, to endeavour to lay before you, in one view, a short account of those dancers from which this kingdom has lately been delivered. I make use of that expression; for it will clearly appear, that it wa9 not only the destruction of the property of individuals, or an attack upon the lives and fortunes of particular descriptions of men; but that the evil was not intended to be partial; that the blow which Providence was plea fed to avert, was aimed at the credit of the constitution, and to the very being of the (late.

Gentlemen, the first remarkable circumstance that calls for your observation,'is, a large concourse of people assembled in St George’s fields on the ad of June. They were called together by an advertisement published in the name of a person stated to be the president of an association The advertise-

ment invited the attendance of thousands of people, appointing signals by which they were to be distinguished, and prescribing the order and coqrfe of their anarch in feveralcolumns to the place of their destination. Charity will oblige us to suppose, that of the persons thus assembled, there were many that came there unwarily, unconscious of any evil intended; but it would be credulity in the extreme, to suppose that there were pot some among them, who knew, who foresaw, who intended, and who practiced to accomplish the purposes intended to be effectuated by that meeting. Gentlemen, a Short time has difetofed to you, that the purpose of that assembly VAh \° overawe the legislature, and <?V

tain an alteration of the establiffced laws by force and numbers. There was a petition present^d to repeal an act fn which they had no special interesti TP petition for the repeal of any ad, is undoubtedly the inherent right of every subject; but, under the name and colour of a petition, to assume the command over the legislature, is th» dissolution of all government: and it is fit to remind you* that fatal experience has shown you the danger of tumultuous petitioning, in the outlet of that unhappy contest, which ended in a total dissolution of this government; and among the first acts, after the restoration of legal authority, was a law passed in the 13 th year of the reign of Charles II. which directs, that no petition for the alteration of matters established by law in church or state, not having previously obtained the approbation of the magistrates, or of the grand jury, Shall be signe^ by more than twenty names, or delivered by more than ten person8 [33. 705.] In opposition to thia law, a petition was Signed and delivered by many thousands; and, in defiance of a principle more important and more ancient than any positive law, the desire of this petition was to be carried by the terror of the multitude that attended it through the Streets, classed, marked, and arranged as the advertisement di-reded.

Gentlemen, this was the transadion that passed in this county. That con-courle of people proceeded to Palace-yard, and to the avenues adjoining the two houses of parliament. How the leaders demeaned themselves there, what was the conduct of the multitude to the members of both Houses, it is not my intention to state: not that it may not be proper matter for consideration in other parts of this business; ray reaSon* for avoiding it is, that it is the only purpose of my addressing you, to inform you of fads (b public, that there can be no doubt upon them, and to convey that information to you in such a manner, as to avoid every circumstance that may inflame, where the application goes too directly to particular persons: for this reason, I pate over in Silence all that part of the transadion, which cannot be related but in much stronger language, and in more indignant terms, than I chuse at present to employ. In the close of that day the assembly dispersed from falacc-yard* $A*ny of tb^m un«}oubt-

1,

July 1780. Lord Loughborough'

fedly retired to their frvcral dwellings: Tome, however, there were that remained, in order to shew, that the threats of the force of that multitude, which had Invaded the ears of all that walked the strecta, were not fruitless, blit meant to be put into execution. When night tame on, the houses of two foreign ministers residing at this court, from powers In amity with his Majesty, were attacked, their chapels stript, plundered, and set on (ire. It is not possible upon that occasion, Gentlemen, to avoid this obvious reflection: Had such an outrage been committed upon a public tninifter firom this state, residing in those countries, upon whom the imputation of bigotry may with the utmost justice be fixed; what horner, indignation, and abhorrence, would it have excited, not only In the minds of all our countrymen, that would have frit the honour of the nation hurt by it ! but all other parts of Europe would have thought themselves concerned in the insult. That disgrace was committed in this country, by one of the first aCte done when they proceeded in the manner I have dated 'to you. Upon the next day, the 3d of ‘ June, there was a fremtog quiet: A very memorable circumstance; for sudden tumults, when they are once over, are commonly at an end; but when they can cease and be revived again, it is impossible not to conceive that it argues something of settled influence and previous direction. The day that followed was Sunday. Gentlemen, that day, by the laws of God and roan, is set apart and kept inviolated by the works of ho-fieft industry; that day, however, there was a renewal of the lawless force and violence in broad day-light. Before night had drawn a veil upon their transaCtioos, many buildings and private houses in MooTfieJds were attacked, stript of their goods, the furniture consumed in a bon-' fire in the public space of Moorfidds, and all this done in the view of patient magistrates. Gentlemen, some magistrates there were who had thought it their duty, upon the first breaking out of these tumults, to exert themselves in ‘ vindication of the laws: persons had been apprehended, and committed by the exertion of individuals and diligent magistrates, for the outrages committed on Friday evening. On the next day, Monday June 5. those that had hitherto fed tbcmfeivcf under <6 little restraiat,

« charge to the Grand Jury. 339

so little check, and in no degree refitted* thought it was a necessary part to accomplish, in the project they had made* to shew, that the law could not be exercised against any that had been in the like delinquency with them, with impunity^ It was the business of Monday to destroy, to pull down, and set fire to the houses of the magistrates, and those officers of the peace, by whose means persons had been apprehended.

Thus far matters had proceeded to no common degree of excess; — but what E have hitherto related to you is far ftiort of what afterwards followed, upon those two days, the 6th and yth of June, which will ever remain a stain upon the annals of these times. Frefti insults were again offered to the members of the le-giflaturc; and towards the latter part of the first day (Tuesday) all who were then in London will recollcCt, that the town had the appearance of a city taken by storm: every quarter of it was threatened and alarmed; neither sex nor age, nor fanCtity of character, nor eminence of situation, nor even honest but humble obfeurity, was sufficient to protect men from the rage, fury, and malevolence, of those who were spreading fire and terror in every quarter of the town. But, Gentlemen, the attempts were not confined to the destruction of indivi* duals: for, in a very short space of time, one of the first general objects appeared to be that which experience and history has ihewn to be among the hit efforts of the most desperate and abandoned conspirators, to break the gaols and set loose the felons, and to engage the guilty hands of desperate men, whose lives were forfeited to the law, in the accession of the crimes that they were about to perpetrate :— this, Gentlemen, was committed with success. After that, the town was set on fire, and the flames were applied to those buildings, in different quarters, that were most likely to spread the conflagration to distant places; namely, the houses of distillers, and those that from their trade contained the greatest quantity of combustible matter. That was not all, but, in the midst of horror and confusion, there was an attempt to prevent the extinction of the flames, by cutting off the water of the New River, and an attempt to strike at the foundation of all the credit of the country, the Bank of England. That was, U is true, defeated, providen-Vu atially

340 Lord Loughborough’s charge to the Grand Jury. Vol. 4a.

,tially defeated ! but under circumstances which left no doubt of their indention to carry it into effect, if it had not pleased God that the execution had been poltponed to that period of the bu-finefs.

Gentlemen, during the very short space df these two days, the incredible activity of that band of furies that walked through the town with flaming torches, had destroyed above seventy private bou-fes, and four public jails; one of which seemed at lead to have been built to withstand the efforts of an armed force. Such is the detail of the alts which were done. — I am not to omit, that while these outrages were committed, the name of religion, and the pure A and most peaceable description of religion, the Protestant religion, was made the profane pretext for feme of these violences which were committed; — the pretext only, — for I am sure there is not in Europe a man so weak, neither will there (bc found a pei son so uncandid, or so unjust to the character of the Reformed church, as to suppose, that any pofltyle motive, even of fall'e zeal, by any pbf-fible perverlion of human reason, could induce men to lay in allies the capital of the Proteltant faith, or that any pretence of any religion could at ail have influenced the minds of those whose object was to destroy all private security, by setting loose all those who had been guilty of crimes against the peace of the country; and all public credit, by the destruction of the Bank of England.

I have now. Gentlemen, ftaled to you, in one general view, the circumftauces by which this calamity began, continued, and, happily, by the exertion of the efforts, to which we are ever indebted, of a gracious sovereign, was at least fufjnrnded for a time. It now remains, that 1 should state to you, what particular parts of this trail lad ion will more immediately call for your consideration. And from what I have said, it mult be evident to you, that there may be persons whose cases wnl be brought under your view that are tainted with the guilt of high treason: for that the object was general, that the design was not of a partial or particular n tuie, that the mischief was not specifically aimed at the individuals who suffered by it, but had a deeper and more extensive operation, it would be credulity to a degree to entertain a doubt. It is necessary, therefore, Gentlemen, that I should state to you, very shortly.

what the htw is with respect to the species of treason, under which it is possible some of those cases which are con-neded with this strange and uoparalleUed outrage, may come.

There are two species of treason, which may possibly have particular cases. If a man doth compass and imagine the death of our Lord the King, that is high trea-fem: if a man doth levy war against the King within the realm, that is high treason. The first species of high treason, that of compassing and imagining the death of the King, is to be demonstrated by some overt ad, as the means to cf-feduate the purposes of the heart: the levying war, or conspiring to levy war, may be an overt ad of that treason; but levying war against the King, is also a distinct species of treason; and as, from the general circumstances of this case, it is very possible, that that may be the species of treason most applicable to the cases which may be brought before you, it is necessary that I should state to you more fully, and more distinctly, what the law has said, with respect to cales that fall under that denomination of crime. And, Gentlemen, I am particularly happy, to be able to state it to you, not from collections, inferences, or dedudions of my own, which i am sensible may be liable to error, and in which the change or inaccuracy of expression might lead to very dangerous mistakes; but 1 am enabled to state it to you from such authority, as that the law itself speaks to you, and that my mouth only pronounces the words of the law; for i shall state it to you in the very words of that great, learned, and able judge, Mr Justice Fofter, that true friend to the liberties of his country.

Gentlemen, these are the words of Mr Justice Eofter. — Every iufurredion which in the judgement of the law is intended against the person of the KiRg, be it to dethrone or iuiprison him, or to oblige him to alter his measures of government, or to remove evil counsellors froma-bout him, these risings all amount to levying war within the statute, whether attended with the pomp and circumstances of open war or not; and every conspiracy to levy war for these purposes, though not treason within the clause of levying war, is yet an overt ad within the other clause, of compassing the King’s death; for these purposes cannot be effected by numbers, and open force, without maoifeft danger

gaol, and setting the prisoners loofo r there are, betides, fbme who are committed for different robberies, by extorting money or goods, under the terror of the mob, from different persons who were remaining in their dwellings, or in the Greets. These are the several cases of felony that will probably be fub-nutted to your consideration; and I am fenGble, Gentlemen, that I need say little to you upon the fnbjeft of these felonies. There are many of you, who pro* fessionally are conversant with the genei ral principles of law, and all of the gentlemen that I hare the honour to foe, are, by their rank and Gtuation in life, undoubtedly instrufted in the general principles of that law, which is commonly difoenfed in the course of the execution ohcriminal justice throughout the kingdom.

1 will just mention to you, that as to these offences, the burning any house, or outhoufo being parcel of a dwelling-house, though not contiguous or under one roof, is at common law a felony, and the benefit of clergy is by law taken from this offence; therefore it is a capital offonce to fot fire to any house, barn, or outhoufo, though the said house, ike. should not be burnt down: and it is not necessary it should be by force, or persons armed or difguifod; for merely to set fire to any house, barn, or outhoufo, is a capital felony by the act 90 Geo. I. ch. 2». To begin to pull down any dwelling-house, or outhoufo, by persons riotoufiy assembled, to the number of twelve or more, is made a capital felony by an act i° Geo. I. ch. 5. which is commonly known by the name of the riot-aft. Having mentioned that act, it is necessary I should just say a few words upon it. I have mentioned those cases that were decided in a period very near to that in which this act passed; the two cases of the persons convicted of high treason, for assembling to pull down Protestant Dissenting meeting - houses. The fame pernicious and dangerous spirit, that by the worst parts of faction had been instilled into the lowest of the people towards the clofc of the reign of Q. Anne, by great artifices, had been Ml kept alive in the minds of difeontented persons: and if we did not foe the strangest perversions of reason, and the strangest inconsistence prevail upon the minds of men, beyond the guidance of reason, it would remain matter of the

utmost astonishment; but It is no lefif true than aftoniihing, what was mifcail-ed a Protestant mob in the beginning of the reign of George 1. was a mob called forth, arranged, excited, and employed, against the Protestant succession, at the mug-houses, and every lurking comer and alley of sedition, raised by the enemies of this constitution, and the Protestant succession; and persons, under the false cry of religion, were gathering mobs to attack the government, and to resist that happy and fortunate establishment, the confirmation of'our rights and liberties, which were held sacred at the Resolution. To prevent that, an act wls framed, and formed, upon an anat logy, in some respects, with the words of former statutes: the effect of it was* to give a farther protection to the civil authority of the state? by making the beginning of mischief dangerous to the perpetrators of it, and subject those whose crimes, by their enormity, brought them under the particular cognisance of the law, to a punishment that the bold-nefa and guilt of their designs well merited; for that purpose, though the mischief was not perpetrated, though the house wa9 not pulled down, the beginning to pull down, even not under the circumstance of a large force, but with a collection of persons riotoufiy assembled, to the number of twelve or more, is justly made a capital felony. There are other parts in this act, in which pef-fons riotoufiy assembled arc, by the rc-quisition of a magistrate, to depart; and if they will not depart, the space of an hour having elapsed, the statute declares them guilty of a capital felony, that con-fills only in continuing together riotoufiy assembled after the space of an hour,’ when required to depart. That was tht provision of the act, in order the better to support the civil authority; but it was not the intention of the legislature, nor is it the effect of the act, during the space of an hour, from the time of reading the proclamation, to disarm the civil authority, or oblige the King’s subjects to de-lift from quelling a riot inltantly, or to oblige them to remain patient and tame spectators of a number of people assembled together in breach of the law and pence. The length of time adds to the offence; but that the etrcumftance «f their continuing together the space of an hour creates the offence, is a fatal mistake i and 1 thought k neoeflary to take

July 1 78o. Lord Loughborough'*

this public occasion of stating, that the Tupping every thing may be done, and nothing be unlawful, till an hour is clap fed, and that therefore the civil authority to all his Majesty’s subjects is suspendedduring.an hour, is a mistake; for the statute means no such thing; it gives an additional force to the power of the magistrate, but it leaves them in possession of all the power which the civil authority po fie fled before. This part, however, of the statute will never come under your consideration.

I don’t conceive it impoflSble, from the excessive and immediate violence of this riot, what fame has said, that the proclamation had never been read; but you are to consider that part of it, “ the beginning to pull down, or destroy, any house, barn, or outhouse, by any number of persona assembled, to the number of twelve or more, constitutes a capital offence.”

Gentlemen, in all these cases I have stated to you, the burning of houses, the setting fire to them, the pulling down or beginning to pull down, the being present, aiding, or encouraging the immediate aCtors, though no act is proved to be dooe by the party himself, is equally criminal, and subjects him in the fame degree to the punishment of the law. It has been the constant and the establiftied opinion of the judges upon the construe-tion of these statutes, and solemnly delivered upon a late occasion, and not that 1 know of ever disputed, that “ the being present, aiding, abetting, encouraging of,” though no act is proved to be done by the party, only his aid, assistance, encouragement, and presence, constitutes him in such a degree of guilt, as amounts to the crime of a capital felony, provided against by the statute.

Gentlemen, the taking, against the will of the owner, under terror of the mob, goods, or money, is undoubtedly a robbery; I have therefore thus gone through these several matters, which the kalendar makes it probable may lie prefaced to your view of all thele offences. You, Gentlemen, are to ioquire for this county, and make true presentation; and* when I consider the character of the gentlemen I have the honour to address, how highly they are valued, and juf*1' esteemed by their country, all ad-ir. v it ion on my part will be totally fu-HetHjou3. In you, Gentlemen, the $Ujbli<j has long placed, and, I am ccr-

eharge to the Grand Jury. 343

tain, upon this occasion, will have no reason to regret that it has placed, the utmost consequence that it can beftovr upon any part of its fellow-creatures. Ydur part, however, is only, upon the evidence presented to you upon the part of the accufcr, to determine, whether the party accused stands so probably accused, that you ought to fend him to takfe his trial by another jury of his country, whose province it is, upon the hearing of the evidence upon both ftdes, to pronounce that he is guilty, or that he is mot guilty. You find only, that there is ground to put him upon his trial: in the course of that trial, if there Oiould be any advantage to be derived from the nicety and caution of the law, if there are any circumstances of alleviation of the guilt of the party, it will be as much the inclination, upon this occasion, as it is the duty, of all, and the constant practice of the learned judges who are commissioned to try upon this occasion, to point out all they can in justice for the prisoner, and to lay the proper weight upon all the circumstances that can, in fairness, alleviate the offence; and, after all, if the sentence of the.law should pronounce upon the guilt of the prifort-er, he may still have recourse to that fountain of mercy, the Royal Bread, where justice is always tempered with clemency.

These, Gentlemen, are the advantages, this the protection, that the government and the laws afford to all; — unr-distinguishing, indifferent, and equal to the just and unjust.

A government founded upon the laws will hold out that protection, and give the full benefit of those laws, to such as are accused of an attempt to subvert that government, and to deprive their fellow-subjects, and their posterity, of the benefit of that constitution for ever.

The excellency of the law, the blessing of that constitution, and the admirable form of this government, cannot be better proved, than by administering, upon an occasion which so directly calls up, in the minds of all men. sentiments of love, veneration, and affection, for that government, by administring to all men indifferently, and equally, the benefit of those laws: they are for the protection of peaceable subjects; they are a safe-guard of their rights; they may have recourse to them; they may claim the benefit of tha.t fan&uarjjr they will have

A Letter from a Gentleman in London to

his Friend Abroad,

Dear Sir,

VOU will have been alarmed at what has lately happened at London, and our enemies will have fed themselves with cruel hopes. Their wishes have not succeeded; God’s providence prevented the far greatest part of the evil intended from being executed. The mistake, that felony could not be suppreSTed but by certain forms, encouraged the ruffians to go great lengths, and prevented at first the necessary exertions being made against them. Government, after the first twenty-four hours of wide-spread violence, acted without attention to that most ab* surd prejudice, and by a few hours spirited exertion the whole was quelled. The extreme humanity of the military, even then, was the occasion that not a tenth part of those perished who most richly deserved it. No government but an English one would, in those hours, have moderated the massacre of heaps of ruffians, thieves, incendiaries, traitors, whose outcries threatened a far wider devastation than they had time to prac-tife. The execrable intentions of the enemy to overthrow us at home, by using the madness of fanatics, assembled in thousands, under the vilest of all hypo-' critical pretentions and lies, seemed to succeed on Tuesday June 6.; because, forfootb, it was ignorantly fancied, that a magistrate was necessary to give orders to fire upon ruffians who were in actual felony; whereas a magistrate is only ne-ceSTary to difperfc unlawful assemblies, before felony is actually begun: but

when it is begun, all men are authorised to proceed to every extremity to put an end to felony. This was not so understood till now; and we ask one another, How is it possible that we could have been so blind, as not to use common sense, or to suppose that common sense was not law I BleSTed be God, common sense broke in upon us; the king and his ministers obeyed it; and the evil Tank at once. The troops came in from divers parts in sufficient numbers to cruSh all before'them j and now* all men here

having learnt that they need not wait for a magistrate in the like cases, Steps are taken against all such events in future, by the inhabitants being prepared and preparing to punish such glaring a Ctsof violence. Government, whose Strength is that of the whole nation, is not to be overturned at home. Our enemies, who cannot blush, may howl. They had fondly hoped, from our seditious papers printed every day, that no Sinews were left among us; otherwise. Said they, such treasonable papers would not every day be printed with impunity. How long government may judge it proper to endure such domestic insults, is not now my inquiry1; I would only tell you, that, notwithstanding all this licentiouf-nefs, government is on its legs; and whoever looks upon us as ripe for destruction, will be baffled and punished. We are Stronger than ever, and the more so on account of these late disburbances. The danger to the whole, from the effects of popular tumults, has scouted the measure. The unmuzzling a mad bear is found too inconvenient, as this late bear did more than its instigators at first seemed to intend. Such is the nature of the bear. We Shall grow up into more sociality; we Shall value order more than anarchy; we Shall look upon it as madness to care only each for himself; we Shall be less French and less American, and less dxmoniacs than ever. Which of our domestic or foreign enemies ex-ected this ? The few prisoners taken ave now been tried by the valuable, mild, and fair forms of law: and it does our country honour, that herein nothing has been hurried, no insult made use of towards these criminals, but the judges and the juries have behaved in a manner unknown in other places. While all the French party in Europe arc decrying us and our government as arbitrary and tyrannical, and wish America delivered from us, as if we were tyrants, is any patience, mildness, or decency, equal to ours, in the American tribunals ? But I mult finish. God has confounded our enemies in this matter, and perhaps we Shall do well in our foreign troubles too. Your’s, A. B.

P. S. The King’s behaviour has covered him with honour; and the military have Shewn, that in desperate cases they do not go too far, and have not htep guilty of a tingle disorder.

His force was great, and in good condition, confiding of twelve ships of the line, and three frigates of superior size. Among the former were several ships of great force, and weight of metal; one carrying 90, another So, and fix carrying 74 guns each; and the squadron was said to have no left than 11,000 men on board. On the other fide, the British fleet under Lord Howe, confided of fix sixty-four gun ships, three of fifty, and two of forty guns, with some frigates and (loops. Mod of the former had been long on service, were accordingly in bad condition, and were also wretchedly manned. If any thing, however, could remedy such essential defelts, it might have been hoped for, from the superior abilities of their commander, and the excellency of his officers.
