Maj.-Gen. Sir Robert Pigot, who commanded the British forces, took every measure in the power of a brave and experienced officer, that could tend to a vigorous and most obstinate defence. The troops, artillery, and cattle, were immediately conveyed from the island of Conanicut; the troops at the out posts in Rhode island were in constant readiness, at the first signal, to join the main body; the works to the sea wereftrength-eoed by every poiHble means; and the seamen belonging to the vessels that were destroyed, as well as those that could be spared from others, were called to their favourite occupation of serving the artillery. The inlet to the harbour from the sea, called the Middle Channel, is narrow, and inclosed by Brenton’s point and the opposite point'of Conanicut, which form the southern extremities of both islands. A bar of high grounds, which erodes the isthmus from channel to channel above Newport, was strongly covered with lines, redoubts, and artillery; so that the peninsula might be considered as a garrison, distinct from the rest of the island; and, under the protection of a superior naval force, might, in a great measure, defy any attempts from the northern fide, supposing that an enemy bad made good it9 landing in such circumstances. But the enemy being masters by sea, rendered the talk of defence, under the apprehen-fion of an attack on both fides at the same time, exceedingly arduous. The commander had, however, just before, received a reinforcement of five battalions; the troops were in excellent condition and spirit; and the body of seamen, both with respect to labour and danger, were no small addition to their means of resistance.

As soon as the determination of the enemy to enter the harbour became apparent, the commanders found themselves under the grievous necessity of burning the Orpheus, Lark, Juno, and Cerberus frigates; as they were soon after of linking the Flora and Falcon.

As soon as Lord Howe received advice of the danger of Rhode island, he determined to attempt every thing which resolution, under the direction of reason and judgement, could undertake for its preservation. His squadron, notwithstanding the late reinforcements, was still, with respect to effective force, and weight of metal, so far inferior to the enemy, that to hazard an engagement, without some collateral advantage to counteract so great a superiority, would seem a degree of raOinefs inconsistent » ith his character. In point of number* he was indeed superior to the French, his squadron now confiding of one 74* seven 64, and five 50 gun ships, besides several frigates: but the great deficiency in other respects appears from the bare recital of the rates. Every thing in such a situation was, however, to be tried, and he was determined that nothing should be left undone. The account indeed he received of the separated state Of the French fleet, some of them involved in the channels, and the bulk lying without, afforded some room for a hope, that he might Bring on an engagement upon more equal terms than could have been otherwise expected.

But notwithstanding the utmost possible expedition, be met with such unavoidable delays, that he was not able to reach Rhode island until the day after the French fleet had entered that harbour [Aug. 9.]. From the situation in which the enemy now lay, he was enabled to communicate directly with Gen. Pigot; the result of which was, that, under the present circumstances, the affording him any efiential relief was impracticable.

A sudden change of wind to the north-east afforded an equal change of circumstances; and on the following day , the French Admiral stood out to sea with the whole fleet, those in the Narraganlet paffhge, as well as the port. A strong gale of wind, which afterwards increased to a violent teropeft, and continued for near forty-eight hours, not only put by the engagement by separating the fleets, for the present, but scattered them in such a manner, and caused so much damage on both fides, as rendered an engagement for some time impracticable.

The French suffered greatly in this tempest, two of their capital ships being dismasted, and others much damaged. Some untoward situations, and unusual circumstances, were produced by this conflict of the elements. The Languedoc of 90 guns, D’Eftaing’s own ship, had loft all her malts, and was met Iq that condition, on the evening of the 13th, by the Renown of 50 guns, Capt. Dawson, who attacked her with such fury, as well as judgement and advantage, that no doubt could have been entertained of the event, if the day-light had continued. But the darkness of the night, and frefliness of the gale, whose violence was not yet quite allayed, compelled Capt. Dawson to cease from his attack, after he had poured several broadsides close into her, and had, besides other apparent damage, (hot away her rudder. He, however, lay to, as closely as possible, for the night, intending to renew the attack in the morning, and considering her as little less than a certain prize. The appearance of fix French men of war, by whom he was chafed at day-light, and who were poifi-bly led that way by the firing, put an end to Dawson’s hopes, and relieved the French Admiral from this very urgent distress.

Although the Britdh squadron suffered much less in the dorm than the French, yet their damage was so considerable, as unavoidably to cod some time at Sandy* hook or New York, in proportion to their wants, whether ohly to refit, or to repair. Lord Howe, having got his ships in condition with an expedition that fur-prised every body, pursued them with the greated eagerness, hoping to over* take them by the way.

In the mean time, Gen. Sullivan had landed on the north end of Long island, by the way of Howland's ferry, on the 9th of Augud, being the day that D’E-ltaing went out of the harbour to meet Lord Howe. The extreme badness of the weather impeded for some days the bringing forward of his dores and artillery, and of course retarded the progress of his army. On the 17th, however, they broke ground on Honeyman's hill, near the British works, and began to construct batteries, ar.d to form lines of approach; the British forces being no less active in throwing up new works, and constructing new batteries, to counteracd theirs. We have already observed, that Gen. Pigot was under no great apprehension of an attack in front; the general object of apprehension was the concurrent assault of D’Edaing on thfc town and works to the water; but the great point of danger was his landing a body of troops in the fouthem penin-fiila, which would have laid the garrison open in the rear, whild they were de-fpcrately engaged on the front and flank in defence of their works.

The critical and most timely appearance of Lord Howe with the British squadron, happily obviated this apprehension and danger in the fird indance; and D’Eftaing’s consequent departure, or flight, to Bodon, removed them entirely. His failing out of the harbour to engage Lord Howe, does not seem by any

mcaus

3jo History 1778: America. Vol. 42#

means to have been a judicious measure. The nature of the port, the narrowness of the passage from the sea, with the means of defence afforded by the island of Conanicut, which was occupied by Imnfelf and his allies, held out, all together, so strong a security to his fleet, that scarcely any naval superiority, which, however, did not exilt, could havejuftifled any attempt upon it. In this (late, it would seem, that he should first have secured his objelt, which ap-ars to have been much within his reach, fore he put out to sea, either to engage, or to seek for Lord Howe. But vanity seems here to have had some fbarc in his determination. The glory of vanquishing a British squadron, and of obtaining a triumph over a commander of great name, and of a country which so seldom afforded such laurels, was a temptation not to be refitted by D’E-ftaing.

Yet, after all the ill consequences of this vain and ruinous pursuit, if he had entered the harbour, and co-operated with the Americans, in conformity with their most earnest solicitations, when he anchored the second time before Rhode island, it would seem that the state of the garrison would have been extremely perilous, and that he had a fair prospect of retrieving, by a stroke of no small importance, the failure of success in his grand object. Such a successtul co-operation would like wife have had a wonderful effect in conciliating the minds of his new allies, and in giving them an idea, which they were not very apt to entertain, of the vigour and efficacy of French counsels and arms. It may indeed be objected, and truly, that his two dif-xnatted ships could not have been repaired, nor, perhaps, t-he rest of his squadron refitted, at Rhode island: but as they might have continued there in perfect security for any length of time, if he had succeeded in his object, this objection does not appear to be of sufficient weight for its being abandoned.

1'he American army in'Rhode island, and the people of the northern colonies in general, complained loudly of this conduit. They laid, that they had been Jed into an expedition of prodigious expence, labour, trouble, and danger, under the assurance of the most effective co-operation of the French fleet; that, under this fanltion, they had committed their lives and liberties on the invasion of au island, where, without a naval pro

tc&ion, they were likely to be inclosed like wild beasts in a toil; and that in this situation they were first deserted, for a vain and fruitless pursuit, and then totally abandoned at the very time that they had brought the business on their fide to the point of completion.

Under these difeontents and apprehen-fions, Sullivan was deserted by the New-England and Connecticut volunteers, who composed the better half of his army; and by this means, if we credit the { American accounts, his numbers were so much reduced, as to be inferior, in point of force, to the garrison. In these circumstances, and under the immediate apprehension of his retreat being cut off, Sullivan extricated himself with a degree of prudence and ability, which would have done donour to an older general; nor would the behaviour of his troops have disgraced more veteran soldier9.

Having begun to fend off his heavy artillery and baggage on the 26th of August, he retreated from his lines on the 29th: and though he was most vigorouf-ly pursued, and repeatedly attacked in every quarter where-everan opening was made by the British forces; yet he took his measures so well, and bad chosen his polls so judiciously, that although much honour was claimed anddeferved on both fides, he gained the north end of the island without sustaining any considerable loss. Being the re, from the nature of the ground, and the' situation of his posts, in a state of security, he passed his army over by the way of Bristol and Hoyland ferries, on the night of the 30th, without interruption, to the continent. Nor was his good fortune inferior to his conduit, as Sir Henry Clinton arrived just j after with such a force from New York, as would have left no doubt of I the fate of his forces if they had ltill continued on the island.

On the fame day that SullivAn abandoned Rhode island, Lord Howe entered the bay of Boston, where, to his great mortification, he found that D’Eftaing was arrived before him. This was, however, increased, when upon a close inspection he discovered that he was so eft'eltually covered in Nantalket road, by the batteries erelted, and the measures of defence taken by the Americans and French, on the adjacent points and i-Hands, that an attack upon him, with any profpelt of success, w*s utterly im« pralticable.

Thus, with great honour to himself, and advantage to his country, did that ££reat naval commander bring the campaign with his powerful adversary to a coitclufion. With an inferiority of force, "which held out mere preservation as the iummit of hope, he, by a continued and rapid succession of the greatest possible exertions, masterly manoeuvres, and wife raeafurea, having ftrlt counteracted, and at leugth defeated all the views and attempts of his enemy, obliged him to fly for refuge to those new allies whom he came to protect, and insulted him under that protection, leaving him in a condition at parting, which rendered him incapable of any further service in those fcas for the remainder of the year.

An extraordinary cure of a Dropsy,

A Few years ago there was at Konigf-berg a poor woman, between fifty and sixty years of age, who was afflicted with the dropsy to the highest degree, and being totally unfit for any kind of work, was obliged to beg for her bread. In this condition (he for a considerable time daily attended the exchange, where ihe received a small pittance from the merchants; to whom (he was by this means well known, and consequently her surprising cure was conspicuous to all. Her distemper seemed daily to increase; and at lait, on her disappearing from her usual station upon the Exchange, no body doubted but ihe was dead. However, in a few weeks afterwards, some of her old benefactors were fnrprifed at seeing her busily employed fwceping the steps before a gentleman’s door, and seemingly in perfcCt health. On their enquiring of her by what means flic had got the better of such a dreadful disease, (he related, that, by the advice of another woman, (he had confined herself to a diet of broad beans (called in England Wind]or beans), boiled in water,

without lait, butter, or fat of any kind. The beans were her only food, and the water they were boiled in (which was only suficient to cover them) her only drink, for eighteen days; at the end of which time ihe began to make water in great quantity; which continued with (hurt intervals for several days; and l>y degrees (lie found herself perfectly well, without any weakness or other ill fyrnp-toms remaining. This day the following royal proclamation was iliued, dated June 5. and was published on that or next day, viz. “ George R.

Whereas it hath been represented to us, that a great number of persons, in consequence of advertisements or hand-bills inciting them thereto, did, on Friday last, the sd of this instant June, assemble in a riotous and tumultuous manner in St George’s Fields, in the county of Surry, from whence they proceeded in several bodies to Palace-yard, Westminster, surrounded both Houses of Parliament, and possessed themselves of the avenues to the fame, (the said Houses of Parliament being then fitting), where they continued in a riotous and tumultuous manner, committing great outrages and violence against several of our subjecte, and feloniously taking money from them; after which, in the evening of the fame day, they insulted and attacked the houses of some of the public ministers of foreign princes and dates, redding at our court, and broke into, spoiled, and set fire to chapels, which in every civilized country are ailowed to such public ministers for the.free exercise of the religion of their country, to the great scandal and offence of all truly pious and good Protestants: We therefore, in order that the reproach of so frantic and atrocious an outrage against the precepts of religion, as well as the obligations of all laws, may not remain a dilgracc to our kingdom, but may be confined to the authors, abettors, and perpetrators of the said outrage, and that they may be brought to speedy and condign pu-nishment, and that such dangerous and wicked practices may hereafter be prevented, have thought fit, by and with the advice of our Privy Council, to publish and declare our resolution to put the laws in force against such offences: and we do hereby enjoin and require all ju* Rices of the peace, sheriffs, mayors, bailiffs, constables, and ail other our loving subjects, to be aiding and assisting to the utmost of their power in suppressing such illegal and tumultuous assemblies, and in detecting, apprehending, and bringing to justice the persons concerned in assembling or inffigating Mie fame, and guilty of any of the violences above mentioned: and for the better deletion of the persons who were concerned in the outrages committed at the heufes of the Voi.XLII.

said public ministers, we arc gracloufly pleased to promise, that if any perfoti shall discover any other per Am or persons who, directly or indirectly, were concerned in pulling down, or beginning to pull down, or in setting fire to the chapel of any public minister, so as that the person or persons discovered may be prosecuted for the fame, such discoverer shall have and receive, as a reward, upon convi<5tion of such offender or offenders, the sum of five hundred and

also our gracious pardon for the said offence, in <^fe Person making such discovery thall himself be liable to be prosecuted for the fame, except the person who first broke into, or began to pull down or set fire to the said chapels.”

During the four days of thelc tumults of which w'e have given an account, the Lords, Commons, Magistrates, and persons in power, of every denomination, in the great and populous cities of London and Westminster, ieemed to be pa-nic-struck. What followed, involved in it the safety of all that was dear and valuable to men, and threatened not only the lives and properties of Catholics, but of all ranks from the king to the loxveft mechanic. Government at length began to fed the alarm, but net time enough to prevent one of the most

horrible seenes of desolation that was c-ver exhibited in a peaceable country.

^ Approach we now to the awful period, when the sovereignty of the king, and the property of the subject, retted solely on laws unsupported, and roa-gistrates confeffcdly intimidated; when the very existence of the date, its trea-furea, its splendour, its civil and military strength, depended on the whim that an unbridled multitude should take in the direction of the mischief they were about to perpetrate, and the destruction and devattation they had already begun to carry into execution.

On Tuesday, June 6. the day appointed to take the Protestant petition into farther consideration [185.], the multitude assembled about the parliament-house was no less numerous than that which surrounded it on the preceding Friday. They did not, as before, regularly assemble in St George’s Fields, but came in small parties from different places. At first they seemed orderly, but apparently resolute. In the course of the afternoon more parties arrived, and they began, by degrees, tobecometumultuous.

In the House of Lords, this day, on reading a private bill, the Earl of Radnor took notice of the impropriety of proceeding to business when their doors were invested with a military force. There was no precedent on the Journals, of any act which had pnffed when their Lordfnips were surrounded with the military, and were seemingly prevented from exercising that freedom and independence, which gave dignity to their deliberations, and effect to their deci-fions. He did not think that an act passed under such circumstances would either have the weight or the nature of a law; and he hoped their Lordships would rather chuse to postpone any business that might Hand for that day, than have a precedent on their Journals, of business being transacted under the influence of an armed force.

Lord Ravensworth lamented that the House should (till be in a fit u at ion so truly mortifying. The avenues were blocked up by the fame multitude that had assaulted it on Friday lass, and their Lordships were Hill in personal hazard in coming to their feats. He said, it was absolutely necessary that their Lordships, in conjunction with the other House, should infiantly take the petition of the people under confederation, and quell

the riots and the distempers of the people at once.

Earl Bathurst said, that their Lordships had, on Saturday, done all that they thought proper and necessary on the occasion; and unless they should think proper to have a conference with the other House, be did not know what further they could do. There were fomc proportions to be made this day in the other house, and be did not doubt that their Lordships would readily wait I to fee if the Commons would wish to have their concurrence. 1

Earl Mansfield said,that in all their Lord- 1

ships deliberations they would wifely be- ! gin with considering the end; they should > look forward to the result of every mca-sure they thought proper to undertake.—

A conference with the other House in the present instance would not contribute to any salutary purpose. The other House were now in consideration of the matter, and this interference would only interrupt and delay their proceedings: they bad done all that they thought proper and necessary in the matter upon Saturday last, and his Majesty’s ministers had taken every precaution against the attack which was made against the freedom and independence of parliament.

The Earl of Denbigh said, that the avenues to the House were nowfo blocked up, that many Peers were prevented from coming to their feats: he had been flopped several times in coming down to attend his duty; and Lord Sandwich had been flopped, attacked, and put in the utmost hazard of his life. He could af-sure their Lordships, that so long as the present tumults continued, he should 1 think it dangerous to come down to the House; and nnlefs some measures, vi- I gorous and effectual, were immediately taken, he did not fee how they could proceed in their business: they might adjourn from day to day, but they would be unable to proceed, if the riots were permitted to continue.

The Earl of Hillsborough begged the Noble Lord to say what further could be done. — His Majesty’s ministers had , taken every possible precaution; they had employed their whole force; but they would thank the Noble Lord to fug-gest any other more effectual step. I

Earl Bathurst said, that every power which the constitution had veiled in his Majesty’s ministers, had been employed, and would continue to be so.

Mr Burke made a very animated speech upon the alarming and dangerous pro* ceedings of the populace. He was extremely severe against those who were capable of misleading the people to such violent outrages against the laws and constitution of their country, as well a9 against reason, justice, and humanity; and he dealt his censure with vehemence against government for that relaxed state of the police which could no longer protect even legislature itself from violence and insult at their very gates. In short, he felt so much for the debased dignity of parliament at that moment that he loft all temper, and bitterly lamented the fate of such times, when those who pretended to be the advocates of freedom, were establishing the most wretched slavery; and exhibited the unhappy prospect which was then at their gates, — bludgeoned mob, and an armed / He

lamented in the most melancholy terms, the dreadful necessity that^ obliged the military power, the notorious bane of liberty, to be called in, to defend not only the freedom, but the very existence of parliament!

Mr Fox also lamented the necessity of calling in the assistance of the military, which be attributed solely to the weak administration of public affairs. During the fitting of the House an attack had been made on the residence of Lord North in Downing street; but a party of light horsemet with and repnlfed the assailants. On the riling of the House, Lord George repaired to the corner of Bridge street, informed the populace what had been done, and advised them to depart quietly. In return, they un-harnessed his horses* 2nd drew him in triumph, together with Sir Philip Jennings Clerke, who had applied to his Lordship for protetfion, to the house of Mr Alderman Bull. While a party of the rioters were thus employed, Justice Hyde, with a party of the guards, endeavoured to disperse the rest. At first they pressed hard upon the guards; but the guards, in their turn, soon advanced upon them, and rode furiously among them. They did not again attempt to face the military.

In the evening, about seven o’clock, the rioters resenting the activity of Ju* ftice Hyde, a detached party attacked his house in Lille street, stript it of the furniture, and burnt it before his door. A party of the guards arrived too late; the mischief was done, and the incendiaries fled. Newgate was now their next concern, and to relcafe their confined associates the objett they had then in view. Like regular assailants, they did not proceed to storm before they had offered terms: They called upon Mr A-kerman the keeper to release their comrades, as the only means to save his man-fion. This he peremptorily refused to do; but, dreading what would happen, he polled to the sheriffs to know their pleasure. In cases of emergency delays are dangerous: While the magistrates were deliberating, the gaol was set on fire, and on his return Mr Akerman found his house in flames. A party of constables, nearly to the number of 100, c.me to his assistance: these the rioters fuft'ered to pass till they were entirely encircled, and then they were attacked with great fury, their staves broken, and converted into brands, which were hurled about where*ever the fire appeared but faintly kindled. It is scarce to be credited with what celerity a gaol, which to a common observer appeared to be built with nothing that would

burn, was destroyed by the flames: nor is it Icfs astonishing, that from a prison thus in flames a miserable crew of felons in irons, and a company- of confined debtors, to the number, in the whole, of more than 300, could all be liberated, as it were by magic, amid ft flames and firebands, without the loss of a Angle life, some from the gloomy cells of dark-pefs in which the devoted victims to public justice were confined, and others from inner apartments, to which the ac-ccfs in tranquil times was both intricate and difficult. But what happened afterwards is not at all to be wondered at; that by a body of execrable villains thus let loose upon the public, the house of that worthy and active magistrate, Sir John Fielding, should be the first marked for vengeance. Accordingly, the i-rons with which they had been fettered could scarce have been unloosed before gfhe public office in Bow street, and Sir John Fielding’s dwelling-house adjoining, were gutted, as their phrase was, and the whole contents committed to the flames. But what must aftoniffi the world, and is more incredible than all the wonders that have been yet related of this wonderful business, is, that the noble house of Earl Mansfield should be forcibly entered about twelve o’clock at night; and, with all its elegant and costly furniture, its valuable paintings, and /till more valuable books and manu-feripts, set on fire, in the fight of a or 300 foldrers, who flood tame spectators of the conflagration, no one magistrate daring to command their service, although the Earl himself was in the house when first attacked, and Baron Hotham not far of T. What followed might be expected from the same infernal crew that now commenced zealots. The prisoners in the New Prison of Clerkenwell were dif-jnifltd, and the number of incendiaries

thereby augmented. Regular notices w^re sent to the other prisons at what time the inhabitants might expe<5t enlargement. In the mean time, Lord Petre’6 house in Park lane; Justice Hyde’s house, at Islington; the houses of MeflT. It may be alked, How were the corporation of London, the citizens of Westminster, and the inhabitants of Wapping and the lower parts, employed while all this mifehiefwas doing to individuals?' The answer is, the Lord Mayor was employed in laying before the court of Aldermen his correspondence with the secretaries of state, relative to the quieting the disturbance, and preventing further mischief; and all the rest of the people just enumerated were either lookers-on or listeners.—About this time, however, parties of the military (foot and light horse) arrived in town, to the number, as it was said, of 7000 men. Yet the rioters continued their depredations.

From a house kept in the name of one Moore, in which is published a weekly virulent paper, intitled. The Scourge, was issued this day the following handbill.

James Bradley being acquainted with what Mcfl'. Gates and Clark had this day said [352.], could not contradict them; but said, that having taken the oaths to the government, he did not think he should assist a Papist; but was very willing to be informed of his duty, and to execute the fame. But the Lord Mayor, by recommendation from the court, ac-

Jpainted him, that from that instant he ufpended him from the place of marshal-man. — The court recommended to the Lord Mayor to take the most effectual methods to prevent any riots or tumults within this city that his Lordship should think proper, and the court would defray all expences.

On Wednesday, June 7. blue cockades were become universal; blue flags were hung out at the doors or windows of al-moll every house in and about the metropolis; and there was hardly a house to be seen that had not written on different parts of it, the watch-word of the insurgents, No Popery.” Even the charity-houses for the old and helpless,. and the parish-workhouses were covered in a manner with fcrawls of chalk, of No Popery; God bless Lord George Gordon.” All those whose business would permit them to leave town, retreated with affright into the country; those who had families, and whose attention to their property kept them in town, sent off their children in whatever vehicles they could procure, not feru-pling any price. As for the sick, the a-ged, and the infirm, who could not be piovcd, or who had not the means to pay for removing themselves, they were under the fad necessity of abiding the e-

vent, having the dreadful prospect before them, of being torn in pieces by the rebellious malecontents, or of expiring amidst the flames of their burning bouies.

Desperate bodies of men patrolled the streets, many of them aping a military regularity, marching in files, armed with a variety of weapons, the most conspicuous and numerous of which were the pointed iron palisadoes torn from the buildings they had destroyed. They raised contributions from all passengers, whether on foot or in carriages, and regularly went up one fide of the streets and down the other, knocking at the doors of the houses and demanding money. Some asked only for shillings, others would have crowns, and many would take nothing but gold. Many house9 were forced to pay three timet over to different parties.

Early in the morning the insurgents sent notice to the King's-bench and Fleet prisons, that they intended to burn both places, and desiring the prisoners to remove their goods. The keepers of these prisons set open the doors, thereby giving the prisoners an opportunity of removing their effects; which they did with great assiduity.

The prisons were all to be destroyed, that there might be no place to confine the insurgents, should any of them be seized; the water-pipes were to be cut off; the Museum, the Tower, the grand arsenal at Woolwich, and the Royal Exchange, were also to be destroyed; and above all, the Pay-offices, the Bank, and the Royal Palaces, were to be stormed and plundered; and to render complete the ruin of individuals and the public, the Temple, Lincoln’s and Gray ’s Inn, as well as all other public buildings, were devoted to dcftrultion.

At a court of common council held this day it was unanimously resolved, That the Sheriffs of London be desired to raise the pofle comitatis immediately, and to pursue, with the Lord Mayor and other magistrates of this city, tbc most effectual legal means for restoring the public peace: That the thanks of the court be given to the Military Affocia-tion, for the offer of their services to restore the peace of this city, and that it be recommended to the sheriffs of this city to accept their offer: That the Sheriffs of London be requested to take tb$ military force under their command, and endeavour particularly to protect

July 1780. An account of the tumults in London, 359

%he Mansion-house, Guildhall, Bank of 'England, or any other place that is in changer: That the thanks of this court be £fon to the officers of the Militia of the city of London, for the voluntary offer of their services, and that they be re-quelled to put themselves under the direction of the Sheriffs of London. — A petition to parliament, praying for a repeal of the act in favour of Roman- Catholics, was resolved on.

The House of Lords met this day, but did little business; and adjourned to the ^Monday following.

The committee appointed by the Commons to inquire into the cause and motives of the present riots, fat for the first time.

The guards encamped this day in St James’s park, with twenty pieces of cannon, and there was likewise an encampment of military and militia in Hyde park: the yeomen of the guards, marshalmen, and all his Majesty’s dome* ftics at St James’s, and at the Queen’s palace, were ordered to be on duty all night, and were furnished with arms to repel any violence; and every necessary precaution was taken at the Tower, to repel a threatened attack on that grand arsenal of arms and stores.

About four after noon 500 soldiers were Rationed in the Royal Exchange, and a strong party in the court of the Bank; all the afternoon troops were taking post in the streets adjoining to and leading to the Bank and Lombard street, to protect the private bankers; the London military aflbeiation occupied [Broad street, where in the course of the night they did capital service.

The Lord Mayor and SherifTs kept patrolling the streets, and when they met bodies of the insurgents they talked and reasoned with them. The officers of the fix militia regiments furomoned every housekeeper in the city, either to fend a roan, or come under arms themselves at beat of drum; but the Lord Mayor and Sheriffs taking it into confederation, countermanded the order.

Regiments of horse and foot kept marching into town. As fast as they arrived they were ordered to the most important ports. Besides the places already mentioned, military were posted at the Excise and Post offices, and at Wood-street and the Poultry cori^>ter9. Strong guards were sent to the New-river head.

the Inns of Court, and the Mansion-house. Most of the members of both Houses of Parliament who had houses in town, applied for guards.

In the afternoon the privy council, finding that the magistrates and the civil authority had betn overpowered for many days, and that the magift rates would not authorise the military to endeavour to quell the insurrection, issued the following proclamation.

“ George R.

Whereas a great number of disorderly persons have affirm bled themselves together in a riotous and tumultuous manner, and have been guilty of many acts of treason and rebellion, having made an assault on the gaol of Newgate, set loose the prisoners confined therein, and set fire to and destroyed the said prison; and whereas houses are now pulling down in several parts of our cities of London and Westminster, and liberties thereof, and fires kindled for consuming the materials and furniture of the fame, whereby it is become absolutely necessary to use the most effectual means to quiet such disturbances, to preserve the lives and properties of individuals, and to restore the peace of the country: We therefore, taking the fame into our most serious consideration, have thought fit, by and with the advice of our privy council, to issue this our royal proclamation, hereby strictly charging and exhorting all our loving subjects to preserve the peace, and to keep themselves, their servants, and apprentices, quietly within their respective dwellings, to the end that all well-disposed persons may avoid those mischiefs which the continuance of such riotous proceedings may bring upon the gulity: And as it is necessary, from the circumstances before mentioned, to employ the military force, with which we are by law entrusted, for the immediate suppression of such rebellious and traitorous attempts, now making against the peace and dignity of our crown, and the safety of the live9 and properties of our subjetfs, we have therefore iflueci the most direct and effectual orders to all our officers, by an immediate exertion of their utmost force, to repress the fame; of which all persons are to take notice.”—Dated June 7.

Conformably to this proclamation the following orders were issued.

This proclamation and these orders gave rife to a false report that martial law was declared.

About fix in the evening great bodies of the insurgents assembled in different quarters of the town. One strong party went over Blackfriars bridge, firit Hopping some time at Bridewell hospital. On their arrival at the King’s-btfnch prison they were joined by the Borough rioters, and immediately set fire to the prison, now cleared of its inhabitants; the marshal of the prifoiV Thomas, having previously set the prisoners at liberty, and given them time to carry off their furniture, or what little necessaries they had. This very extensive prison, Which had been but a short time before greatly enlarged and rebuilt, was instan-taneoufly in flames, from the fire balls and chymical preparations thrown in by the insurgents, and with which they were well provided. This prison kept burning furiously all night. They also burnt the large elegant house near the prison, belonging to Mr Thomas the marshal. The new Bridewell in St George’s fields was also burnt at the same time.

Another formidable body broke into the Fleet prison, now also clear of prisoners, and set it on fire: it continued burning with great violence all night. This prison was also quite new, having been but lately totally rebuilt.

A third ftrorg body of the insurgents went again to the unfortunate Mr Lang-dale’s at Holborn bridge, where the night before, and all this day and pait of the evening, they had spirits brought out to them in as great plenty as they chose to drink or to call for. About seven o’clock in the evening the y forced their way into the still-house, rolled out the calks of spirit9, of which numbers of them drank immoderately. Whilst this was going on, others were plundering and gutting the inside of the house: they threw or brought out every thing that would bum, and all the empty casks from the still-house, piled them up of-

polite to St Andrew’s church, and mad* an immense bonfire. About nine at night the back part of the still-house was set on fire, the spirits that remained soon blazed up with incredible fury, (hooting upwards, as they catched, in columns of fire. The flames soon reached the back parts of the houses in Field lane, where several were burnt down. About ten o’clock they reached the front of Mr Langdale’s dwelling-house, which, with two houses on one fide, and one on the other fide, were entirely con fumed.

About half part nine o’clock, the time this house was set on fire, a large body of insurgents, preceded by a man with a flag, went up Holborn hill to a ne<r house just above Fetter lane, also belonging to Mr Langdale, or rather to his son, which had a large distil-house behind it. Into this house they instantly' burst, demolished the windows, threw all the furniture and utensils out into the street, where they were burnt; and many calks of spirits were rolled out, emptied in the street, and the casks thrown on the bonfire. At hats past ten the house was set on fire: the flames communicated to Bernard’s inn, part of which was burnt. About eleven, that is half an hour after the house was set on fire, a party of the Northumberland militia came into Holborn: they had marched that day near forty miles, and the moment they arrived at their place of rendezvous, which was Lincoln VInn fields, they were fentinto Holborn. As soon as they appeared before the bonfire in front of Mr Langdale’s house, the insurgents attacked them. The party then discharged their pieces; but, as they were only loaded with powder, no harm was done. This experiment was tried to intimidate the ruffians; for drift orders had been given, to use every lenient measure before coming to extremity. But it had no effect. The insurgents continuing their attack, and one of the officers being very much hurt, it was found absolutely necessary to fire with ball. Yet this firing was very prudently conducted: for the soldiers were not permitted to fire along the street; their officer drew them up on the north fide of Holborn, fronting the bonfire and the house on fire, so that no ptrfon could be hurt but those who were rioting round the fire, buying the furniture and calks, and those who were ft ill in the house plundering it. By this second firing two

or three were killed. This judicious conduit of the officer deserves great praise, for the streets were much crowded with people moving their most valuable goods, as they could not know where the fire would stop. No innocent perfoo could suffer by this firing, for it went directly across the bonfire into the fcoufc on fire. This second volley had the proper effclt, and drove off the in-furgents from the front of the house. But many rioters and plunderers were still within, the fire not yet having reached the front of the house. When any of these appeared at the doors or windows, the soldiers on the flank of the party ran up in an Igxlian file, that is each man singly following the track of the first, to the door or windows, and gave their fire, each man filing off as he fired. This continued till two o’clock. Both in this upper house of Mr Langdale’s, and in the lower house at Holborn bridge, numbers of the mif-creants got so intoxicated, that they fell Upon the spot, and were cither burnt, suffocated, or (mothered by the falling in of the roofs; for many bodies partly burnt, and others wirhout any marks of bu ruing, have been dug out of the ruins. Many also died of the excessive draughts which they took of the unreltified spirit9. Within a few hours after the calks bad been turned out, numbers on both fides of the streets were seen lying on the ground dead drunk. Such vast numbers of calks of spirits were rolled o\it of the upper still-house, and (laved, that the kennel ran with spirits; which being taken for water, was dammed up, and served to the engines playing on the houses adjoining to the lower house at Holborn bridge; for when the flames threatened that whole neighbourhood, two engines were permitted to play.

-AH over the town the inhabitants were obliged to illuminate, as on Tuesday night, and contributions were levied at the houses all the day and night; many people paying four, five, and fix times to different parties.

But the grand objelt of the iofurgents was the Bank. Between nine and ten seems to have been the appointed hour of mischief, as all the different conflagrations were then begun. About a quarter pad nine the attack on the Bank began. The fires just lighted in so many different quarters, feehave been designed to draw away part of the detachments from around the Bank. Luckily they had not the effect, All the a-Vol.XLIL

venues to the Bank were well guarded: A strong party at the Poultry barred the passage that way; another occupied Bartholomew lane on the other fide; a third and stronger party possessed the Royal Exchange in front of the Bank; a fourth were Itationed within the Bank court; the rear of the Bank they could not get at without fiift demolilliing a street of houses: no way was open for them but Threadneedle street, and on coming out there they were exposed to the posts at the Exchange, Bartholomew lane, and at the Bank. The first attack of the in* furgents began at the Poultry, to force the main passage: the objeH was too important to trifle with them; about a dozen solid platoons beat them back. At the same time another choice body of the insurgents, joined by the most de-sperateof the Borough rioters, made the grand attack on the Bank itself by Threadneedle street. To excite the felons the more warmly, a horse loaded with the chains of Newgate preceded instead of colours. Here they made a desperate attack, and drove back the horse with their cutlasses: but they were received by the foot in the same manner as at the Poultry; about a dozen solid platoons difeomfited them.

At the Fleet prison the rioters continued till three in the morning; when the military, after shewing most aftonifliing patience and forbearance, fired on them; by which three were killed.

In Broad street the insurgents demolished two houses: they were o'pposed by the gentlemen of the London Military Association, who at last were forced to fire, and killed nine; but the number of wounded is not known. The insurgents were completely defeated here likewise.

The Toll-houses on Blackfriars bridge were also burnt. The insurgents here got a very considerable booty in halfpence. Between one and two in the morning, a party of light horse came, and disturbed them in the midst of their plundering. One or two were killed, and several wounded, some of whom have fincedied in the hospital.

The inhabitants in the villages round London were up the greatest part of this night; they thought the whole city was on fire, so many places were in flames at the same time. The wind being north-east, those to leeward could, at a great distance, distinltly hear the voices of the ringleaders, give the vord when to huzza. In all about thirty-two platoons rL werf

were heard, with innumerable dropping and scattered {hot at intervals. The tremendous roaring and raging noise of the insurgents abated confulerably after the firings; their leaders with great difficulty could get them to huzza; their voices were clearly heard spiriting up the wretches they headed; and when they did huzza, it was in a very faint broken manner. In fatt, before day-light on Thursday morning, people who had attended to the progress of these infur-rections, declared, that they were in a great respect already suppressed.

During this night there were great commotions at the New Gaol, Marshalsea, Borough Clink, and in Kent street, where the watch-houses were destroyed. The insurgents who remained at the >.ing’s-bench, got fa intoxicated in the night, that they quarrelled among them-ielves, and attacked each other with brickbats, by which several of them were maimed.

Nothing could make a more dreadful appearance than the city of London and its environs on this night. The huge bodies of fire issuing from the different conflagrations of the Fleet prison, King's-bench, the Toll-houses on Blackfriars bridge, together with Mr Langdale the distiller’s two houses and warehouses on Kolborn bridge, and the top of the hill, and other fmiiler fires, and the illuminations all attending into the air, and consolidating together, formed an at-mosphere of flames, impressing the mind of the fpcvff.itor with an idea, as if not only the whole metropolis was burning, but all nations yielding to the final con-summation of things.

Had the Bank, Doctors Commons, and the public offices, been the first objects of the fury of the insurgents, instead of Newgate, the Fleet, and the KingVbench prisons, let any rational mind (igure to itself the confusion that thuft have ensued, the ruin that would bave been spread, the distresses in which prpbans, widows, natives, and foreigners, persons of all ranks and conditions, in whatever station, in whatever employment, would have been involved, by the annihilation of so many hundreds of millions of property, and the total abolition of all public credit ! Who can but for a moment think on the danger, without looking up to Heaven in grateful acknowledgement to the Supreme Being for ip signal a national deliverance

E Recorder gave his charge to the grand jury in the court of feflionat Guildhall, which was greatly crowded, on 1

Monday, June 26.----- He said, he should

not think he had acquitted himself in the 1 discharge of his duty, if he did not endeavour to throw out some hints whereby the gentlemen might understand the nature of the extraordinary business that would come before them. He then entered into a diferimination of the offences recently committed, and gave a description of the crimes. The grand jury (he said) should distinguish between misdemeanors and felonies. Those who directed their attention to any particular chapel, were guilty of misdemeanor or felony, according to the part they acted; and those who combined for a general destruction, were guilty of high treason against the state. The learned Serjeant reprobated the proceedings of the lawless rabble in terms of great indignation:

He compared them to the havock which a conqueror makes in an enemy’s country; and said, the devastations lately made were aftonifliing, and terrible in their effects. After touching on the late dreadful seenes with some energy, he desired the jury would blot them from their memories when they came upon their duty, and decide without the final, left prejudice: they were to receive and consider evidence with caution, and particularly to remember the confusion of ! the times, which was such as would render it highly incumbent upon the witnesses to be very circumspect in their testimony. On the one hand, the grand . jury were to be the guardians of the li- berty of the subject, and to prevent im- j proper motives having an influence to 0- 1

verturn the principles of fair and impartial justice taking place towards every man charged as a public offender. It was their province also to bring criminals of fa Angular a degree to the bar of Justice, that they may be dealt with according to their deserts. They were to act with firmness and impartiality, re-girdlefs of threats, and without favour or all’edtion. Having said much more on the subject, the Recorder concluded, with thanking them for coming forward ' with alacrity at this particular period, i to perform a ^ry disagreeable and trou- ] blefomc office, not altogether free from the njo{\ serious danger.

persons in a very sensible and pathetic speech. He informed them, that if there were any favourable circumstances in their cases, they must now represent them to his Majesty, who alone could extend mercy. He expatiated on the enormity of their offences, and their dreadful tendency to society. He said, that his own feelings in discharge of the most painful part of his duty, and the regret which every man must feel at fee. ing such a number of unhappy persons sentenced to the just punishment of their offences, were considerably abated by the extreme tenderness and care which the juries, and every other person concerned in the adeninistration of justice on the melancholy occasion, had exercised. He exorted them, that the sense of the enormity of their offences might be im pic fled on their minds and consciences; that they might employ the short time allowed them to live, in preparing themselves for that mercy at a higher and more important tribunal, which the laws of civil society could not extend to them. He then proceeded to pass sentence of death.

The number tried was 85; of whom 35 were capitally convicted, 7 convicted of tingle felony, and 43 acquitted.

Fifty of the rioters were tried by a special commission, to continue till the a9th of July, at St Margaret’s Hill, Southwark, before the Lord Chief Ju-if ice Loughborough, Mr Justice Gould, Mr Baron Eyre, and Mr Justice Buller. We have inserted the Lord Chief Justice’8 charge to the grand jury entire [337], as taken in short hand.—'The trials began on Tuesday July 11. and were continued every day till Tuesday, July 18. inclusive, Sunday excepted.

On the first trial, Mr Wallace, the Attorney-General, stated the general law to the grand jury, That though the prisoners fhotild not be found to have taken an active part with their hands, yet if they were proved to have encouraged the mob to acts of outrage by their presence, they would in the construdion of law be equally guilty.

William Hayter was tried for pulling down the houie of Mr French, of East lane, June 7. It was proved, that in Mi French’s house he had examined the people respecting their religion, and talk-ed much about the feriptures and St Paul, and against Popery; and also declared, that be would be glad to fee all

estates fp levelled, that no man in the kingdom should be possessed of more than a thousand a-year. He was fbusd guilty, but recommended to mercy the jury. Baron Eyre said, he wookl very willingly concur with the jury in a -ny proper meafore; but he referred it to their own prudence to con6der, bow far it would be proper to recommend to mercy a man who had broached a doctrine, so dangerous to the state, and to society, as that of levelling fortunes, which could not be done without robbery to the present proprietors of estates worth more than a thousand a-year. The jury acquiesced in this remonstrance. Hayter, though he was so fuR of religious conversation, got so intoxicated with the liquor in Mr French’s cellars, that he fell on the spot. Early-next morning he was found dead drunk by Mr French’s servants.

Before Lord Loughborough summed up the evidence against John Bartoo, who was tried, July 14. for pulling down the house of Edward Dodd in Lombard street, he said, he had been considering with his brothers on the bench, whether the prisoner might be benefited by any doubt of law upon the construdion of the statute; as it had not been proved, that there were so many as twelve persons concerned in destroying the house: one had sworn be believed there were about twelve in number; another, that there were about half a score: and if the numbers could make any thing in favour of the prisoner in point of law, every allowance would be made for him. They were however all of opinion, that no doubt could arise upon the construe-tion of the act of parliament. Any number were sufficient in point of law to constitutu a riot f it is not necessary that there should be so many as twelve in number.

On Saturday July 15. twenty prisoners, who had then been convicted, were put to the bar, to receive sentence, viz. July 1780, Trials of the Rioters; 3

iannah Howard, Samuel Lyman, John Wyde, William Inbeft, Samuel Jordan, George Fletcher, James Palmer, Benjamin Rowland, Oliver Johnson, Eliz. Collins, Theodore Atkinson, and John Barton. — Lord Loughborough addressed them as follows. “ You have each of you been indicted for several offences, in unlawfully demolishing and pulling down the dwelling-houses of federal different persons; and for your trials you have put yourselves upon God and your country. You have had a full trial by a jury of your country; that jury has convicted you upon the clearest and fulleft evidence: you have all of you had the benefit of a trial by a jury, who have not only been attentive, instruded, and enlightened, but full Of humanity and companion; who have omitted no circumstance that could induce them to entertain the least favourable opinion in the case of any of the prisoners that have come before them; you stand therefore now objects of the justice and of the fe-verity of the law; your lives are forfeited to the law, for very dangerous offences committed against the property of your neighbours, and the peace of the country. The end of all punishment is example: the danger and enormity of the offences that have lately been committed will require a severe example, to deter such of your associates as have not been detected, and all others, from committing such dangerous excesses. Among the number of those unhappy ob-jeds who are now before me, it is possible there may be found some persons less guilty, towards whom his Majesty may be induced to shew that mercy which is ever near to his royal breast; but as all of you have forfeited your lives to the justice of your country, let me intreat you, that none of you will flatter himself, that it will be his or her lot to be saved: the necessity of public joftice requires a severe and a strong example. If there arc any whose cases are more favourable than others, (and I am very sorry to say there are very few cases marked with any circumstances of favour), those instances will be, which is the only satisfaction you can now feel, as fairly represented as they will bear. The tenderness of the jury has induced them, among the number that now stand to receive sentence, to recommend three [Hayter. Palmer, and Barton 1 particular objects to mercy; I shall think it my

duty, upon every occasion, to represent how excellent the conduct and behaviour of that jury who made that recommendation has been; at the same time I shall think it my duty, painful as it is> where, in my opinion, circumstances occurred that ought to turn back the tide of mercy, to state those circumstances, though they make against the recommendation: let not, therefore, those persons who have had the benefit of a recommendation to mercy from the jury, flatter themselves that their case is certain to be saved: 1 am much afraid that certainly all of, them cannot be saved; and therefore each of them should suppose that their case may be to be among the sufferers, in order that they, together with all the others, may have all that time which now remains for consideration in rtheir unhappy situation. There is time for you all to turn your minds to the fountain of mercy, where mercy andjustice can be reconciled by ways inscrutable to our understandings. You will have the benefit of the assistance of a worthy and pious clergyman, to direct your thoughts to a proper preparation for ;that more awful tribunal before which you are shortly to appear: I trust that you will make good use of the time that will remain. 1 trust the approach of that day which the commandments of God has directed to be kept holy, will dispose your minds and hearts to a better frame; and that you will avail yourselves of the time the indulgence of the law allows, between the sentence and the execution, to prepare yourselves for mercy, if mercy should be the fate of any; if not, to receive that awful dissolution of life by the bands of public justice, with a due reverence to the laws of your country, and with a proper temper of mind becoming Christians. — Nothing now remains to me, but the painful part of pronouncing the sentence of the law. The sentence of the law is, and the court awards it. That you, and every one of you, be taken from hence to the place from whence you came, and from thence to a place of execution, and that you be there hanged by the neck until you are dead; and may the Lord have mercy upon all your

fouls !”----- The prisoners all fell upon

their knees, and cried, Amen.

William Saunders, a lad about sixteen, was tried for beginning to pull down a house. One witness fvvorc, that Saunders

366 Trials of the Riotefs. Sir Fr. Drake VOL4I.

ders broke a pane of glass in the house with a (tick; that he (the witness) immediately said to him, Begone, you little rafeal! what business have you here f upon which Saunders went away, and returned no more. — Lord Loughborough said to Mr Howartb, counsel for the crown, that the boy’s case was but just within the law, and he wished Mr Howarth to relieve him and the jury. Mr Howarth consented to drop the prosecution; and the jury brought in a ver-di<5t, Not guilty.

James Ewer, who had given evidence against one of the prisoners on the 15th, informed the court, that he had been threatened by the lieutenant of the prels-gang to which the prisoner belonged, to impress him. The court immediately dispatched a messenger after the lieutenant. When he arrived, Lord Loughborough made some very pointed observations on the impropriety of any person’s endeavouring to intimidate witnesses from giving evidence, or insulting them for having given it; and informed this young gentleman, who did not seem to know the consequence of impeding the administration of justice, or to be sensible of the lenity of the cqurt, that Ewer mult be expressly under his protection, and that he mult give it in orders to all his men, to protect him, as he would answer for any insult or injury offered him at his peril.

On Tuesday, July 18. four more prisoners were brought to the bar, and had sentence of death passed on them by Lord Loughborough, thus.—“ Henry Penny, John Bridport, Richard Miller, Joseph Haynes, You have each of you been indicted for the several offences of pulling down dwelling-houses; for your trial you have put yourselves upon God and your country; and you have been convicted of these offences by a verdict of your country. I trult it will be unne-cefiary for me to enlarge to any of you upon the nature of the offences that you have committed. The fellowship you mull have had with the unfortunate men, upon whom it has lately been my misfortune to have passed a similar sentence, mud, 1 hope and trust, have awaked in your minds a just sense of your offences, and produced in you sentiments befitting the situation in which you stand. The only part of the duty imposed upon me that I can ever discharge without pain, will be, to represent, at the foot of the throne, those circumstances that may

draw forth his Majesty’s clemency to such of you as shall seem to deserve it.

— Two of the unhappy men whom I now address, have severally upon their trials met with a favourable representa- \ tion from the jury; but it is my duty not to awaken a false hope, nor suffer it to rife in the mind of any one of you who heard that recommendation, and was tried upon a second charge, upon , which the lentiments of the jury did not , support their first wish for mercy. It is j uncertain what effect any representation j that may be made of your cases can have i to obtain mercy upon earth; it is, I am afraid, but too certain, that, in compas-lion to all his Majesty’s subjects, mercy cannot reach all of you; it is therefore the duty, and it will be the interclt, of each of you, to suppose that he is amongst the number of persons who can find no mercy here; and it will then only remain that you all apply yourselves, with fervency and devotion, to obtain mercy of that awful power, who par-doneth and absolveth all them that truly repent, and unfeignedly confess their faults.”—The sentence was then pronounced in the usual form.

The Lord Chief Justice then dismissed j the grand and petit jurors, with polite acknowledgements for their attendance and attention.

At St Margaret's hill, the number tried was 50; of whom were capitally convicted, and 26 were acquitted.

In all tried, at the Did Bailey and St Margaret*s-hill, 135; of whom 59 were convicted.

Sir Francis Drake: naval anecdote.

'THE late Sir Francis Drake having fell in with a French 60 gun ship off Cape Saint Vincent’s, which he, though in a frigate of only a8 guns, engaged, i and by a variety of devices, and plying the Frenchmen close with all his guns, at taft forced to strike her colours, anil brought her safe into Portsmouth, was surprised on his arrival, at an order for him not to go on fiiore till he had received the royal pardon for risking his ship’s crew; which accordingly came down to him a few days after. “ Well,

(fays the Captain), if it be a crime for a little Englishi ship to take a large Frenchman, I must quit the service, and get promoted; for i plainly fee I shall be often making an attempt to be guilty of 1 such crimes.”

PARLIAMENT. [318.3

On the ad of June a petition of his Majesty’s loyal Protestant subjects of the cities of London and Westminster, borough of Southwark, and their environs, was presented to the Commons [283], nnd read; setting forth, That the petitioners, fcnljble of the many blessings which, asProtestantsand Britons, they enjoy, cannot but be alarmed at an act, passed in the 18th year of the reign of his present Majesty, repealing certain penalties and disabilities imposed on the Papists by a ftalute enacted in the nth and nth years of William III. for further presenting the growth of Popery; and it is with the deepest concern that they petition this Honourable House against a law which has already received the royal assent; but, as this act was so suddenly introduced, and so hastily passed, before the sense of the nation at large could be obtained, or any opposition formed against it, they arc encouraged to hope that this application will not prove too late for redress; and that, notwithstanding the late aft might be intended to repeal only detached parts of a single statute, the petitioners conceive that it is an absolute repeal of the most efiential clauses in that statute, and will operate virtually to render those acts against the Papists that are yet unrepealed of none effect; and that the petitioners apprehend, that the Papists construe the late indulgence of parliament to be a toleration of Popery, as appears by the mass-houses and schools which they are opening throughout the kingdom, and by their printing Popish books, and publicly exposing them to sale; and that Po-pish bishops, priests, Jesuits, andschool-masters, now openly exercise their functions; whereby the people, especially the riling generation, arc in danger of being led into fupsrftition, idolatry, and rebellion; and that Papists can now purchase what lands, tenements, or hereditaments, they please, and inherit the fame; that they will thereby influence oureleftions in future parliaments, and that this must tend to the destruction of our happy constitution; and that as Papists can now by legal authority confess the ecclesi alii cal or spiritual jurisdiction of the Pope and fee of Rome, which otir laws, before the passing of the late aft, have constantly disavowed, the petitioners are very much alanncd left

they should be involved in the guilt of perjury, when called to declare upon oath, “ That no foreign prince, person, prelate, state, or potentate, hath any jurisdiction or authority, ecclesiastical or spiritual, in this realm and that Popery is in its nature intolerant, in a Protestant country seditious, and in this kingdom disaffected to the present reigning family, and therefore to encourage it tends to the fubvcrlion of the state, to dethrone the King, and to set aside the fucccftion of the illustrious house of Hanover to the crown of this kingdom; and that as England and Scotland are united, the petitioners cannot but think it ahardftiipuponthe people of England, to have Popery countenanced among them by law, when their brethren in Scotland have been ofticially allured that no law shall be made to favour Popery in that country; and the petitioners presume, that their peaceable deportment, and the constitutional steps they have taken to obtain redress, will meet the approbation of this Honourable House; and that the petitioners do not desire to persecute the Papists, but to preserve themselves and their posterity from a repetition of those rebellious and bloody scenes which Popery, under pretence of promoting the interest of the church, has exhibited in these kingdoms: wherefore, to preserve the fucccftion of the il-laftrious house of Hanover in the Pro-’ testant line, and to secure our civil and religious liberties against the incroachments of Popery to the latest posterity, the petitioners humbly pray, that leave may be given to bring in a bill, to repeal the act lately passed in favour of the Papists.

The petition was referred to a committee of the whole house.—And a motion being made, for the House to rc-solve itself immediately into the said committee, another motion was made, to adjourn; which passed in the negative. Lord North, Mr Fox, Mr Burke, and the Lord Advocate, in long speeches, argued against the repeal of the bill. T'hey pointed out the fatal consequences that must befal the Roman-Catholies if the act should be repealed. Many of them had acknowledged themselves publicly as proprietors of estates which they bad formerly held in trust; a repeal must Tob them of these: Many, in conference of the late aft, had made wills, and marriage-settlements, which it would be the height of injusticeto annul or violate. On that ground a repeal certainly ought not, and consequently could not take place. With respect to schools for Roman'Catholic children, every man who had a spark of humanity must admit that they were necessary; as it would be barbarity in the extreme, to force children from their parents to rear them up in a different religion.

Mr Ambler and Sir P. Clerke argued for a repeal. The latter said, that he did not want to force Roman-Cat holies to bring up their children Protestants; but he wanted to prevent them from reducing the children of Protestants, with a view to make them Papists. A Noble Lord, be said, had declared, that the petitions were not grounded upon fast: in order to discover whether that after-tion was true or not, he moved. On the 21st of June, while the petitions for a repeal of the act i8° Geo. III. in favour of Roman Catholics was under the consideration of the Commons, there was a conversation on that subject in the House of Lords.----- The Duke of Graf-

ton expressed his astonishment, after so many petitions had been presented for the repeal of that act, and such dreadful disturbances had happened, that no motion had come from any Noble Lord in administration to bring the matter under consideration. He had heard, he said, of resolutions made in the other House, and of a bill to be brought in there for some amendments in the offensive aft; but he thought, that in all cases that concerned the combined civil and religious rights of the nation, but more especially the religious, bills should originate in the House of Lords, because they had the assistance of the Rt Rev. Bench of Bishops; and therefore he called upon that Bench, to know, if they had been convened to give their opinions upon the expediency of repealing or amending that bill, by his Majesty’s ministers, cither before or since the disturbances; and de^ dared, that if some good reason was not assigned by them, or the Lords in administration, why that House should wait for a bill coming up from the other, he would move for a day to take the repeal 3 A o

3 70 Parliament: Loi

of the act into consideration, before the Bishops were called off by the season of the year to their annual visitations, when the House would be deprived of their assistance.

The Bishop of Peterborough delivered his sentiments in an eloquent concise speech. He held it as a maxim. That when popular prejudices, and the passions inherent to human nature, ran high in the breasts of the people, that is not the season for abrogating old laws, or enacting new ones, militating against those prejudices and passions. He thought the time unfeasonable when the act was passed; for we were involved in the unhappy American contest, and on the eve of a war with France and Spain; so that nothing was wanting but the fury of religious phrenzy to fill up the measure of our national misfortunes. Though no man was a warmer friend to religious toleration, nor a greater enemy to perfection; yet he saw defeats in that bill, and at the second reading had expressed his apprehension, that mifrepresentatioii6 and ill consequences would arise from the want of a clause to prevent the Roman-Catholics making converts, and educating children of Protestants in their faith; especially as he knew that they offered pecuniary rewards, which are arguments that indigent persons will find too powerful to resist. He was therefore glad to hear, that a bill was to be brought into the other House to rectify this defeat. Another objection was, the enabling Roman-Catholics to purchase lands, and settle them on their children, the fame as Protestants; as this might lead to acts of cruelty and injustice; for if the eldest son should renounce the Romish faith in the lifetime of his father, he might settle his Estate on theyoungeft, being a Roman.

The Bishop of Llatidnff and the Bishop Cf Rochester both lpoke against a repeal of the act, as it had been in many parts roifunderstood, and because it would be a dangerous precedent at this time; but they thought it highly expedient, while they were secured in the peaceable enjoyment of their religion, and the natural right of educating their own children, that they should be strictly prohibited from seducing the children of Protestants to be educated in their schools. As the petitions were presented to the other House, they recommended that the resolutions of that House, or the bill framed there, should be waited for.—-The

If therefore their Lordships should be of opinion, that Popish schools have increased, and that Protestant children are seduced to be educated in them by Romish teachers in the principles of their religion, the provisions in this bill would be very proper, and should meet with his concurrence.

The Marquis of Rockingham was for restraining the Papists as much as possible from educating the children of Protestants; but when that wa9 done, he hoped there would be no infringement on the toleration granted them for the free exercise of their religion, nor any restric-tian upon their building or repairing chapels for public worfliipt for it did not appear to him that they had erected many new ones. Much had been said about the chapel at Bath; but, after all, it was not a new one: there had been a chapel on the spot ever since Elisabeth’s time; part of it had been pulled down to widen a street, and an addition had been made in another position about five years ago, before the bill in their favour was thought of, which made it larger than the old chapel, and suitable to the

An idea had got into the world, that the number of Papists had very much increased within late years. Now the reverse was the fad. When this description of men were enumerated about two generations ago, by an eminent divine. Dr Gaftrcll, the total amount at that time was 68,coo.
