Roman-Catholics be permitted to teach any thing in any manner they think proper, only that they be not differed to keep boarding-schools, or any such fe-minary as may give them the exclusive government of the children under their direction.

On a motion of Earl Bathurd, an amendment was made to the Bishop of Rochester’8 amendment, with that Prelate’s consent, by inserting the words, “ inltrudtion, government, and boarding, of Protestant children.’*

The Bishop of Ely was for putting off the bill to another session, and for moving a committee of the whole House early in the next session, to inquire into all the laws in being respecting the Pa-picts; for, he said, neither Papifh nor ProtedantB knew them at present. Some more Lords were for poflponing it.

After the body of the bill was gone through, a very material alteration was made in the title upon a motion of Earl Bathurd. It ran before tn these words: “ A bill to securethe Protestant religion in Great Britain from any encroachment® of Popery,’* See. His Lordship said, God forbid it should go out of that House, that the Rev. Prelates, or any other Lo*“ds, thought the Protestant religion in this country in any danger front any encroachments whatever. He then proposed the following title, which was agreed to: “ A bill for more effectually rest raining Papists, or persons professing the Romish religion, from taking upon them the education, government, and board of the children of Protestants.”

The committee passed the bill without any further amendment.

On the report, next day, the Ardi-bishop of Canterbury said, that being more fully informed as to the import and tendency of the amendments proposed, he mull give them his negative. His Grace exprdTcd himself an enemy to every species of intoleration; and conceived, that it would be acting with a degree of harfliness and severity wholly iinjuftiliable, to deprive parents of the privilege of educating their children in the religious principles they themselvca professed. But as the Roman-Catholier entertained principles inimical to the civil and religious institutionsof this country, the duties of his Ration compelled him to declare, as his opinion, that Roman-Catholics ought not to be afforded the opportunity of teaching in day-

schools,

I

July 1780. Parliament: Lords on Popery. m

fbheols, or of having in any other manner the power of instilling the principles of the Roman-Catholic faith into the minds of their pupils, which the propo-fed amendments would most certainly enable them to effect. His Lordship, for these and other reasons, could not a -gree to the amendments, which had made the bill more offensive to him than it was in the state in which it came from the Commons; and his Grace concluded with saying, that he believed some absent members of the bench coincided* with him in opinion.

The Bishop of St David'9 said, he had not yet spoken on the subject now under difcufiion. When the bill passed for repealing the act n° "ia° Will. HI. he had not the honour of a feat in that House: bathe had,among his private friends observed, that it did not promise to produce any salutary effect, as the Roman-Catholic subjects of these realms were not under any kind of perfection 5 and expressed his appreheufions that the bill might probably create jealousies and animosities in the minds of the Protestants.

An erroneous idea had been diflemi-r nated abroad, purporting that the Bench of Bishops were inclined to encourage Popery: but he could take upon himself to alTert, that the Rev. Prelates entertained not the most distant idea of encouraging any doCtrines militating against the established religion and civil institutions of the country. Indulgences that would not interfere with these im-ortant doCtrines, he would not oppose; ut was apprehensive, that permitting day-fehooh to be kept by Roman-Catholics, would afford too fatal opportunities for the perversion of young minds from the religion of their ancestors.

Persons of the Roman Catholic persuafion are zealously attached to their religion, and indefatigably aflidnous in making converts to their faith. The son of a poor man cannot be educated under a Protestant schoolmaster at a less expence than forty shillings a-year, while a Roman-Catholic will instruct him without any pecuniary reward. Thus is a dangerous temptation held forth to people in indigent circumstances.

The Noble Lord on the woolsack had the preceding day observed, that Papists being allowed to teach Protestant children the languages, mathematics. See, could not expose them to the danger of

being perverted to Popery: but he mull observe, that zealous advocates for Pope* ry, acting as instructors in these branches of learning, would seize opportunities of influencing the minds of their pupils.

Noble Lords need not be informed of so notorious a fact, that children educated in foreign seminaries, generally returned to England impressed with pre-possessions in favour of Popery. He wilhed that every indulgence might be granted to Papists which could be allowed with (perfect safety to our laws and religion; but at the same time was desirous of adopting every necessary precaution for defending our religion from the innovations of Popery.

There were objectionable clauses in the act ii° Sc i%° Will. III.; they were oppressive, and inconliftent with the principles of Christianity, particularly that clause for disinheriting issue professing Popish doctrines.

His Lordship hoped the laws relating to Popery would be subjected to a revision, and that a bill might be framed to abolish every cause of complaint from the Papists, and secure the Protestant religion from every innovation.

The Lord Chancellor said, he had the preceding day delivered his sentiments pretty fully; but the high respect that was due to all that came from so truly respectable authority as the Most Reverend and Right Reverend Prelates who had just now fpokc, called him to speafe further.

The Rt Rev. Prelate had taken up the question in two points of view. By the first argument it was meant to be inferred, That the principles of the Romance holic religion were subversive of the religious establishments, and the laws of civil focrety and political government of these realms. By the second, That the bill and the amendments were calculated to countenance and encourage Popery* and endanger the Protestant system.

The Noble Lord admitted, that the Roman-Catholic9 zealously endeavoured to gain proselytes to the religion they profeffed. All men who are sincere iir their religious tenets, wish to infptre others with the opinions which they conceive to be right. Independents, Me-thodifts, Jews, &c. follow different modes of religious practice; and it \s but fair and reasonable to allow them the privilege of acting according to the conviction of their own minds.

His

$74 Parliament: Lords on Popery* Vol. 4

His Lordship went into an elaborate difeuflion of the arguments advanced by the Rt Rev. Prelate, and endeavoured to prove, that the bill was not likely to be attended with any injurious consequences. He would rather wifti that the whole body of Roman-Catholics should be exterminated the kingdom, than that they should remain the monuments of theinjustice, caprice, and levity, of parliament. His Lordship concluded with declaring his sentiments were strongly in favour of every species of toleration that could be granted consistent with thecon-ftitutional establifhments of this country.

The Marquis of Rockingham corrected some miltakes in the Archbishop of Canterbury, shewing, that the act 180 Geo. 111. did not repeal the whole, but only particular parts of the act xi° & ia° Will. 111. [40. 393-]

The question being put, Whether the amendment should be admitted ? the House divided: Contents 44, non-contents 10.

The Lord President, in order to get rid of the bill and amendments together, moved, That the bill be further considered on Tuesday next, before which time parliament will be prorogued; and upon a division there were. Contents 9, non-contents xi.

On £ motion made, July 5. to read the bill a third time, the Duke of Chan-dos intreated the attention of the House for a few minutes. He professed his attachment to Protestant principles, and laid there w'as nothing which he would not do, or fuller, in their Support. Yet he disclaimed all principles of perfection; and thought that Roman-Catholics, being quiet and loyal fuhjcCls, had as good a right to toleration as any of the other numerous fcCts in this country. But the circumstance which chiefly determined him to vote against the bill, was the lime and manner of its being brought into and enforced in the other House. He dclired their Lordships to recoiled the indignities and outrages they suffered from a mob, who Called themselves Protestants, and who were the patrons of this bill, a few weeks ago. Not a Lord of Parliament could for several days come to this house, or go out of it, without endangering his life. These things he presumed were fresh in their Lordships memory, nor would they easily forget therm It was unfuitabk to their Lordships gravity,

and unbecoming their dignity, by hastily agreeing to a bill brought into parliament in such circumstances, and' urged by such indecent and outrageous threats, to encourage mobs and infurreCtion6. A bill of such importance ought not to be agitated in a thin House, and at the end of a session; but in a full House, and at a time when there should be leisure to give it a patient hearing and a full dif-culfion. But that was not the circumstance which made the deepest impref-(ion on his Grace’s mind: He considered the treatment that this bill should meet with as the touchstone by which the nation would judge, whether the independency and freedom of parliament were to be maintained with vigour, or whether the parliament were to yield to the importunities and insolence of every mob. If this bill should be paftVd at this time, he expected to fee the House of Commons and the House o( Peers be-set, on a thousand occasions, and on every occaGon indeed, by a paflicnate and misguided tumultuous rabble. He would# as long as he enjoyed the honour of a feat in that House, speak without fear the sentiments that flowed from his heart. Such were those which he now expressed. His Grace added a few words, and concluded with moving, “ That the bill be rejected.”

On the question' being put, the House divided, when the numbers were. Contents (for rejecting the bill) 17, Not-contents 9.— So the bill twas rejected by near two to one.

By this bill, any Papist, orperson pro-felling the Popi(h religion, who should take upon him or her the education or boarding of the child or children of a Protestant, and any Protestant who should knowingly fuller his or her child or children, or any child or children of Protestant parents intrusted to his or her guardianship, to be educated or boarded by a Papist, were subjected to a fine and imprisonment; teaching the arts or ex-crcifcs of music, drawing, dancing, fencing, or riding, excepted. By the amendments made, all Popish teachers# public and private, were to register their names and places of abode; and per Ions teaching as tutors, orasschoolmasters or schoolmiftrefles, not so registered, were to forfeit 1001. and to suffer one year’s imprisonment: and a clause respeChng boarding-schools was extended to day* lchools,

Philadelphia, June 17. Last Wednesday evening arrived Lt-Col. Ternant, with the following dispatchesfrom Maj.-Cen. Lincoln to Congress.

4* Sir, Chorlejluivn, May 24.1780. The inclosed papers will inform Congress of each important circumstance which has occurred in this department since I did myself the honour to write to them on the 9th ult. by Mr Cannon.

They will hereby observe, that after every effort and exertion made by a handful of brave troops, contending with mimberlefs hardships and difficulties, (to all which they most chearfully submitted), we were reduced to the fad necessity of treating with Sir Henry Clinton, and acceding to the terms of capitulation which accompany this letter.

1 shall not at present go into a detail of the matter, as I expert to reach Congress before this: but should I not, Lt-Col. Ternant, who is the bearer of this letter, will be able to give a minute state of things: 1 must beg leave therefore to refer Congress to that gentleman; and to allure them, that his steady attention to duty, and zeal for the service, intitle him to every respect.

I have the honour to be, See.

B. Lincoln.

P. S. Lt-Col. Ternant will be able to inform Congress what has caused so much delay in getting off the dispatches.”

In the London Gazette of July 8. we have a letter from Gen. Clinton to Lord George Germain, dated, Headquarters, Charlestown, South Carolina, June 3. 1780, a hand-bill and two proclamations mentioned in that letter, and an address from divers inhabitants of Charlestown to the Commissioners for restoring peace in North America; which we insert verbatim, viz.

Sir Henry Clinton's u My Lord,

I have the honour to inclose, for your Lordship’s information, the copies of two proclamations I have found it necessary to issue as commander in chief, and the copy of a hand-bill which has been circulated amongst the inhabitants, and appears to have had a very good etfe<ft.

I have the honour to be, &c.

H. Clintom. Hand-bill issued after the surrendsr cf

Chatleftown,

41 When the Royal army arrived in South Carolina, the Commander in Chief avoided, as much as possible, every measure which might excite the loyal inhabitants to rife in favour of government, and thus bring danger and trouble upon themselves, at a time when the King’s army, being employed in the reduction of Charlestown, could not assist or second their struggles.

The blood of the loyalists that had been unhappily lhed, and the severities which had been tofli&ed on them by the rebels, in consequence of the former spirited but ill-timed infurredlions of the King’s numerous friends on the back of both Carolinas, had already occasioned too much grief and regret to his Majesty and their fiellow-fobjeds in Europe, for him wantonly to bring again into hazard the lives and happiness of o*ep who deserve so well of their country.

But Charlestown with its harbour, and Fort Moultrie, being now reduced, and their garrisons, to the amount of 6coo men, with ail their arms, stores, artillery, and ships of war, being in possession of his Majesty’s fortes, the time is come when it is equally the interest and duty of every good man to be in readiness to join the king’s troops, and assist them in establishing justice and liberty, and in restoring and securing their own property, whenever they shall march to support them against the small rebel parties that still linger at a distance in the province.

After so much disorder, violence, and opprelBon, the helping band of every man is wanted to re-establi(h peace and good government; and as the Commander in Chief wilbed not to draw the King’s friends into danger when any . doubt could remain of their success; so, now that that is certain, he trusts, that one and all will heartily join, and, by a general concurrence, give effect to such necessary measures for that purpose, as, from time to time, maybe pointed out ! to them. And they may rest assured,

that every means will be used to avoid

giving them any trouble that may not be necessary to secure to them, peace, liberty, and prosperity.

In order to attain these happy ends, ft is the duty of all men who with well to themselves and their country, to be ready, at a moment, with their arms, to regain their just rights, and support the free constitution of their forefathers, under which we all increased and prospered.

Those who have families, will form a militia, to remain at home, and occasionally to assemble in their own districts, when required, under officers of their own chusing, for the maintenance of peace and good order. Those who have no families, and can conveniently be spared for a time, it is hoped will chearfully assist his Majesty’a troops in driving their rebel oppressors, and all the miseries of war, far from the province.

, For this purpose it is necessary, that the young men be ready to assemble when required, and serve with the King’s troops, for any six months of the ensuing twelve that may be found requisite, under proper regulations. They may chuse officers to each company to command them, and will be allowed, when on service, pay, ammunition, and provisions, in the lame manner as the troops. When they join the army, each man will be furnished with a certificate, declaring, that he is only engaged tp serve as a militiaman for the time specified; that he is not to be marched beyond North Carolina and Georgia; and that when the time is out, he is freed from all claims whatever of military service, except the common and usual militia-duty where he lives.

He will then have paid his debt to his country, and be intitled to enjoy undif-turbed that peace, liberty, and proper? ty, at home, which he had contributed to secure.”

July 1780. America: Proclamations at Charlestown* 377

“SOUf H CAROLINA.

By hi* Excellency Sir Henry Clintdn, Knight of the Aloft Honourable Order of the Bath, General and Commander in Chief of all his Majesty’s Forces within the Colonies lying on the Atlantic Ocean, from Nova Scptia to Weft Florida inclusive, PROCLAMATION.

- Whereas, notwithstanding the gracious offers which have been made, to' receive to his Majesty’s peace and protection, with pardon and oblivion for their pait offences, all those his deluded and infetuated subjects who should return to their duty and a due obedience to the laws [260.]; yet there are some wicked and desperate men, who, rc-gardlefs of the ruin and misery in which the country will be involved, are still endeavouring to support the flame of rebellion, and, under pretence of authority derived from the late usurped lcgif-latures, are attempting, by enormous floes, grievous imprifouments, and fan-guinary punishments, to compel his Majesty’s faithful and unwilling subject9 to take up arms against his authority and government; and it is therefore become necessary, as well for the protection of the loyal subjects, as to procure the establishment of peace and good government in the country, to prevent, by the terror of example, such enormous offences being committed in future; 1 have therefore thought fit to issue this my proclamation, to declare. That if any person shall hereafter appear in arms in order to prevent the establishment of his Majesty’s government in this country, or shall, under any pretence or authority whatsoever, attempt to compel any other person or persons to do so, or shall hinder or intimidate, or attempt to hinder or intimidate, the King’s faithful and loyal subjects from joining his forces, or otherwise performing those duties their allegiance requires, such person or persons so offending shall be treated with that severity so hardened and criminal an obstinacy will deserve, and his or their estates will be immediately seized in order to be confiscated. And for the encouragement of the King’s faithful and peaceable subjecto, 1 do again assure them, that they shall meet with effectual countenance, protection, and support; and whenever the situation of the country will permit of the restoration of civil Vol.XLII.

government and peace, they will, by the commissioners appointed by his Majesty for that purpose, be restored to the full/ possession of that liberty in their persons and property which they had before experienced under the British government. And that so desirable an event may be the more speedily accomplished, 1 do hereby, in his Majesty’s name, require and command all persons whatever to be, aiding and assisting to his forces, whenever they shall be required, in order to extirpate the rebellion, and thereby restore peace and prosperity to this, at present, desolated anddiftra&ed country. Given under my hand, at head quarters

in Charlestown, the sad day of May,

,78o> H. CLINTON.

By his Excellency’s command, Nathaniel Philips, AIM.See.”

“SOUTH CAROLINA.

By his Excellency Sir Henry Clinton, &c.

PROCLAMATION.

Whereas, after the arrival of his Majesty’s forces under my command iff this province, in February last, numbers of persons were made prisoners by the army, or voluntarily surrendered them* selves as such, and such persons werd afterwards dismissed on their respcCtivE paroles [267.]: And whereas, since the surrender of Charlestown, and the defeats and dispersion of the rebel-forces, it is become unnecessary that such paroles should be any longer observed j and it is fit and proper that all persons should take an aCtive part in settling and fecu-ringhis Majesty *s government, and delivering the country from that anarchy which for some time past hath prevailed: I do therefore iflbe this my proclamation, to declare, That all the inhabitants of this province who are now prisoners upon parole, and were not in the military line, (those who were in Fort Moultrie and Charlestown at the times of their capitulation and surrender, or were then in actual confinement, excepted), that from and after the 20th day of Juneinstant they are freed and exempted from all such paroles, and may hold thcmfclvea as restored to all the rights and duties belonging to citizens and inhabitants. And all persons under the description before mentioned, who shall afterwards nc-gleet to return to their allegiance, and to his Majesty’s government, will be con-2 B tidcred

u To their Excellencies, Sir Henry Clinton, Knight of the Bath, General of his Majesty’s Forces, and Mariot Ar-buthoot, Esq; Vice-Admiral of the Blue, his Majesty’s Commissioners to restore peace and good government in the several Colonies in rebellion in North America,

The Humble Address of divers Inhabitants of Charlestown.

The inhabitants of Charlestown, by the articles of capitulation, are declared prisoners on parole; but we the underwritten, having every inducement to return to our allegiance, and ardently hoping speedily to be readmitted to the character and condition of Brkifh subjects, take this opportunity of tendering to your Excellencies our warmest congratulations on the restoration of this capital and province to their political connection with the crown and government of Great Britain; an event which will add lultre to your Excellencies characters, and, we trult, entitle you to the most distinguishing mark of the Royal favour.

Although the right of taxing America in parliament, excited considerable ferments in the minds of the people of this province; yet it may with a religious adherence to truth be affirmed, that they did not entertain the most distant thought of dissolving the union that so happily subsisted between them and their parent-country; and when, in the progrels of that fatal controversy, the doctrine of independency (which originated in the more northern colonies) made its appearance among us, our natures revolted at the idea; and we look back, with the moll painful regret on those convul-fions that gave exigence to a power of subverting a constitution, for which we always had, and ever shall retain, the most profound veneration; and substitu-. ting in its Head a rank democracy, which, however carefully digested in theory, on being reduced into practice has exhibited a system of tyrannic domination, only to be foqnd among the uncivilized part

of mankind, or in the history of the dark and barbarous ages of antiquity.

We fmcerely lament, that after the repeal of those statutes which gave rife to the troubles in America, the overtures made by his Majesty’s commissioners, from time to time, were not regarded by our late rulers. To this fatal inattention are to be attributed those calamities which have involved our country in a state of misery and ruin: from which, however, we trust it will soone-merge, by the wisdom and clemency of his Majesty’s auspicious government, and the influence of prudential laws, adapted to the nature of the evils we labour under; and that the people will be restored to those privileges, in the enjoyment whereof their former felicity confided.

Animated with these hopes, we intreat your Excellencies interpofttion, in aflu-ring his Majesty, that we shall glory in every occasion of manifesting that zeal and affection for his person and government -with which gratitude can inspire a free and joyful people.

CharUjlown, June 5. 1780.

[Signed by 210 of the principal inhabitants.]

Gen. Clinton, who was preparing to fail from Charlestown for New York on the 4th of June [301.], arrived at the last-named place on Saturday, June 17. under convoy of Adm. Arbuthnot and the fleet, to the great joy of the inhabitants. The troops his Excellency took with him, were the light infantry, British grenadiers, 4ad foot, Queen’s rangers, and Hessian grenadiers, in all about 5500 men.

A letter from Charlestown, dated, June 8. fays, “ On the 6th an express arrived here from Lord Cornwallis, desiring coarse Irish (heeting for frocks for 750 men, who had come fromr North Carolina, and joined him, which made the amount of his army 5200. He was to march in a few days for North Carolina.”

A garrison of 3000 men was left at Charlestown.

“ Sir, The Congress have received authentic information, that his Most Christian Majesty is preparing to fend a powerful naval and land force to fomepart of the Continent of North America. This force, generously calculated either to

produce a diversion in our favour, or to forward the operation of our arms, by being directed to the fame object, may either, by our exertions, be made the means of delivering our country in the course of the campaign from the ravages of war, or, being rendered ineffectual through our iupinenefs, serve only to fully the reputation of our arms, to defeat the benevolent intention of our great ally, and to disgrace our confederacy in the eyes of Europe.

Every State that reflects upon the de-pretiation of the currency, and their own deficiency in the payment of their taxes, must necessarily conclude, that the treasury is exhausted. The military departments are at a itand for want of money to put them in motion. The Congress have no resources, but in your spirit and virtue; upon this they confidently rely. You know the value of the prize for which you contend; nor need you be informed bow much you are interested in a fptedy termination of this distreff-ing and expensive war.

The general assembly and council of Pennsylvania have published the following resolutions.

tf In Generalrfjftmbly of ,

Thurfdiv, June I. Whereas the exigencies which may arise in a (late of war, arc frequency of a nature that require such sudden and extraordinary exertions, as arc impossible for the legilla-tive body to provide for by the ordinary course of law, therefore resolved unanimously, That during the recess of this House, should the circumstances of the war make it necessary, the President or Vice-President in Council be authorised and impowered, and they are hereby authorised and impowered, to declare martial law, so far as the fame may be conducive to the public security, and to the safety and defence of the good and faithful citizens of this commonwealth.’*

“ In Council, Philadelphia, June 6. Whereas in the present ftatc of public affairs, it may be nee (T.iry 10 make extraordinary exertions for the supply of the a:ny, and supporting other measures which may be adopted for the safety and security of the State in it* freedom and independence; and it will be just and

reasonable, that, in the execution of such measures, a diferimination should be made between those who have manifested their attachment to their country, in the present contest with Great Britain, and those of a contrary character t whereupon,

Resolved, That it be recommended to all persons who have ihewn their allegiance to the State, by taking an oath or affirmation of fidelity to it, and of their abjuration of the King of Great Britain, to keep their certificates thereof ready to be produced on the (horteft notice; and to all persons who have lost their certificates, to obtain duplicates, or to give such assurances, so as to obtain certificates; or they may be in danger of loling the indulgence and distinction to which they are justly intitled.*

“ In Council, Philadelphia, July 6. Whereas the residence of the wives and children of those persons who have joined the enemy, has at all times proved inconvenient to the public interests, in consequence of the correspondence and intercourse which has been constantly preserved; and as it has become too dangerous to be longer permitted or connived at:

Resolved, That public notice be given to such persons, that they depart this State within ten days; and any of theirt remaining after that time, will not be deemed as intitled to any protection, but liable to be proceeded against as enemies of the State.”

o’clock p.«. The terms of capitulation I now inclose, which 1 hope will meet with your approbation.

I have also the honour of fending your Excellency, by Lieut. Thomas Moun-fey, the colours of the fort and of the regiment, as well as returns of the cannon and (lores taken in it.

When I came to Cape Gracios a Dios, there was not an Indian to be seen; fomc villains there had taken pains to persuade them, that the English army had come merely with an intent of enilaving them, and fending them to Jamaica; it was therefore some time before any of them ventured to come in J took the opportunity of fending them small presents by one of their people, who had ventured down to make observations on our motions. He being acquainted with Mr Campbell, was undeceived by him, and brought to me; which had the desired effect, as most of the tribes came in very ioon after.

Your Excellency’s letter of the 17th of March I received the 20th, just as 1 entered the river St John. I shall ever retain a grateful sense of the sentiments you. was therein pleased to express for me; and I am sorry that the many delays I met at the Cape, and other places between that and the harbour of St John, from the want of craft, and the back-wardnefs of the Indians in coming out, prevented my operations keeping pace with your Excellency’s expectations. I however hope you will do me the justice to bcKcVe, that no time was loft which could polfibly be saved, situated as I was. Jt was the 3d of March before any Black-river crafts arrived; and they were the only ones then provided. It is true the Indian governor promised a great many; but when I came to his country, there was not one ready; and we got them at last with a great deal of difficulty. The Superintendent was entirely deceived by the Indians in the number of crafts and men, and still more so in point of time.

Capt. Nclfon, then of the Hinchinbrooke, came up with thirty-four seamen, one serjeant, and twelve marines. I want words to express the obligations 1 owe that gentleman: he was the first 00 every service, whether by day or by night; there was scarcely a gun Bred but was pointed by him, or Lieut. Defpard, chief engineer, who has exerted himself of every occasion. I am persuaded, if our ihot had held out, we should have

had the fort a week sooner. As Capt. Nelson goes to Jamaica, he cata inform you of every delay, and point of service, as well as I could, for he knows my very thoughts.

The bearer Lieut. Mounfey can inform your Excellency of many things that may escape my memory; he is a very good officer, and commanded the party I sent to reconooitre the Look-out, and began the attack of it in concert with Capt. Deipard and Capt. Nelson, who with his seamen volunteered that duty.”

By the capitulation, which is dated April *9. the garrison were to be prisonera of war, to be conduced to some port of North America subject to the crown of Spain, and to give their parole not to bear arms against his Britannic Majesty till exchanged; the British forces were to be put in possession of the principal gate of the fort between the hours of four and five after noon, at which place centinels should be kept, to prevent the Indians committing any irregularities; and the garrison was to march out, colours flying, drums beating, lighted matches, muiket and fide arms, with two rounds each man, to the foot of the glacis, opposite the iouth front of the castle, where they should pile up their arms and accoutrements (the officers keeping their swords); the officers and soldiers were allowed to keep their baggage and money, but every slave to be detained for the use of his Britannic Majesty; and the garrison were to be allowed to carry with them all such ornaments and effects as were nec^fTary to their religion and mode of worship.

There were taken prisoners, i captain, and governor, i lieutenant, 2 fub*lieutenants, 1 captain of engineers, 1 chaplain, 1 surgeon, 3 serjeants, 3 drummers, 9 corporals, 17 soldiers of artillery, 1 cadet, 31 Spanifti soldiers, 61 soldiers of colour, 17 batteaux-men, 6 slaves, women and children, 3 malefactors, 17 women , 13 children, 1 mailer-carpenter, 1 carpenter, 1 blacksmith, 2 masons, 25 wood-cutters, 1 corporal of ditto, a officers and chaplains servants, 14 taken at the advance-post.

The British loss was 9 killed and 6 wounded, (none of them officers), of the 60th and 79th regiments, the Loyal Irish, and the Jamaica volunteers. — The garrison held out from the xath of April till the 29th.

Under an article, dated, Admiralty-office, July 29. we have an extract of a letter from Capt. John Harvey, of the Panther, to Mr Stephens, dated, Gibraltar bay, June 8. viz.

“ Nothing material has happened here necessary for their Lordships information, lince Com. Elliot failed, till the 7th instant; when, about one in the morning, his Majesty’s ship Enterprise, whose captain I directed to moor northward of the New Mole, to prevent any attack on the ships and vessels here, made the signal of an enemy’s approach. Seven ships or vessels were immediately perceived to be set on fire, and coming towards the New Mole. Some of those fireships drove; and the others were, by the boats of the Enterprise, St Fermin and ordnance-transports, all towed by two o’clock to the southward of the New Mole. The largest went on shore in Rofcia bay. Four of them drifting towards the Panther, lying off Rofcia, her boats were sent to grapple them; which they soon effe<5ted, and towed them on shore. At half pad two, discovering two vessels making towards the Panther, several guns, with round and grape ffiot, were fired at them; on which they were set on fire. The boats, without difficulty, in a little time, towed them clear: PPC of them, a brig, jrot on fhoic at

Europa point; the other, a ship, drove to sea. Though the enemy had the most favourable wind, weather, &c. for their purpose, you will please to acquaint their Lordships, not the lead damage has been done to the shipping, nor had we a fin-gle man hurt. A constant fire from all the ships and the garrison was kept up on the enemy’s gallies and boats, which were very numerous; but the darkness of the night prevented our discovering what execution was done. By what remains of the several vessels, and the species of stores and combustibles unconfu-med, it is evident no labour or expence has been spared in their equipment. The larged ship is about 1x7 feet in the keel, had two tier of ports, and was about the size of a fifty-gun ship. One ship appeared to be of about 300 tons, another of about 200, four brigs, and two small vessels.

By the flames of the fireships we observed several ships standing off and on the entrance of the bay, which at daylight we found to be the Spanish Rear-Adm. Barcelo, in a 0>ip of the line, with two frigates, and several xebecks and other armed vessels. They failed in the night from AIgtfir, with a view of intercepting any snip that might have been obliged to go out of the bay. A ship of ttie line likewise failed the day before from that place to the eastward, as is supposed with the fame design. In the morning the Spanish Admiral, with the frigates and xebecks, returned and anchored at Algcfir, where he now remains.

I cannot conclude my letter without-requesting that you will be further plea-fed to inform their Lordships, that the officers and company of his Majesty’s ship under my command behaved with the greatest steadiness and resolution, particularly the officers and people in the boats, who grappled and towed off the fireships, notwithstanding the frequent cxplolions of shells, 6cc. The behaviour of Capt. Leflic, the officers and company of his Majesty’s ship Enterprise, was equally exemplary, not only for their vigilance in discovering and keeping a good fire on the enemy, which in a great measure was the means of frustrating their attempt, but likewise for the vity of their officers and people in the boats, towing oft' the fireships, &c. Capt. Faulknor, of his Majesty’s floop St Fermin, then lying in the Mole, like-

A letter, dated, Gibraltar, June 7. gives an account of the Spanish and British naval force there when the above affair happened, viz. “ The Spanish naval force here is composed of five line-of-battle ships, three frigates, and five xe-becks, befidea their gallies and small craft; while ours coofifls only of the Panther of 60 guns, Gibraltar of 16, Enterprise 28, and the Fortune and St Permian, of 16. The two first are stationed off Bonayifta, for the laudable purpose of affitting vessels in getting in here, which Hervey is moil alert in doing, as often as in his power. The St Permian keeps in the New Mole to protect the Spanish prizes left here by the fleet; and the frigate and floop are, by Hervey’s express written orders, constantly anchored between the New Mole and Ragged Staff, without fide of all the other ships, for their protection, and to give the alarm in cases of danger.”

At five p. m. the Monarch made the signal to denote that (he passed the fternmost of the enemy’s ships without securing them; as soon afterwards did the Foil-droyant, and feme others of the head-most fiiips; and at the same time we could plainly discover from the Victory’s Mast-head, that they were nearly up with the rest of the enemy’s ships. Soon after seven a thick fog unfortunately came On, and I shortened fail in order to close with the ships nearest me, fleering the fame course under an cafy fail until daylight the morning after. I with piraCure acquaint their Lyrdships, that all the

ships have since rejoined me, except the Monarch and Defence, which I am informed were left in chace of the enemy’s ship of war, under whose protection the convoy had failed.

notwithstanding our Teeming fiiperi-ority, I hope the return of the killed and wounded will sufficiently evince, that my officers and ship’s company have acquitted themselves in 4hc most gallant and spirited manner. Indeed I feel it is impossible to do justice to their merits.

In justice to Lieut. Binks of the marines, I must beg leave to observe to their Lordships, that his party behaved with the utmost Itcadinefs and bravtry,keeping up a regular and constant fire from the beginning of the action, till necessity called them to the great guns, where they shewed an equal share of spirit and good order.

But while I am thus giving those well-deserved encomiums to his Britannic Majesty’s subjects, I should feel myself in honour bound to give his enemies, oil this occasion, the merits they are so truly deserving, did not the condition of their ship, and the heavy loss they have l'uftained, sufficiently speak their praises. 1 must beg leave to add, in honour of M. de Cherval, who commanded La Ca-pricieufe at the time ihc surrendertd, that the colours were not hauled down till the ship had five feet water in her hold. The freedom of the city of Corke has , been presented to the Earl of Hillfbo-rough and to Lord North, to each in a gold box. — On that of the Earl of Hillsborough, which was presented on the 13th of July, the inscription was, “ To the Earl of Hillsborough, the first act of whose miniltry completed what it had been the business of his life to promote, the happiness and prosperity of Ireland: The freedom of the city of Corke is presented as a tribute of national gratitude to the ornament and the pillar of his country.” — On that of the Lord North, which was presented on the sift of July, the inscription was, To Lord North, in whose administration, and by the exertion of whose abilities, commercial rights have been re- stored to this kingdom, on constitutional principles, and commercial favours bellowed of the utmost magnitude, the freedom of the city of Corke is presented as a tribute of gratitude to the pro-te<stor of the commerce, assertor of the liberties, the patriot and friend of Irb-1* AND.”

On Sunday, June 25. Lyon Michael, glass-cutter, a Jew, born in Germany, who has resided a good many years in Edinburgh, was publicly baptized, by Dr Andrew Hunter, Profeffor of Divinity, before a very crowded congregation, in the New Gravfriars church of that city. He is about thirty-two years of ace, and has been about four years married to a woman of this country, of ‘the religion of the dtablithed church. Though fcrongly attached to Jewifm at his marriage, he hn< for fometimepaft frequently attended divine worthip in the churches both of Glasgow and Edinburgh; so that his change of religion is rot a tiling done suddenly. By different conversations with him, Dr Hunter was fully convinced of his perfuation of the truth of the Christian religion, and attachment to its intcrells. ile (howed

the Doltor a confession of his faith, and signed it in his presence: and on being aiked, if it was his own compolition, he answered, that he had written it in Hebrew, and had got one to translate it for him into English. — Dr Hunter on this occasion preached from Alts ii. 41, first clause of the verse. Before the baptism he read Mr Michael’s confession of faith, and he gave the young Christian a suitable exhortation.—We have been favoured with a copy of the confession, and insert it, viz.

“ 1 Lyon Michael, a native of Pruf-fia, was born and bred a jew; and have lived, till of late, in the strictest conformity to their law. But, after mature deliberation within myself, I an now convinced, that the ceremonial law, and all its observances, are now quite done away: And I solemnly profess, before God and this congregation. That I believe with my heart, that Jesus Chrifk is the only Son of God, the true Mef-fiah; and believe, that he came into the world in the fullness of time, to take a* way fin, by the sacrifice of himself; and that he really suffered and died on the cross, was buried, and rose again from, the dead, and aicended into heaven; where he (till continues to make inter-eession for his people. And I likewise believe, that he is God and man in one person; and that he will come again, in the end of time, to judge the world; when he will reward the just, and eternally punish the wicked.-Being the re-

fore deeply convinced of these truths from the Sacred Scriptures, I hereby, in pretence of the Almighty God, and this people, solemnly renounce my former profrifion of Jewifm, and cordially embrace Christianity, as the true religion; and will, in a humble dependence upon divine grace, endeavour to regulate my conduct by its precepts to my life’s end.

LyonMichael.”

In the end of June an advertisement appeared in the Edinburgh papers, for a meeting of persons qualified to serve as jurymen in the judiciary or circuit courts, to be held in the parliament-house, Edinburgh, on the ift of August, to take under consideration the Litter to Lord Brax-field and to concert measures for

Charles Cox, a soldier in the 31st foot, who was sentenced by the high court of justiciary to be hanged on the 24th of May, in Edinburgh, for (hop-breaking, but was recommended to mercy by the jury, has got a pardon.

On Wednesday, May 17. sixty pipes of French brandy were imported at the Broomielaw, Glasgow, out of the Triton, Thomas Martindale master, from Dublin, for which the duties were paid at the custom-house and excise-office in Glasgow. This is the first importation at that place, it being now a port independent from that of Port-Glasgow.

About two o'clock in the morning of Friday, May 5. the theatre in Glasgow was discovered to be on fire. It was near three quarters of an hour before a-«y material assistance could be procured, and the inside of the building, together with the Wardrobe, Scenery, &c. were entirely consumed.

“ Edinburgh, April 22. It is with pleasure we can inform the public, that the great canal, from the baton, as far as Auchintarrie bridge eastward, is comprehended within the district of Glasgow. Entries for good9 to and from foreign parts, are now admitted at the custom-house there, and officers appointed to the shipping at the Broomielaw and Hamilton-hill. This privilege will prove of great utility, both to the coasting and foreign trade.”

“ London, July 31. The QueeD has

been pleased to settle a pension of 2001. per annum on Dr Hugh Blair, one of the Ministers of Edinburgh, as a mark of the pleasure her Majesty received in perusing the sermons lately published by that Rev. gentleman.” [108.]

Art account of the manner in which

fians treat persons aff by the fumes of

burnitir charcoal%

In a letter from Dr Guthrie of Petersburg, to !)r Prif/lley.

From the Philosophical W.

69. port a. 1779.

Uflian houses arc heated by the means of ovens; and the manner of heating them is as follows. A number of billets of wood are placed in the perch or stove, and allowed to burn till they fall in a jnafs of bright red cinders; then the vent above is lliut up, and likewise the door of the pcech, which optns into the room, in order to concentrate the heat. This makes the tile9 of which the peech is

Vol.XLII.

uy.

R

composed as hot as you desire, and fufli-ciently warms the apartment. But some-» times a servant i3 so negligent as to (hut lip the perch or oven before the wood is sufficiently burnt: for the red cinders should be turned over from time to time to fee that no bit of wood remain of a blackifii colour, but that the whole maf* is of an uniform glare (as if almoit transparent) before the openings are ihut; else the ugar or vapour is lure to succeed to mismanagement of this fort. And its effects are as follows.

If a person lays himself down to sleep in the room exposed to the influence of this vapour, he falls into so found a 11. t p that it is difficult to awake him, but be feels (or is sensible of) nothing. There is no spasm excited in the

3 D or

394 Guthrie on Fumes. Number of Papists inquired into. VoL 42J

or lungs to rouse him; nor does the breathing, by all accounts, seem to be particularly affected; iq (hert, there is no one symptom of suffocation: but towards the end of the catastrophe, a fort of groaning is heard by -people in the next room, which brings them sometimes to the relief of the sufferer. If a person onty fits down in the room, without intention to Deep, he is, after some time, seized with a drowsiness, and inclination to vomit. However, this I aft symptom seldom affects a Ruffian; it is chieAy foreigners who are awaked to their dangers by a nausea: but the natives, io common with strangers, perceive a duH pain in their heads; and if they do not remove directly, which they are often too sleepy to do, are soon deprived of their senses, and power of motion, info-mnch that if no person fortunately dif-dover then* within an hour after this worlt stage, they are irrecoverably loft: for the Ruffians say, that they do not succeed in restoring to life those who have lain more than an hour in a state of infen Ability.

- The recovery is always attempted, and often efi'e&ed, in this manner. They carry the patient immediately out of doors, and lay him upon the snow, with nothing on him but a fhtrt ind linen drawers. His ftomfleh and temples are then well rubbed with snow; and cold water, or milk, is poured down his throat. This friction is continued with fresh snow until the livid hue, which the body had when brought out, is changed to its natural colour, ;ind life renewed.1 Then they cure the violent head-ach which remains, by binding on the forehead a cataplasm of black-rye bread, and vinegar.

In this manner the unfortunate man is perfectly restored, without blowing up the lungs, as is necessary in the case of drowned per Ions; on the contrary, they begin to play of themselves ft> soon as the surcharge of phlogiston makes its escape from the body.

It is well worthy of observation, how diametrically opposite the modes are of restoring to life those who are deprived of it by water, and those who have loft it by the fumes of charcoal; the one confiding in the internal and external application of heat, and the other in that of cold. It may be alleged, that the ftimu-Jus of the cold produces heat; and the fact seems to be confirmed by the Kuffiah method of restoring circulation iu a fro-

zen limb by means of friction with snow. But what is Angular irt thr oafe of people apparently deprived of lifd in the manner treated of, is, that the body is much warmer when brought out of the room than at the in ft ant life restored, and that they awake cotd anti thivering. The colour of the body is also changed from a livid red to its natural complexion; which, together with forte other crrciifli-stances, would almost lead one to suspect, that they arc restored to life by the snow and cold water fomchow or other freeing them from the load of phlogiftog with which the system teems to be replete; for although the first applicatios of cold water to the human body produces heat, yet, if often repeated m a very cold atmosphere, it then cools ki-stead of continuing to heat, just as the cold bath does when a person remains loo long in it.

Rev. Sir,

HP hi E Lord Archbishop of Canterbury * has communicated to me the purport of a letter which he received from Lord Vitcount Stormont, one of his Majesty’s principal secretaries of state, Agnifying to him his Majesty's pleasure, in consequence of a» addreis prelected to his Majesty by the Bouse of Lords in July last, that his Gtace /hould give directions to all the Biftiops, his suffragans, to procure from rhe parochial clergy in their respective dioceses, an account of the Papists, or reputed Papists, withia their several pariffiesj and that tb<y should have the fame ready to be laid before the House of Lords on the first day of the next session of parliament. [371.]

I do therefore hereby require you to prepare such a list as far as relates your parish, and fend it to me in St James’s square, on or before the 10th day of October next; and if there /hould be arty Papists or reputed Papilts in your parish, you will likewise inform me of it by the time above mentioned. And I delire you also to inquire, whether there is any Popish school or schools within your parish; and acquaint me, whether any; and what number of children of Protestant parents ate taught therein.

I am. Rev. Sir, your afe&ionate brother and humble fervanf, -R. Londox.

Aug. 1780. . An account of the tumult9 in London, &c.: 401

Account of the tumults, concluded, [362.]

Our last ended with the occurrences of Wednesday, June 7.; in the evening of which day, the military, by order of the king and council, began to act without waiting for an order from the civil magistrate; by which the mob was soon discom sited.

On Thorfday, June 8. in the morning,

several hundreds of the iron hoops of the calks that had been burnt the preceding night, were lying opposite Mr Lang* dale’s upper house. In this house, 90,000 gallons of spirits were destroyed, and in the lower house 30,000.

The following hand-bill was circulated.

“ It is earnelflly requested of all peaceable and well-disposed persons, as well Protestants associated as others, that they will abstain from wearing blue cockades: as these ensigns are now afiumed by a set of mifereants, whose purpose it is to bum this city, and plunder its inhabitants; and who wifti, by distributing among better-disposed persons, and prevailing on them to wear those marks and distinctions, to fereen themselves from the detection and punishment due to their enormous crimes. And it is further recommended to all tradesmen and mailers of families, not to employ or re-taip in their service any persons who distinguisii themselves by wearing blue cock-

ades.”

About eleven o’clock, a great number of people went to the Marilialfea, and begun to pull it down; but before they could set fire to it, a military force arrived, and prevented their effecting their purpose. It is said that near thirty persons were killed on this occasion.

Between two and three, a fellow with a blue cockade went into a house in Bi-fhopfgate street, and with a drawn knife in his hand, demanded money, swearing that the house should be pulled down if the reoueft was not granted. He was vnftantly secured, and brought to the Poultry compter.

A young chimney-sweeper headed about twenty of the rioters, and going into an eminent house in Newgate-street, demanded to fee the religious books. They were accordingly produced; and, to the aftoniihment of the gentleman and his family# this footy hero turned the Bible upside.down, <and seeming to read, laid, “ it was the right religion, — it . Vol.XLIL.

was Lord Gordon’s religion, by God ! ”

In the afternoon, about four o’clock, a party of the mob got through the ruins of the Fleet prison, and attempted to set fire to some houses adjoining; but a large party of the military arriving, surrounded the prison, and sent in a detachment to drive them out; while the party at the door knocked down and wounded most of them with their bayonets as they ran off.

The gentlemen in the borough armed themselves, and attacked a large party of the insurgents in Tooley street, routed them, wounded several of the ringleaders, and took numbers prisoners.

All this day numbers of gentlemen, with the military, were employed in pulling the blue cockades from the hats of c-very person they met. It was now as unsafe to be seen with these emblems of rebellion, as it was unsafe during some of the preceding daye to be seen without them.

Seventy of the rioters were taken among the ruins of Newgate in the afternoon. The fire had broke out again: whether they had kindled it anew or not, is uncertain.

The lituation of Lord Rockingham’s house in Grosvenorsquare carried with it every appearance of its being a feat of war. Every front-room of the house was full of soldiers, prepared for the reception of the rioters; and his Lordship’s stables were turned into barracks, in which troops of cavalry were continually in readiness for action.

All the barristers of the several Inns of Court armed themselves for their own defence. The Attorney - General and Mr Mansfield were the first promoters of this spirited plan; which has been honoured with his Majesty’s approbation.

The civil officers, aided by tin. military, all this day patrolled the streets, and surrounded and fearehed all the houses and lanes noted for the resort of disorderly people, in order to apprehend the felons escaped from the' several jails. Many were retaken.

In the evening the following hand-bill was circulated.

“ NO FRENCH RIOTERS.

This is to give notice, that it now ap-. pears, that the horrible riots which have been committed in this city, have been promoted by French money; and to call upon all honest men to (land forth against rioters, who, under the cloak of reii-3 E gion.

L.

An account of the tumults in London, &c. V0I.4S.

gion, arc wantonly destroying our property, and endeavouring to overset our happy constitution. If the French are fullered, by these means, to prevail. Popery will certainly be introduced; which we have no reason to fear from a British parliament.*

The House of Lords met this day, and adjourned to Monday June 19.— The Earl of Abingdon had prepared a speech, but the House was adjourned before he came.—The speech is publish-cd, viz.

“ My Lords,

I do not rife to trespass on your Lordships time. I know the hour is come, when to ad, and not to debate, is the business of this House. But, my Lords, I rife most earnestly to intreat and implore your Lordships to stop the effufion of human blood, and to rdtore quiet to this kingdom, by repealing the act of parliament that has given occasion to our present anarchy and confusion. Let me conjure you, my Lords, in your wisdom, to do this: 1 lay, in your wisdom; for, my Lords, supposing the ad ever so just, suppose it ever so right, have you power to maintain it ? is your force equal to this ? It is not. But if it . was, do you think your soldiers would fight for you in such a cause ? If they do not, my Lords, what is to become of our country ? Let me conjure you, my Lords, for justice fake, to repeal this law; I say, for justice fake. It was held out as a law for Scotland; and one of your secretaries of state took upon himself to answer for the legislature, and to say that it should not extend to Scotland [41*109.]: and if Scotland is to have this indulgence, why is it to be refused to the people of England ? My Lords, I could multiply arguments without end, to induce your Lordships to a concurrence in opinion with me. But, as I said before, this is not a time for debate. There is one argument, however, which I must mention: I fear, my Lords, that there is, at the bottom of all this disturbance, the deep-laid scheme of a dark cabal to dissolve the constitution of this country, and to establish a military government upon its ruins. Let us, my Lords, defeat the purposes of those devils incarnate, and by giving peace to the people, restore the constitution to itself.

My Lords, I shall therefore move your Lordships, That leave be given for bring-

ing in a bill, for the purpose of repealing the ad of the 18th George III. intitled,

“ An act for relieving his Majesty’s subjects professing the Popish religion, from certain penalties and disabilities imposed on them by an act made in the nth and nth years of the reign of King William 111. in titled, /ftt ait further pre

venting the gr truth of and that

the fame be brought in immediately.*’

The Speaker of the House of Commons, attended by a sufficient number of members to authorise his taking the chair, went down to Westminster so early as twelve o’clock, and opened the House. — The Lord Advocate rose immediately: and after expatiating on the I impropriety of proceeding to business in the present di(traded state of affairs, be j moved, That the House do adjourn to Monday the 19th inltant. Which was agreed to ntm. con.

The arrangement that had been made of the military this day produced fogood an etTcd, that there was no riot or disturbance in the course of the night.

On Friday, June 9. peace and tran- 1 quillity were so far restored, that men 1 had leisure to refled on the horrors to which they had been exposed, with temper and coolness; and returned to bu-finefs, which had been suspended for

some time.----- In order to dissipate the

idea that the rioters were to be tried by martial law, the following hand-bill was circulated in every quarter of the town.

“ Whereas some ill-designing and malicious persons have published, for the purpose of disquieting the minds of his Majesty’s faithful subjects, that it is intended to try the prisoners now in custody by martial law; notice is given, by authority, that no such purpose or intention has ever been in the contemplation of government; but that the said prisoners will be tried by the due course of law, as expeditiously as may be.”

At eleven o’clock a council was held at Lord Stormont’s office. It broke up at one o’clock; when the Lords in administration went to St James’s, where the King had a levee. His Majesty retired to the closet soon after two; where a cabinet-council was convened, to take into their serious consideration the most effectual means of securing the promoters of the late alarming commotions.

In consequence of the above council, a 1 warrant was issued by the Secretaries of ' Suite, directed to Mann and Staley,

two

Aog. 1780. An account of the

two of his Majesty’s mcfiengers in ordinary, for apprehending Lord George Gordon. The messengers instantly repaired to his house in Welbeck street, and were introduced to his Lord ship; whom they made acquainted with the nature of their visit. Lord George replied, — “ if you are sure it is me you want, I am ready to attend you.” Upon which, a hackney-coach being previouf-ly got ready, and a party of the light horse having received orders to attend in an adjacent street, his Lordship was conducted safely by them, about fix o’ dock, to the Horse-guards. The inhabitants of Welbeck-street collected about the carriage, and inveighed against him bitterly, as the author of all the present calamities; to avoid which reproaches, he desired the blinds to be drawn up; which requestthe messengers complied with. A long examination took place in the war-office before the Lord President, Lord North, Lord Am-herft, the Secretaries of State, and several other Lords of the Privy Council; and at half an hour after nine. Lord George was committed a close prisoner to the Tower. The guards that attended him were by far the great eft in number ever remembered to guard a ftatc-prisoner: A large party of infantry preceded in front; his Lordship following in a coach, in which were two officers; two soldiers rode behind the coach; and immediately followed Gen. Carpenter’s regiment of dragoons; after which came a colonel’s guard of the foot-guards; besides a party of the militia, which marched on each fide of the coach. The cavalcadepafled over Westminster bridge, through St George’s fields, the Borough, and so on to the Tower; where his Lord-fiiip alighted, seemingly in good spirits, about ten o’clock. His LortUhip rested the first night in the Governor’s apartments. The populace who attended on this occasion conducted themselves with singular decorum.——The commitment, and the order to the gaoler and two wardens, follow, viz.

The commitment.

“Thefe are, in his Majesty’s name, toauthorise and require you, to receive into your custody, the body of the Rt Hon. George Gordon, Esq; commonly called Lord George Gordon, herewith sent you for high treason; and you are to kap him late and close, until he

shall be delivered by due course of law. And for so doing this shall be your warrant.

Given at Whitehall, the 9th of June 1780.

Stormont. Hillsborough. To the Lieutenant of the Tower

of London, or his Deputy.”

The orders to the Gaoler and

1. The wardens appointed to keep a close prisoner, shall not presume to leave him a moment alone, either night or day; or to change their duty with other wardens, but by order of the constable, lieutenant, deputy-lieutenant, or major of the Tower.

a. There must be no pen, ink, of paper, brought in to the prisoner, nor he suffered to ufc any, or receive any paper, or books of any kind, until they are examined by some one of the officers above mentioned.

3. They are to permit no person to have admittance into the room he is confined in, or to speak to him; nor any cloaths, linen or woollen, or any other thing whatsoever, to be carried out or brought in to the prisoner, until they are examined by the wardens before the gentleman-gaoler; who are carefully to fee that no arms, money, or writing whatsoever, be concealed in them. If any such are found, a report to be immediately made of it, as also of any other thing that may happen extraordinary, to such of the Tower-officers as shall at that time command within the Tower.

4. The wardens must not suffer the prisoner to walk in any other apartment of the house, or to come out of his room; and if any other person or persona shall attempt to speak to the prisoner, either at the window or any other way, the wardens shall immediately call the guard to apprehend them, until further orders from the officer commanding in the Tower.

5. If the gentleman-gaoler shall ne-gled to fee the prisoner, either when he locks up at night, or when he unlocks in the morning, the wardens shall be obliged to make a report of such neglect, to such of the Tower-officers as shall then command in the Tower.

б. The wardens, therefore, with the gentleman-gaoler, are every ni^fit and morning to examine all parts of the pri-

foner’s room, to fee that no alterations are made at the windows, or door, or locks, or bolts withinfide, or bars forced, or walls broken down, or any thing else attempted, that might favour an escape, or look like a correspondence abroad; of all which they shall immediately give notice to the officer commanding in the Tower.

Tower, June 9. 1780.”

The circumstance that first induced administration to take Lord George into custody is said to have been this. In the heat of his too successful enthusiasm, he wrote a letter, which he sent for insertion to the conductor of a morning-publication, add re fifed to his religious af-fociatcs, wherein he recommended to them to nouriffi the noble spirit that had so laudably taken pofiTession of them; and told them, that he did not in the smallest degree doubt, that an unlimited compliance with all their requisitions would be the undoubted consequence of their perseverance. He at the same time indeed annexed a kind of exhortation for the preservation of peace and good order; but as this concluding suggestion was too repugnant to the general tenor of the epistle, and also far too faintly urged to produce an adequate effect, the printer deemed it the best step he could take for the welfare and quiet of his country, to fend this letter to government; which he accordingly did, in a note addressed to Lord Hilifborough. His Lordship, on the receipt of the letter, caused a council to be convened; before whom he produced it. The unanimous opinion they held upon it was, that it was of a very inflammatory tendency, and that the author was undoubtedly amenable to the laws as the promoter of a traitorous ledition. As a farther fanCtion, however, for the measures they intended to adopt towards him, an order was immediately ifl'ued to the pott-office, enjoining all letters franked G. Gordon, to whateverquarterthey might be addressed, to be from that time detained, and transmitted to the office of one of the secretaries of state. In consequence of this mandate, several letters, written by the deluded young nobleman, were (topped, and afterwards laid before the cabinet. Molt of these were directed to Scotland, and were replete with observations equally subversive of order, religion, and liberty. He gave a faithful narrative of the transactions which

had taken place in the metropolis; and instead of accompanying the relation with those expressions of horror and disapprobation which must have actuated every good and well-disposed citizen, be seemed rather to exult in the event, as nothing pervaded these cpiftles but rapturous encomiums on the “ glorious cause,” and extravagant panegyrics on the noble spirit displayed by his biethrt* in the defence of it.—-Besides letters addressed to or franked by Lord George Gordop, a great number of intercepted letters, that had been (topped at the post-office and other places, belonging to foreign agents, were carrried to the offices of the secretaries of (late for examination.

A third royal proclamation was this

day ifl'ued, viz.

“ George R.

Whereas a great number of disorderly persons have for several days past assembled themselves together, in a riotous and tumultuous manner, and have been guilty of many acts of treason, rebellion, and felony, having formed designs to break open and dertroy the gaols and prisons, and to set loose the prisoners confined therein, and having actually broke into and let on fire the gaol of Newgate, the king’s-bench prison, and the prison of the Fleet, and released the prisoners, and made attempts on other gaols and prisons in and about this metropolis; and having set fire to and pulled down the dwelling houses of divers-of our peaceable subjects. and also set fire to, burnt and destroyed the materials thereof, and the furniture and other effects found therein; and also taken money from divers of our subjects: We therefore, taking the fame into our most lerious consideration, have thought fit, by and with the advice of our privy council, to pubhfli and declare our rc-solution, That the authors, abettors, and perpetrators of the said offences be prosecuted as expeditiously as may be. according to the due course of law; and we do hereby enjoin and require all ju* dices of the peace, ffieriffs, mayors, bailiffs, courtabies, and all other our loving subjects, to be aiding and assisting to the utmolt of their power, in detecting, apprehending, and bringing to justice the said offenders. And we are gracioufty pleased to promise, that if any person shall discover any other person or persons who are guilty of any of the said

offences.

offences, so as that the person or persons clifcovered may be prosecuted for the fame, such discoverer shall have and receive, as a reward, upon conviction of such offender or offenders, the sum of Jifly p rands, and also our gracious pardon for the said offence, in case the person making such discovery shall himself be liable to be prosecuted for the fame: and the commissioner8 for executing the office of Trcafurer of our Exchequer are hereby required to m*ke payment accordingly of the said rewards.’* Dated June 9.

This day the (hops in the city were opened as usual; business in the courts in Westminster hall, which had been suspended, was resumed; many stories were propagated which time has since discovered to be groundless; and the ptirenzy that had possessed the people, terminated by strengthening the hands of government.

In the London papers of June 17. there is a copy of the return made to Lord Amhcrft, of the number killed and wounded in the late disturbances, viz. Killed by the association and guards 109, and by the light horse iot, is aio, and died in hospitals 75, in all 285; and pri-fbners then under cure 173. Total killed and wounded 458.

To the preceding narrative we shall add remarks selected from the papers by the compiler of the Gentleman’s Magazine, viz.

“ 1. A set of well-meaning men, who could not have been aware of the consequences, met for the defence of the elta-blished religion, and called themselves the Prolijlant /fficiation.

2. In con feq tie nee cf this association, Lord George Gordon called a public meeting to present a petition to parliament, profcffcdly with a good intention.

3. As no proper police exists in this country, the ndvertisement issued for such purpose had a direct tendency to collect thieves, pickpockets, housebreak-ers, See.

4. Such an assembly (not the affocia-tors themselves) being unwilling, in the first instance, to manifest their real intentions, attacked such places as rather led the public off their guard; but the moment they found themselves formidable, depredations were committed upon the private property of individuals, magistrates, See.while the bank itself was nut forgotten.

5. If any regular plan for burning the city, or ruining the nation, had beetl previously concerted, common fenfc would have dictated, that an attempt upon the bank should have been the first ouvert act, before the burning of Newgate, when violence raged in its fury, and when it met with no oppofitioru That there was no previous plan, is obvious to common ftnfe; because it i«. not so much as pretended that there ever was any private meetings held by the association; nor is there the least shadow, of reason, from any thing that has appeared upon the trials of the rioters; to suppose that there ever was. Tbetr meetings were as public as the meeting* at Ranelagh, or any other place of entertainment, and the object they had m view as publicly recommended as the interest of religion is at church. Did this look like a plot to rob the bank and burn the city ? Surely he muff be a credulous man indeed to believe in a plot., where, to make it so, they could only find one conspirator

6. If there had been any ringleaders of nfote, even behind the curtain, the trial* of those who have been condemned as moll active in the riot would certainly have led to a discovery; but we find, that a blind and ignorant fury, inflamed by drnnkennefs, and seconded by the in-teiefted views of thieves and housebreak-ers, has, in fact, been the cause of the whole commotion.”

Quiet was now reffored in London and its environs; but a diffurbance of the like kind broke out at Bath; which, however, was happily soon quaffied. — In the evening of Friday, June 9. a footman and some boys began the mifehiefy by breaking the windows of a house where the Roman-Catholic priest resided, adjoining to a new Romiffi chapel lately, built. At night they were joined by great numbers, most of them strangers; who broke open the chapel-doors, and began to throw the materials out at the windows. The magistrates and peace-’ officers assembled as soon as possible; but ere they could exert themselves, the mob had increased to a great multitude. The riot-act was read, and some persons were seized, but instanfly refeued. The magistrates and many respectable citizen* endeavoured to prevail on the mob to; disperse, but without effect. Major-Molesworth, with a few of the city-volunteers hastily colkdtdd*, got into the'

chapel.

chapel, and put out the fire several times; which the mob repeatedly kindled. About twenty more of the volunteers were soon after got together; and Capt. Du-perre, at the requell of the mayor, headed them, and led them into the chapel, their pieces not loaded. The instant they entered the chapel, the mob rulhed in upon them on all lides, and a pistol was fired at Capt. Duperrc; which fortunately mifled him, and as fortunately killed an old rioter, who had been once before wounded at an iufurreltton at Trowbridge. But this so incer.fed the mob, who supposed the man to be (hot by one of the volunteers, that they immediately set fire to the chapel: and the volunteers, finding it impossible to rdift so large a body, made a llovv and good retreat. The chapel, and lixorseven hou-fes that surrounded it, were er.tirtly burnt by four in the morning. The mob were then prevailed on to difpcrfe, without carrying into execution the remainder of their diabolical plan; for they had declared their resolution to burn the old chapel, and the houses of several Roman -Catholics refidirg at Bath. Exprefies had been sent to Wells, Devizes, &c. for troops; and Maj. Mallock, of the Queen’s dragoons, arrived, with about 4ohorfe, before five next morning; Capt. Taylor, with 60 more of the fame regiment, before seven, from Devizes; and Capt. Barnaby, with about 240 of the Hereford militia, by nine o’clock, from Wells. Quiet was immediately restored. The gutting and firing of the chapel was executed with amazing haste and regularity; and not a single person was infulled, except those who attempted to seize the rioters. It was the general opinion, that the leaders in the riot were persons sent from London.

At London the magistrates, &c. were now busied in discovering and apprehending the persons guilty of the late atrocious riots. Parties of the military, accompanied by Sir John Fielding’s men, and directed by them, began, on Thursday, the 8th, to search the old haunts of those incorrigible criminals who but a day or two before had been released from confinement; and there found numbers of them, ready to pursue their former courses, or to return to the dungeons from whence they bad been delivered. It is inciedible with what facility almost the whole body of these delinquents were in a few days apprehended.

O11 Saturday, June 10. about five { clock in the morning, Mr Fisher, the secretary to the Protestant Association, whs apprehended, at his house, and carried to the Tower. In the evening he was examined before the privy council; and nothing appearing against him, the committee, or the association, he was honourably difniifled. Mr Hopkins, one of the managers of the association, was likewise taken up, examined, and dismissed.

This evening a deputation of the committee of the association waited on Lord North, offering, if they had done any thing contrary to law, to stand a trial His Lordship received them politely, 1 and told them, that no charge whatever was detigned to be brought against the secretary, the committee, or the alfoci-ation.— They shewed his Lordship a copy of a letter deflgned to be printed, which his Lordship approved of — Of this letter many thousands were distributed. It was dated next day, which was Sunday; and wc insert a copy of it, viz.

“ Sir, Loud.mt 11.1780.

The Protestant Association has no connexion, dircvtly nor indirectly, with these law Ids

The Lord Mayor laid before a court of Aldermen held on Saturday June 10. the following letter.

“ After our hearty commendations to your Lordship,—

Whereas great numbers of disorderly persons, armed with guns, piltols, and other offienfive weapons, have affictnbled themselves for feme nights palt, and have committed divers the most daring and violent outrages, to the great terror of all his Majesty’s subje<Sts: And whereas there is reason to apprehend, that many of them Itill lie concealed within the cities of London and Westminster, and the liberties thereof, and within the borough of Southwark, in the county of Surrey, with intent to affiemble them-Cclves again, in order to proceed to the commission of the like a<5is of violence: And whereas we think it necessitry that the sheriffs of the city of London, and all other the civil magistrates and peace-officers within the said city, should be called upon at this time to exert their utmost diligence in the discharge of their respective duties, not only to suppress, but to prevent all riotous meetings and tumultuous assemblies: We do there-

fore, in his Majesty’s name, and by his expref3 command, hereby pray and require your Lordship, to call upon the Laid sheriffs of London, and all other the civil magistrates and peace officers of the city of London, and to signify to them his Majesty’s pleasure, that, taking with them such affiiflance as they shall judge necessiiry, they do cause the most strict and diligent search to be made for all i-dle and disorderly persons lurking with-in the city of London; and them having found, they are forthwith to apprehend and secure, that they may be dealt with according to law: and the sheriffs of the city of London, and all other the civil magistrates and peace-officers of the fafd city, are in like manner required to make the most diligent search, m all public houses, and other places within the city of London, where they may have reason to fufoeft that any guns, piltols, or other offensive weapons may be concealed, and to seize and secure the fame. And so not doubting of your ready compliance herewith, we bid your Lordship farewell.— From the Council-Chamber fit Whitehall, the 9th day of June 1780. [[Signed] Bathurst, P. Geo. Ger-main. Onslow. North. Edce-fUMBE. C.Spencer. Townshend.
