On this the court came to the following resolutions.

“ Resolved, That the several Aldermen of this city do immediately repair to their respective wards; and, calling together the peace-officers of each ward, with such other assistance as they may find neccessary, immediately issue warrants for searching for and securing all idle and disorderly persons, who shall be found lurking in their respetftive wards; and also for searching for and seizing all arms which shall be found in suspected places, or in the poffieflion of such idle and disorderly persons; and to cause diligent search to be forthwith made accordingly.

Relblved, That all persons keeping public houses within this city and the liberties thereof, do (hut up their houses at ten o'clock in the evening, and not fell any beer or other liquors after that time, till five o’clock in the ensuing morning. ”

On Friday, June 9. the following plan was sent by the Lord Mayor to the Aldermen and Deputies of the several wards, recommended to the general meeting of the house keepers. “ The Alderman or Deputy of each ward, and the several housekeepers and gentlemen being met, that they throw themselves into four divisions by ballot; each tfivi-.Son, with their arms, to take watch by notation. That the housektepers and gentlemen not on guard, have their arms xeady prepared, to procted on beat of drum to the head-quarters of the ward. Tat roles to be sent about the ward, in order to keep a watch over alehonfcs and disorderly places; also to prevent a-iiy number of people collecting together; to suffer no signs of countenancing the rioters to prevail, as wearing badges, huzzaing, and illuminating; to commit those who cannot give a good account of themselves, and take from them their weapons, See.; to have intelligence established with the neighbouring wards, and with the Mansion-house; and to have the fame with the military, if judged necessary; and a watch-word for the night settled among the commanders of the whole. To recommend to housekeepers, parents, and makers, to keep their Servants, children, and workmen at home, towards and during night, to prevent their being mixed with or countenancing the guilty by their numbers. To report every morning the proceedings of each ward to the Lord Mayor at the Mansion-house, that just information may be had, on which to found measures for the whole/*

Earl Bathurst to the Lord Major, Council-chamber, June 20.

“ I have been honoured with your Lordship’s letter of the 17th instant, de-firing a further explanation of the letters sent by Lord Amherst to CoL Twifleton-, See.and have taken the first opportunity of laying yourLordAiip’s said letter be* fore the council; and I am to say, that it iB the opinion of their Lprdships, that the matter has been folly explained in say letter to your Lordship of the 15th.

But although his Majesty’s Trotestant subjects may have arms for their defence suitable to their conditions, and as allowed by law; yet they cannot by law assemble in bodies armed, and be mustered and arrayed, without the authority of his Majesty.”

A fourth royal proclamation was issued, dated June ax. viz.

“ George R.

Whereas information has been received, that the late riots and turault9 in and about this metropolis were encouraged and abetted by money, or other rewards, given, or promiled to be given, to the persons concerned therein; and also bells, flags, and other (ignals and devices, were used during the said riots and tumults, to direct the rioters in their criminal acts and proceedings, and to give notice of attempts or preparations to flipprefs, refill, or interrupt the fame: We therefore, taking the fame into our most firious consideration, by and with the advice of our Privy Council, in order to detect and bring to justice the persons guilty of any of the criminal acts above mentioned, are graciously pleased to promise, Xh*t if any person shall difr cover any other person or persons, who, by themselves, or their agents, gave or offered, or promised. to give, any money, or other reward, to any person or persons, to engage or continue in the said riots or tumults,* or to do or assist it* any of the criminal acts committed dfij ring the continuance of the said riots and tumults, or who made pfe of any bells, flags, or other fig/ials or devices, to »li-red the rioters in any' of theft* criminal

acts and proceedings, or to give notice ! of attempts or preparations to suppress, refill, or interrupt the fame, so as that the person or persons discovered may be ' prosecuted, such discoverer shall nave and receive as a reward, upon conviction of such offender or offenders, the fam of

One hundred pounds; and the Commis* sioners for executing the office of Treasurer of our Exchequer are hereby required to make payment thereof accordingly;”

It was resolved in a court of Aldermen on Tucfdayj July 18. 14 That as the executions have passed with perfect peace and quiet, and there being no appearance of any riots within this city, no further allowance be made to the troops by this city after Saturday next.”

, The greater part of the military were removed from the city on the 25th of July, and the rest soon after.

The encampment in Hyde park broke up on the 10th of August.

As in the account of these tumults, we have related the proceedings in parliament each day before the adjournment, we shall here insert the proceedings concerning the tumults on the meeting of parliament.

Both Houses having met on Monday, June 19. the day to which they had adjourned, the King made the following speech to them.

“My Lords, and Gentlemen,

The outrages committed by bands of desperate and abandoned men, in various parts of this metropolis, broke forth with such violence into acts of felony and treason, had so far overborne all civil authority, and threatened so dircdly the immediate subversion of all legal power, the destruction of all property, and the confulion of every order in the state, that I found myself obliged, by every tie of duty and affection to my people, to suppress, in every parj, those rebellious in-: iurredions, and to provide for the public safety, by the most efiVdual and immediate application of the force intrusted to me by parliament.

I have directed copies of the prbcla-mations ifined upon that occasion to be laid before you.

Proper orders have been given for bringing the authors and abettor^ of these , in furred ions, and the perpetrators of such

prjm.injfl acts, to speedy trial, and to such t condign punishment as the laws of their

...country I

\Aug.1780. . Lord* fcn the tumults.

country prescribe, and as the vindication of public justice demands.

Though I trust it is not necessary, yet I think it right at this time, to renew to you my solemn a flu ranees, that I have no other object but to make the laws of the realm, and the principles of our excellent constitution in church and state, the rule and measure of my conduit; and I shall ever consider it as the first duty of my (lation, and the chief glory of my reign, to maintain and preserve the established religion of my kingdoms; and, as far as in me lies, to secure and to perpetuate the rights-and liberties of my people.”

After the King had withdrawn, the JDuke of Dorset recapitulated the several circumstances of the late dreadful riots; expatiated on the amiable virtues, great resolution, and admirable conduit of his Majesty, at that alarming crisis; and then moved an humble address, to thank his Majesty for his gracious speech, and to express the highelt approbation of that House, of all the measures taken to sup-dress the riots; which address was worded nearly in the fame terms as the speech.

Lord Dudley Ward seconded the motion, and followed the fame line of detail on the subject of the riots, and was equally warm in his commendations of the wisdom and lenity shewn by his Majesty on the occasion.

The Duke of Richmond rose, not to oppose the motion for an address, but to objelt to some parts of it, to which he could not so readily assent, unless he heard some further explanation of them. His Grace could not think, that an immediate application had been made of the force entrusted to his Majesty by parliament; neither could he so clearly fee the absolute ncccflily there was for using the military power; for if the magistrates had done their duty in time, the civil power might have been sufficient to have quelled the riots; but at all events the military came too late, and therefore it could not be said that immediate relief had been given to the subjelts in the hour of their greatest distress.

His Grace then adverted to the proclamations < n the table, which he said he ftiouid dtfire to have read; and v.ilhed to have it knov n, whether we are now under martial law, and how long the mir litary force i r. idvn und us: for,jn his opinion, purlia’.iKnt could hardly be said

to be free; while it was overawed by the near approach of such'an arnied force, it might be dangerous to speak with freedom, as he now intended to do, and to censure the conduit of the commander in chief of the army. Hie then took notice of two letters font by Lord Amherst into the city to Col, Twifleton, who commanded the military in the city, requiring him to dilhroi the citizens, who had armed for the defence of their lives and properties, and to order them to deliver up their firelocks; declaring it to \?t unlawful for them to appear in arms without a commission from the King, or his approbation.

These letters his Grace considered as a violation of the constitutional right that every Protestant subject has to keep and bear arms for the defence of his person and his property; and if the doltrinelaid down in those letters was once admitted, there was an end of our liberty. If these letters therefore were to be taken as part of the conduit of government, he should be sorry to have it go forth as the voice of the House that they approved it by an address of thanks: neither could he, confident with the principles which he had always adhered to, consent to approve the conduit of an administration which he had always difap-proved.

He had likewise, he said, had the mif-fortune to differ in opinion from the Noble Lord who has the command of the army, upon many occasions; and he owned he could not approve of these letters; copies of which his Grace had obtained, and thought they demanded the ft ri It eft parliamentary scrutiny.

Sir P. Jennings Clerke accused the ministry of having been extremely negligent in suppressing the tumults. Many of the buildings that now lay in ruins, might, he said, have been saved, if his information had been attended to He himself had waited twice on the commander in chief and others, and informed them of the danger with which the king's bench prifoo was threatened; but his information was disregarded, and the prison was reduced to ashes.

TThe Secretary at War acquitted the ministry of negligence; and attributed what was called indolence in them to the want of troops; for when the riot first began, and the apprehensions of individuals became violent, so many applications had been made for troops, that, previous to the arrival of the militia, it wasimpoffible to satisfy half the calls that had been made.

A motion was then made, that an bumble address be prefeoted to his Majesty, to return to his Majestjr the thanks of this House, for communication the reason for which the Rt Hon. Lord George Gordon, a member of this Hook, was apprehended and committed. — The motion was agreed to; and the add refs ordered to be presented to his Majesty by such of the members as were Privy Coua-fellors.

Both Lords and Commons, in their addresses in answer to the King’s speech, express their utmost abhorrence and de-teftation of the outrages committed, in various parts of the metropolis, by bands of desperate and abandoned men; — and both approve of the measures that

were taken to suppress them.----- The

Lords say, “We beg leave to testify oar warmest gratitude to your Majesty, for your parental care and concern for the protection of your subjects, so manifest in the measures your wisdom directed in this urgent necessity, to suppress, in e* very part, these rebellious insurrections, and to provide for the general safety, by the restoration of public peace.” — and the Commons say, “We think it our indispensable duty, to acknowledge, with the warmest emotions of gratitude, duty, and affection, your Majesty’s paternal care, and concern for the protection of your subjects, in the measures which your Majesty, as the father of your people, and the guardian of public safety, took in the hour of extreme and imminent necessity, for the immediate and effectual suppression of those rebellious insurrections.”

The King, in his answer to the Lords address, fays, “ Your abhorrence of the rebellious insurrections, and your unanimous approbation of the measures taken to suppress them, must have the raoft fa-lutary effects — and in his answer to the Commons address, after returning them his cordial and particular thanks,

“ for this loyal, affectionate, and unanimous address,” he adds, “ Union, at this time, must have the best and most important consequences: nothing can more powerfully assist me in preferring the public safety, and securing reverence for the laws, and obedience to legal government. Be allured that it is my constant and ardent desire to promote the happiness of all ray subjects, and to deserve the confidence and support of a free people”

Executions of the Rioters.

Executions tf the Rioters,

*T*Wenty-six of the rioters on whom fcn fence of death was pronounced, Slave been executed at or near the places where they had been respectively concerned, In demolifliing houses, or 5n extorting money [362.]. — The executions began on Tuesday, July n. on which day five were executed. — A little before seven o’clock about 200 of the London Association, and about 50 of the White regiment of city-militia, with upwards of 300 constables, attended at Newgate, (which was refitted for the reception of prisoners), to guard the malefactors to the place of execution. —The first was James Pateman, who a-feended the cart about ten minutes pad seven, and was executed in Coleman street. The procefiion was as follows. First, a dozen of the sheriffs men on horseback, with javelins; then thirty or forty constables, with staves; Mr Gates, the city-marihal; a company of the White regiment of militia; two companies of the City-Association; the Sheriffs in their chariots; Mr Vilette, Ordinary of Newgate, in a carriage; the cart with the criminal and executioner; the under-sheriff in a carriage; constables; and the procefiion dosed with two parties of the City* Association, the latter of whom was habited as light infantry. It was with difficulty Mr Vilette could prevail on the criminal to strip his hat of a blue cockade, which he Insisted to wear, declaring, that he died a martyr for the Pro* testant cause, and should leave the world chearfully. Mr Vilette remonstrated with him on his mistaken notion of religion, and advised him not to palliate his guilt, but to prepare for his awful fate, a necessary part or which was, to abandon that spirit of riot which had brought him to an ignominious death. The prisoner then differed the cockade to be taken away. At the place of execution he looked several times at the ruins of the house he bad assisted to demolish, and ihook his head. He appeared penitent, and when the cart drew away, cried a-loud, Lord Jesus, receive roy foulI” From Coleman street the Sheriffs and all their attendants returned to Newgate, and carried William Browne to execution in Bishopsgate street. His father, a gray-headed man, accompanied him, and kissed him before the cart dr$w away. Mr Vilette having alkcd the pri-Vql.XLU.

foner, if he was not sensible of his guilt? he answered, that he really believed he was; and said he was in liquor when he committed the crime.

William Macdonald, Mary Roberts, and Charlotte Gardner a negro, were executed on Tower-hill. The man was hanged between the two women: they embraced each other, and he (hook hands with them. They were all penitent.

Three were executed on Wednesday, July 12. viz. Richard Roberts, a lad of seventeen years of age, for demolifliing Sir John Fielding’s house, and Benjamin Taplin, for extorting money from an apothecary in Russel street; these were executed at the end of Bow street, Covent-garden; and James Henry, convicted of being a ringleader in demolifliing Mr Langdale’s house, was execute^ at the bottom of Hatton-garden, Hol-burn. They were all penitent, and acknowledged their guilt.

Enoch Fleming was executed at the end of Woodstock street, on Thursday, Inly 13. He was penitent, and acknowledged his guilt. He was an industrious roan, maintaining a wife and four children by his business, that of basket-ma-king, at which he worked fifteen hours a-day.

Three were executed on Thursday, July 20. viz. William Gamble, at Bethnal green; Samuel Solomons, a Jew, at the end of Black-Lion yard, Whitechapel; and James Jackson, opposite Newgate. — Gamble prayed with great zeat and devotion, was by trade a cabinetmaker, and had a wife and four or five children. Solomons, on coming out of Newgate, said, “ Though I am a Jew, pray for me, dear brother*prisoners and spedfators, as 1 do for you all: I hope to fee you all in heaven.” He prayed^ fervently, and frequently said, u God forgive my enemies.” He was some time Once a merchant at Smyrna, and carried on a great traffic thence to Holland, and other parts of Europe. Jackson's behaviour was devout and manly: he was by trade a watchmaker, had a. ^ood character, and left a wife, so delirious on account of his fate as to be con-, fined, and three young children.

Five were executed on Friday, July at. viz. Thomas Price, James burn, and Benjamin Waters, in Old* street road, opposite the end of Golden Lane* and Jonathan Stacey and George Staples {n Moorfields.—Price acknowledged^ $ G. he

Two tyere executed on Saturday, July aa. viz. Charles Kent and John Gray, in Bloomsbury square. They were both cripples, and young, and behaved penitently.

Six were executed on Wednesday Aug.

9. near the Kings-bench, in St George^s fields, viz. Robert Lovell, Edward Dorman and Mary Cook, Oliver Johnson and Elisabeth Collins, and John Bridport; and all behaved decently.

One was executed on Tuesday, Aug. jt*. in St George’s fields, viz. Henry Penny. He was a captain among the rioters. He had been respited a fortnight on a plea of insanity; which not appearing to be founded, orders were sent Aug.

29. for his execution.

Of the rioters there were tried, at the Old Bailey and St Margaret's-hill, in all, 135; of these, 76 were acquited, and 59 were capitally convicted; and of the convicts, 33 were respited, and 36 executed.

Eight of the Bath rioters [406.] were tried at Wells, on Friday, Aug. by the Judges Nares and Heath; of whom seven were acquitted; and one, John Bu tier, late servant to Mr Baldwin of Bath, was found guilty, and received sentence of death. His indictment bore, that he, and twenty others, on the 9th of June, began to demolish the house of John Brewer, (the Popish priest, whose house bad a communication with the chapel at Bath), and an outhouse belonging to him. — This condemned criminal was carried privately in a post-chaise from Wells to Bath on Sunday, and was executed next day, Monday, Aug. 28. at the end of Pear-tree lane, near the Ro-mifh chape).

[We hope our having given a full account of these riots, fwhich Judge Nares, in his charge to the jury at Wells, pronounced to be unparallelted in the annals of this or any other country), will be

justly observed, that the proper measures

of prevention were neglected to be taken. Our readers will remember, that, in 1771, upon ao apprehension only, that the livery of London meant to attend the Lord Mayor, at delivering a petition to the King, notice was sent to his Lordfiiip, that no person6 beyond the number allowed by law £33. 705.] would be admitted; and accordingly only that number, ten, attended his Lordship, dec. [33. 388.].—What mifebief (fays a correspondent) might have been prevented, by giving such notice to Ld George Gordon on this occasion, and by authoritatively informing the people assembled in St George’s Fields of the illegality of their proceedings and intentions ! If all our senators had forgot, as probably Lord George did forget, the ait 1661; yet surely such members as were of the common council of London in 1771 cannot be supposed to have forgot the requisition then made to comply with that law, (as just now mentioned), and the obedience paid to that re? quisition, by persons who were not in a humour of submilting to the least encroachment on their rights and privileges. Our correspondent adds, that it may with ju-dice be pleaded for Lord George, as an alleviation at least, that though he gave the most public notice of his intentions, both in the public papers, and in parliament, yet not the least intimation was made to him that they were illegal. — Lord Loughborough, in his charge to the jury [338.], reminded them of tbs ad 1661, above quoted, when the pu-nifliment of delinquents was the object in view: how happy might it probably have been, had that law been humanely intimated to Lord George Gordon, and the multitudes he had convened, by way of prevention, before any mischief wai committed !]

Avg.17So. Parliament: Lords on Lord Amherst's letters. 4

PARLIAMENT. [374.]

Thx Duke of Richmond, in a short speech, on Wednesday, June ax. in the House of Lords, said, that some expref-fions in Lord Amherst’s letters to Co). Twifleton had given a general alarm, and spread doubts amongst the people, whether we are not at present under military government. then had the

three letters from Lord Amherst to Col. Twifleton, dated June 1%. ij. and 14. [4o8,4ix.]read by the clerk, and afterwards the act i° Will, and Mary, for settling the succession to the crown; in which the rights of the subjects are declared, jfnd, amongst others, the right of all Protestant subjects to keep and bear arms for their own defence. His Grace then founded a motion thereon to the follow-purport: * That it is the opinion of this House, that the letter from Lord Am* herft of the 13th of June, to Col. Twifleton, then commanding an armed force in the city, in which he orders him to disarm the citizens, who bad armed themselves for the defence of their lives and properties, and to detain their arms, contains a command or order which invades the property of the said citizens, and violates one of their most sacred rights, that of bearing arms for their defence, declared to be so by the act of the first of William and Mary.’* — The Duke contended that this was a very unwarrantable step of the commander in chief, and highly censurable. He did not consider it as an error in the hurry of business at such a crisis, but as a direct attack upon the liberties of the people, and a step towards introducing the military power to act upon all occasions of disturbances without the civil power, and to discountenance all plans of the people to aflbeiate and arm themselves for the preservation of the peace. — In fine, he called upon the law-lords, and laid there were enough of them in the House, to give their opinion, whether the military acting under an order of the privy council without the magistrate, are acting according to the law of the land, or if we are to consider ourselves as under a military government.

A long and interesting debate took place, in which little was advanced on either fide, but what has been said, upon moving for copies of Lord Amherst’s letters on Monday. [413.]

The only speakers lor the motion, be-

fldes the Noble mover, were the Duke of Grafton and Lord Ravensworth.

The opposers of the motion were the Earl of Carlisle, Lord Townshend, Lord Stormont, the Lord Chancellor, and Lord Mansfield.

The main ground of their argument4 was, That the necelfity of the case jut ftified the conduct of the commander iq chief; that his orders had clearly been under ft ood by Col. Twifleton, not to mean disarming reputable citizens, who badaftbeiated and armed themselves, and were thanked for so doing; but upoii the representation of the Lord Mayor, that persons of no character were going about the streets with weapons, and carrying rusty firelocks to be cleaned and repaired; he had applied to Lord Am* herft to know how to act, and had re* ceived orders to seize the arms of all fii* fpicious persons, and to keep them safe till further orders.

The Lord Chancellor maintained, that the fast stated in the motion was not true$ and even if it were, he said he would vote against the motion at this crisis; a a he did not know that the mischief was yet at an end, and it would be highly improper to paf$ a censure on the cou-dust of Lord Amherst, and the military under him, who had deferred the highelt commendations for their conduct. Hit Lordship defined what a military govern* ment was, and plainly demonstrated that no such thing exists in this country. A government cannot be military while the soldiers and their officers are amenable to the common law of the land.

Lord Mansfield, in confirmation of the doctrine he had laid down on Mon? day, that the military on the late occasion acted under the common law, as all other subjects are requested to do in cases of rebellion and felony, mentioned a new circumftauce concerning the late diabolical premeditated plan for the destruction of London: A party of the mob attempted to cut off the water at the New-River head, Qn the Wednesday evening, when there were so many fires, but were providentially prevented by some sailors. They had also designed to destroy the offices of records. In ftiort, said his Lordship, whether the plan was from a foreign or a domestic enemy, it was to compass no less than the reducing all London to ashes, and destroying public credit. — His Lordship seemed to think the danger not yet over 3 and till it

420 Parliament: Commons on the military acting. Vol- 4

is, that the affidance of the military is necessary, and according to law, being justified by the occasion.

The motion was rejected without a di-▼ifion.

After the concltifion of the debate, Lord Amherst rose, and declared, that though he would not venture to trouble their Lordships with any thing respecting himself, he should think he acted very Improperly, if he neglected to express the very great satisfaction which all the principal officers, whose conduct he bad articular opportunity of observing, had iven him, by the spirit, regularity, and Attention, which they manifeded during the late alarming insurrections.

' On Monday, July 3. Mr David Hartley rose in the House of Commons, and said, he intended to make a motion re spefting the military government which had been introduced into the capital in consequence of the late riots. He was pot then prepared to say, whether the necessity for calling in the military was Dot in the fird indance ptirpofely created; but this he was free to declare, And ready to maintain, that, parliament fitting, and in the full exercise of the ifovercign power, that it amounted almost to an abdication of that power, hot to refumc their conditutional functions, and sanctify, by law, what in itself, from the spur of the occasion, had become perfectly judifiable, the preservation of the date superseding every form, and of course every inferior confederation.— The absence, however, of the Noble Lord in the blue ribbon, the o-ftenfible minider, who was absent, as he underdood, from indifpofuion, prevented him, in point of candour, from bringing on the quedion; he would therefore podpone his intended motion for the present; reserving to himself, at the same time, his fird intention, that of bringing it forward some day before the prorogation, in order that it might iindergo a full and candid difeuffion.

Accordingly, on Wednesday, July 5. the fame gentleman made the following motion: “ That an humble address be presented to his Majesty, dating, that although his faithful Commons have thought it their indispensable duty, to express in the drongeft terms their abhorrence of the proceedings of the late tumultuous assemblies, and of the criminal alls of outrage and violence com-

” by which act such magistrates as are iuveded with the dvQ power, after proclamation made, (or prevented by violence from being made), are, according to their judgement and discretion, authorised and impowered to command all his Majesty’s subjects of age and ability to be affiding to them: To represent to his Majesty, that it is the most alarming of all possible considerations to his faithful Commons, to lee the civil power superseded by thp military by a general order throughout the kingdom, in consequence of which, the military now remains under orders to act without waiting for directions from the civil magistrates: That his faithful Commons, in these circumstances, think it their indispensable duty to their countiy, which they represent, most eaineltly to requed of his Majesty, that he will be graciously pleased to take these things into his consideration, and to give a suit* able relief to his people, by replacing the military force as usual under the direction of the civil power, for the pur* pose of suppressing tumults and riotous assemblies according to the laws and constitution of these kingdoms.”

Gen. Conway read many extralb from ^dilts of the present King of France, which breathed the purest language of patriotism, and paternal afftdlion for his people. Louis XVI. had fticceeded a grandfather whose reign had been a fe-ties of dissipation, which the grandson ieemed determined to avoid. The war was carried on, the public expences pro tided for, and yet no additional tax had been imposed. While we had been obliged to raise by new taxes the interest of k>an6 to the amount of forty millions, the French King had been enabled by {economical retrenchments in his honfe-bold, to with-hold new burdens from the should era of his people: he gave up much of the pomp of his court, and by real alts of paternal affeltion had well deserved the name of a patriot king. The French ministers who gave such good advice to their sovereign, deserved every encomium; whilst our governors, by not having advised their prince to any alt of feconomy, fell far behind.

Lord North made the following observation with regard to the finances of France and England. The former were indeed under the management of a very able, and a very honest financier. But the conltitution of both countries was no less different in point of finance, than in that of legislation. In England a mi* nifter was obliged to bring a lptcifie tax for every public loan. In France it was otherwise: the power of the crown, which in popular governments is divided between a number of people, procures expedients in an absolute monarchy unknown to a limited one; and perhaps, of the few things that were good in absolute governments, the prerogative of the crown in that particular was the best. But when so much ttrefs was laid upon the reform that took place in the household of the French King, it should be remembered, that the French civil list had three times the income of that of England. And in that income were not included the salaries for the support of ambafiadors at foreign courts. The secret-service money was supplied from a distinlt fund; as was all'o the whole establishment of the courts of justice; so that the King of France might

well suppress a great many unnecessary places, and leave himself, for his house-hold only, exefufive of ambafHidors, judges, &c. double the sum which the King of England expends for the support of his court, his emballies, and his judges. If the British Sovereign enjoyed such an income as the King of France, and did not save something from it, the charge against his ministers for not giving him better advice, would come with a good grace. But he would not admit that as great a reform had taken place in France as was supposed, at least if there was not better authority for the opinion than the mere wording of the royal edilts: for during the last reign in France, which had avowedly been a reign of dissipation and criminal munificence, the royal e-dilts spoke as patriotic and as (Economical a language, as that which is expressed in the edilts of the present reign.

But gentlemen were not to suppose that his Majesty was not at great expence during the war, in his private capacity as well as in his public. — The films paid inconsequenceofaddrefiesfrom that House, were advanced out of the cb vil lift, and not repaid for a considerable time. The sums advanced for the support of the American refugees, to the amount of 67,000! together with 40,0001. for secret services, were all advanced from the civil list. A petition had also been presented to his Majesty from the island of Barbadoes, complaining of great want of several necessaries in consequence of a bad crop, and praying relief: the King caused 30,0001. Worth to be sent to the island, and ordered that they should be sold at prime ccft; part of the money had since been repaid; but part remained (till, and ever would remain unpaid. Antigua had afterwards petitioned for limilar relief; and i6,ooool. worth of goods had been sent to the inhabitants on the fame terms; they afterwards drew on the treasury for 20,0001. and as they were in distress, it was thought proper not to distionour the bills. The’' crown advanced all this money; and not only was unreimbursed for a long time, but never would receive a shilling of a great part of the sum. Hence a very considerable loss to the crown, and hence likewise the cause of its being often in arrear.

He had been alked, when he applied

for an additional 100,0001, a-year to the civil lilt, if he would come any more / to

to parliament for the purposes of the civil lkt 00 any account whatever, even when the household of the Prince of Wales should be formed ([19. 297.]. At that time he had not thought it pro* per to make a reply, because he was delicate how he made a promise, unless* he were satisfied he should be able to perform it. Now he could speak, he believed, with confidence on the fubjcA; and he was happy to inform the House, that he hoped an establishment for the Prince of Wales could be defrayed from the civil list, without any more assistance from parliament.

If ministers had not stood forth and offered to the public a part of the civil list towards the defraying of the expences of the war, after the example of Anne, it was because the civil list would not bear it. That Queen gave up 100,0001. to the peopje for the purposes of the war, and gained great popularity for the €t: but after the royal grant had been given away for five years, the then mi-niftry were obliged to come to parliament for the payment of a debt of

6oo,ooool. contracted by the civil list ! If he had refrained to advise his Majesty to follow the example of Q^Anne, it was because be knew the civil list would thereby fall into arrear, and the royal ge-ncrofity would be turned into a mockery of the people.

From ail these observations he trusted they would acquit him of the charge to be deduced from the companion made between the British and French ministry by the Hon. Gentleman.

Rules for curing the Gout radically.

pvR Grant, in his Observations on the origin, progress, and method of treating the Atrabilious Temperament and Gout, lays down the following rules to those who mean to be cured of tbq Gout radically.

1. He must quit the flat fertile cultiva? ted plains during the summer-season ever ry year, and remove to the highest parti of the country, where the air is thin, pure, and piercing.

- 2. Learn to amuse himself with counr try-diversions so as to fatigue himself every day. Of these, fi firing, and wa-r ding in the clear stream, is the belt 3 walking is next; riding is only a fucce-daneum; and a carriage is not equal tq the intention.

3. Avoid populous town9 and great cities; ihun all occasions of anxiety, de* bilitating passions, noxious dissipations, exhaufling pleasures, and heavy mental concerns, or intense thinking, even on agreeable subjects.

4. Keep to regular hours of exercise, eating and sleeping; short sleep is best, on hard beds, in the early part of the night.

5. Let his apartments be large, lofty, ill finished, and well warmed by strong fires.

6. He will soon feel the advantages of frequent bathing, much friction, and warm cloathing.

7. His diet mqft be in proportion to

his

4I4 Grant on the Gout. America: Col. Campbell’s letter. V0I.42.

ills etercife, both in quality and quantity; but he' multi never quite satisfy his hunger at any time.' In general, it is proper to abstain from butter, fat, high-leafoned, salted, and smoked meats. Some forbid black flesh and pork, but I do not find much difference, so that the fat and skin are taken away. Wildfowl and game of all kinds are proper, and so is fish without butter. Animal food mu ft not be used above once a-day: four days in the week he may eat meat; but never mix fish and ftefh Jo the fame day, far less in the fame meal. Butter-milk, whey, fruit, greens, roots, feeds, bread, and dishes prepared from them,* ought to constitute the greater part of his nourishment, especially during the summer and harvest. Tea and coffee, thin chocolate, and cocoa, agTee with most people, mixed with milk, provided they eat no butter along with them.

8. The best common drink is cold water; but when he takes animal food he may drink small-beer, cyder, wine, or spirits very much diluted.

9. When he eats heartily at dinner, he ought to eat no supper, but suffer the stomach to be quite empty once in twenty-fouj* hours.

10. He mud be well rubbed all over every night and morning; and although I wish him to be warmly cloathed, yet I think he ought to walk out in the air as much as may be.

11. When he is quite free from all complaint, sea-bathing, or even cold bathing, will agree with most people, to restore the strength.

la. But in all situations he mud take care to ke$p his body regular, by taking sulphur at night when he iscoftive; and if he should be tormented with wandering pains, the aron-root and feurvygrafs Will be of service to him.

By such means as these, properly conduced, and steadily prosecuted for a length of time, a man of observation, by only attending to th t and latanda in his own constitution, will in general radically cure any recent gout, always moderate it, never injure his natural ftatc of health, or shorten his life.

1 do not expect that It can be in the power of every one of my friends to observe all my directions literally; but it is my province to draw the line, and them to follow it as near as circumstances may permit, carefully observing what do$a and what does not agree,

In the Connecticut Journal of June 8. there is an article, dated Boston, May 15. saidtobcan “ extract of a letter from Col. Campbell, who was some time past a prisoner [39. 249,50.] in this state, and who afterwards commanded the British troops that first took post in Georgia [41- 79.1 This letter was found iu a prize lately brought in here,” viz.

“ My dear Hugh, London, Jan* 15.

In my last I informed you, that the defence of Savannah gave great Satisfaction here; and it has had a very grand effect upon the minds of the people, whose spirits were down, on account of the length of time which elapsed without any effort even to do good: and though this was in some measure no more than a gallant defence, yet it made.an impression almost equal to what a victory would have done. The plan which is now understood to be determined upon here for carrying on the war, is, to take a certain number of posts in America, in such a way as to command the trade of the country; and to bave no other object in America than the maintaining of these posts, and the ruling of the trade by our fleets. The posts said to be fixed upon are Halifax, Penobfcot, New York, Port (mouth in Virginia, Charlestown, Savannah, and St Augustine; to acoom-pliffi which we imagine that you arc now employed in taking Charlestown, and e-stablishingapoftat Portsmouth. Thefeob-jects being accomplished, we understand

12,000 men are to be detached to the West Indies, which is to be the active feat of the war against the French and Spaniards. It is evident, that unless we can carry on an active and defensive war against them abroad, we can never succeed or do well. Last campaign we were all defensive, and every thing went badly with us. I never wish to fee such another campaign. We will be anxious, however, 'lo know what our wife heads in America will think of this plan for the American war. To be sure, the more troops you can spare from thence, to drub the common enemies, the better; yet I fear the number mentioned is more than you can give, after putting the posts mentioned >0 a proper condition. A CAMPBftL.

Lieut, and Adj. Hugh Campbell,

ad bait, 71st reg. Georgia.”

Previous to the departure of Gen. Clinton from Charlestown [301.3, the following arrangement took place till civil government be restored. The troops were left under Lt-Gen. Earl Cornwallis, as commander in chief; Brig.Gen. Paterson was appointed Governor of the province of South Carolina; Lt Col. Darke was named to succeed Gen. Pre-voft in the command at Savannah; and the Hon. George Hanger of the 71st was appointed Major to the Continental militia taken in Charlestown, who are embodied; and of which corps. Major Patrick Fergulbn, of the fame regiment, is Lieutenant-Colonel Commandant. — According to another account, the corps set under the command of Col. Fergu-Fon was a regiment of militia (secretly well-affected to government) inhabiting the back parts of South Carolina, who, on bearing of the taking of Charlestown, seized their colonel, Thompson, their lieutenant-colonel, major, and other officers devoted to the Congress, and delivered them to the commander in chief, desiring themselves might be embodied under Col. Fergufon.

Before Gen. Clinton’s return to New York [378.3, Gen. Knyphaufen made an incursion into the Jerseys, with about 5 or 6000 men, (leaving the militia and the independent companies to guard New York), and landed at Elisabeth-town point on the 5th of June. They advanced six or seven miles into the country, towards Morris-town, where Washington lay with his army, said to consist of between 5 and 6000 men. Gen. Clinton, on his return from Charlestown, went and joined Knyphaufen. The camps of the two contending armies were not above eight miles distant. According to accounts received towards the end of August, Clinton had several times reconnoitred Washington’s camp, and found it so strong, and judiciously formed, that unless he altered his pofi-tion, it would be hazardous to attack him. Several manoeuvres had been trial to draw Washington out, but without effect. Advanced and foraging parties had frequently engaged in trifling Ikir-mifhes; but Washington’s army never made an attempt to alt in any other manner than on the defendve.

Martial law, See* 42$

- A proclamation was llTued, in the name of Joseph Recd/Esq; President, ind the Supreme Executive Council of Pennsylvania, dated at Philadelphia, June 9.; which, after reciting the refo-hition of afiembly, impowering the Council to declare martial law [379.J, proceeds thns: “ And whereas, since the passing the said refoiution, the Hon*. Committee of Congress now with the army, and his Excellency the Commander in Chief, have, in the most pressing and cogent terms, represented theabfolute nc-cellity of procuring certain enumerated suppliesforthe army, in lb short a space of time that the usual and ordinary forms must be dispensed with, or the requifi-tion remain unexecuted; and the late offensive movement of the enemy, of which certain intelligence has been just received, admits of no delay in procuring a number of horses and waggons to be forwarded with all expedition to’ camp: And whereas the iodiferiminate admission of all strangers into this city, without examination or enquiry, enables our artful and inveterate enemy to introduce their spies and emifTaries, distribute their counterfeit money, and by such like practices defeat the belt designs for thg public welfare, and can no longer, with a due regard to our own safety, be permitted or connived at; for the preventing whereof, we have thought proper todirelt an office of enquiry to be opened, and commissioners to be appointed, with power and authority to apprehend all lufpicious persons, and take such other measures as the public safety may require: Wherefore, for the purposes aforesaid, and no farther at present, we have thought proper, and do hereby declare martial law; authorising and requiring the persons by us to be appointed, to proceed as they may be specially directed from time to time, to execute the purposes aforesaid. For all which this our proclamation shall be their sufficient warrant and authority.*’

An address from the council and general assembly of the Bahama islands, dated, New Providence, April *7.1780, to the King, runs thus. — “ We, your Majesty’s dutiful and loyal fnbjells, the Council and Assembly of your Bahama islands, in General Assembly convened* having lately experienced a. very distin-

guishing proof of your paternal goodness, in so graciously hearing and fit speedily redressing our grievances, cannot omit the earliest opportunity of expressing onr just and great sense thereof, and at the same time transtnitting to your Majesty our most humble and grateful acknowledgements, for removing Montford Browne, Esq; from the government of these islands, and appointing Col. Maxwell to preside over us; whose conduit, since his arrival here, gives us the most pleasing cxpeltations of seeing your Majesty’s service advanced, and hw, fatety, and good order restored to this community.”

“ Kingjhn, June to A cenotaph of

marble, "highly executed by the celebrated artilt Mr Wilton, of the Royal Academy of London, w*as last week erelted on the north fide of the communion-table, in the church of St Jago de la Vega, to the memory of our late worthy and much respclted Governor, Sir Bazil Keith, Kt.

It consists of a rich ornamental archi-teltoral niche, between two fluted statuary pilasters, with enriched frize pediment and cornice. Within the niche, which is of the finest dove-coloured mar« ble, is an emblematical figure of Immortality, founding a trumpet, and holding a wreath over an urn of white marble, and upon which the family-arms are em-bofled. Beneath the urn, are trophies signifying Justice, Policy of Commerce and Government. The height of the pediment is nearly fifteen feet, and the ornamental parts being mostly executed in alto relievo, produce a fine effelt. Un-

der the trophies is fixed a plate of black marble, upon svhich is the following inscription in letters of gold.

Sacred to the Memory of Sir Basil Keith, Knight, Governor of Jamaica,

Who departed this life on the 15th day of June r777.

In the duties of his office he was affiduoas, Wife and impartial in the administration of justice ;

A friend to mankind, and a father to the people over whom be presided.

This monument was erelted by the Assembly, to transmit to posterity the gratitude of the people of this island, for the hap-, pine ft they enjoyed under hit mild and upright government.”

By Mr Richardson, fentor.

THE bow and arrows, and a dove,

Weie once enough for Cupid thought, Till Mercury was sent by Jove,

For Venus pray'd he might be taught.

Taught! what ? To read. But why to read

Love knows the language of the eye ;

No other learning does he oeed,

Nor can be cheated by a lie.

Bis (kilful mother better knew;

She saw what times were coming on;

Bis darts mud now be tip’d anew ;

For love is mercenary grown.

Fancy not then that Mercury

Did from Olympus’ top dismount.

To teach the god his A, B, C.

No* no! it was to cad account.

But have a care, thou filly boy

For *tis a most pernicious art;

If learnt, it will thy power destroy ;

The Tongue may Jove, but not the Heart.

Anew HUNTING-SONG.

SEE Phoebus begins to enliven the East,

And fee the gay dawn wears away;

Come, route, fellow huntsman, relinquish dull rest.

And join in the sports of the day.

No longer in sloth let your tenses remain, Untainted the sweets of the morn ;

Drive (lumber away, and make one in our train.

To follow the found of the horn.

What rauGc to ours can for sweetness compare,

What sports such a pleasure can yield

What (cent so resin'd as the new morning air

What profpc& so bright as the field

Let misers for riches each transport forego, 'Midst their treafuies dirtrefs’d and lot lorn, We taste cv’ry joy, and forget ev'ry woe,

So charming the found of the horn.

Such pleafores we feel while from vanity free, Our hours pass contented along ;

In innocent pastime, in mirth, and in glee, With a hearty repast and a song.

Ye mortals unbiafs'd by honours and wealth, These titles that fonow adorn.

Would you taste the calm joys of contentment and health.

Then follow the found of the horn.

STANZAS to NIGHT.

HAIL, lucid Night! thy grisly black Alfifts each thief to fill his (ack;

While Motphcus, lounging, drowsy god.

Has tent all honed men to nod.

Hail, star-deck’d Night! thy lucid beam*

Silver o’er the bubbling dreams:

The youths now softly fttike the lyre;

T he god of Love their songs inspire.

Hail, frigid Night! thy chilling breezes On the vital cut rent seizes.

Until of wine a flowing bowl Expands to mirth the frozen foul.

Hail, sultry Night ! thou friend to Love,

Thy potent heat the pultes move;

Thy warmth the poet’s head doth fill,

And vibrates quick the goose’s quill.

Hail, rtormy Night! thy toaringthunder Makes us mortals dare and wonder;

With vivid lightning flames the Iky,

The pelting hail-stones round us fly.

Then hot or cold, or black ot bright.

Or fair or foul, hail, goddess Night;

With this falutc 1*11 dole my lay.

For yonder dawns the blush of Day.

Turky.

<c Leghorn, May 30. According to the last letters from Smyrna, the plague, which broke out lately in the quarter of the Greeks in that city, continues daily to carry off a number of people; and the lands in the environs are not yet freed of the locusts,* which increase, and destroy all the fruits.’’

“ Vienna, July6. We have accounts

from Constantinople, that that city is thrown into the greatest consternation, by the plague breaking out again, info-much that ao,oooo of the inhabitants have left that capital.”

“ Cotijlaniimple, M4. The head of

a person hath lately been struck. off, of whose country and religion we were totally ignorant, but in other respects he was perfectly known for employing himself principally in the relief and care of the poor, especially of prisoners and Haves. This man spoke the Turkish and Greek languages fluently, and was believed to be a Mahometan. He had had no other occupation for a long time than that of begging alms of persons of every nation, and distributing them indiscriminately among Turks, Creeks, Armenians, Jews, &c. The houses of the greatest part of the grandees of this empire were open to him; and the chiefs of the law took a pleasure in the conversation of a man, whose talents were excellent, and his knowledge in controversy profound, especially in the fundamentals of the Mahometan religion. The Grand Vifir having heard frequent mention made of this person, and curious to know whether he was really a Turk or not, fentforhim, and alked him, if he was a Turk and a Mahometan i to which he replied, he was a Turk, and retired. But, five days afterwards he came again voluntarily into the presence of the Grand Vifir, and boldly avowed, i* That he bad falsely declared himfdf

to be a Turk, as he was really a Chri- ftian, and a disciple of the Lord Jesus Christ.” In consequence of which declaration the Grand Vifir ordered hit head to be publicly struck off immCf diatcly.”

Russia. [271.]

The Emperor of Germany has made a tour to Moscow. His Majesty set out from Vienna, on the 26th of April, pursued his journey through Poland, and arrived at Brody on the aift of May, and at Mohilow on the 23d; at which place the Empress of Russia, who was then on a tour through part of her dominions, arrived on the 25th. Count Cobcnzel presented him to the Empreis by the title of Ccunt Falkenflein. After a reftdence of five days at that place, their Imperial Majesties supped the 30th at Schklow, a country-feat of Gen. So-ritz, and then proceeded together to Smolenfko. Thence the Empress con* tinued her route, and the Emperor proceeded to Moscow. His Majesty arrived at Petersburg on the a8th of June, passed the day following with the Empress at Czarlko-Zelo, returned in the evening to his apartments at Count Co-benztl’s, and went next day again to Czarlko Zelo. After having seen every thing worthy his curiosity, in Peterf-burg, and its neighbourhood, his Majesty set out thence on the 20th of July, and arrived at Vienna, in perfect health, on the 23d of August.

The courts of Copenhagen and Stockholm have delivered their declarations to the belligerent powers, setting forth their accession to the maritime affocia-tion propoled by the court of Russia for the protection of the neutral trade. [212,270.]

The declaration of the court of Copenhagen is dated July 8. After a proper introduction, setting forth the necessity there was for coming to the resolution of protecting the trade and navigation of the neutral powers, it goes of in these words.

*« If the rights of neutrality are sacred, and if the laws of nations, founded on impartiality, established by cuff om, and avowed by all countries, are of any weight, no neutral nation should by a war between other powers lofc the rights it possessed before that war. Such nation is authorised to trade to all parts of Europe, as if there was a general

peace*

43*. Af&irs fa Rofta. 'Vo

peace, (contraband traffick excepted). The King desires nothing beyond what neutrality allows; this is his rule, and that of his people; and his Majesty not thanking it right that any belligerent power should interrupt the trade of hit estates, he therefore. lays before them the principles he ever has maintained, mid ever will, in conjunction with the £mprefs of RufTia, whose sentiments are the fame with his, viz; z. That neutral vessels shall fail from port to port, and on the coasts of nations at war. 2. That the effects belonging to neutral powers shall be free in neutral vcfifcls, except contraband goods. 3. That under the denomination of contraband goods is understood only what is expressly mentioned as such in the 3d article of his Majesty’s treaty of commerce with G. Britain in 1670, and in the nfith and 27th of his treaty with France In 1741; and the King will keep to the fame with regard to any powers with whom he is not now in treaty. 4. That by a blocked-up port is understood one Into which no veil'd can enter without evident danger from the ships placed to block it up. 5. That these principles Jhal) serve as rules for trials in courts of admiralty,’’ &c. The declaration then concludes with saying, that it is his Majesty’* determination to protect the honour of his flag, and the freedom of the trade and navigation of his fub-jgfts; and that he has for that purpose fitted out part of his naval force.

- ’The declaration of the court of Sweden is to the following purport.

“ Since the beginning of the present war, his Majesty, the King of Sweden, has laid it down as a law to observe the ftriCteft neutrality, and hits kept up to the fame: notwithstanding which, his trading subjects have been obliged to claim his protection; in conl’equence of which, his Majesty prepared to fit out a certain number of ships of war for the protection of the Swedish trade; of which his Majesty gave notice to the belligerent powers. Whilst this wjs going forward, some other neutral powers, who were of the fame way of thinking, gave notice of the fame to the King, and at the same time the Em-press of Rufiia delivered a declaration to the courts of London, Versailles, and Madrid, making known her determination to protect the trade of her own subjects, and the general rights of neutral powers taiaj. The King found

An explanation hat been demanded by the court of Sweden, relative to this system of neutrality, and an answer hat been given by the court of Russia. — We subjoin the answer to each qoeftion, viz.

Que/Htm v. How and in what manner a reciprocal protection and mutual assist* ance shall be given

Answer. This must be settled by a for# mal convention, to which all the neutral powers will be invited; the principal end of which is, to insure a free navigation to the merchant-ships of all nations. Whenever such vessel shall have proved from its papers that it carries no contra* band goods, the protection of a squadron or vessels of war shall be granted her, under whose care she shall put her* ft If, and which shall prevent her being interrupted.

2. Whether each particular power shall be obliged to prated the ge* neral commerce of the whole, or if ta the mean time it may employ a part of its armament in the protection of it* own particular commerce

Anf. Each power must concur in the general Security of commerce. In tbc meantime, the better to accomptish this object, it will be necessary to settle, by means of a separate article, the place* and distances which may be judged pro* per for the station of each power. From that method will arise this advantage, that all the squadron* of the allies will form a kind of chain, and be able to af-fist each other; the particular arrange* ment to be confined only to the knowledge of the allies, though the convention in all other point* will be communi* cated to the power* at war, accompanied with all the protestatioas of a ftrict neutrality*

affli<sting accounts from Austrian Molda-.vta, that the locusts which appeared in Autumn last in the district of Herza, in that province, having then deposited their eggs, they now appear in a thou,-sand times greater number than last year, and are two inches long. They are divided into three formidable armies: the first extends seven leagues in length, and tune in breadth, from Herza to Potu-'(han; the second extends from Roman 3 i to

‘ “ P<!,rjtt July 1 8. We have received the Following account of the eruption of Jjount, Etna.

/( towards the end of last January, ma-py (hocks of an earthquake were felt in different parts of Siciiy; and from that time the summit of Mount Etna was observed to fend forth a thick smoke, whic^ lifually extended eastward; and soon after a new eminence was discovered, which Was visible at the distance of fifty miles, and which was a manifest sign of a local explosion. On the 28th of March, and 8th of April, the earthquakes were renewed, and with greater violence, in a direction from north to fonth; ^nd the smoke of the volcano increased so much, that on the a8th of April it was seen li-sing from the crater in the form of a strait pioe, the head of which was hid in the clouds, calling final) fragments of pumice stones for twenty miles round; and in this (late it continued till the 17th of May, when the smoke suddenly cea-fed.

The late tumults have produced one good effect, a reconciliation between the King and his brothers.— A card was sent, 'tis said, by the Duke of Gloucester to the King, on Thursday, June 8. to the following purport: That he presented his humble duty to his Majesty, begging to be immediately employed in the defence of his person at so critical a time as the present.” The King instantly wrote an answer full of affection and tenderness, appointing the next evening for a meeting. They accordingly met at the Queen’s palace, and were together above an hour. The consequence was, that next day the King saw the Duke of Cumberland, and a fraternal affecttion took place between the three Royal brothers.

In the London Gazette of July 29. the following article appeared.——

“ Kennet, Mayor.—A common council holden in the chamber of the Guildhall of the city of London, on Monday, the 24th day of July 1780; — Resolved unanimously, That the thanks of this court be given to the officers and privates who have done duty in this city, for their exemplary conduit and strict attention to military discipline, during the late alarming riots.” - The military began to remove out Of the city next day. [412.]
